GLOSSARY
Aman               -     Rice planted before or during the monsoon and
harvested in November or December.
Aus                -     Rice planted during March or April and harvested
during June or July.
B.                 -     When preceding a crop means broadcast. (B. Aman).
Beel               -     Low-lying area subject to flooding by rain or river
water.
Boro               -     Rice planted in January or February and harvested in
May or June. The term is also used locally for
unirrigated rice which is planted in November or
December in the drained out flood plains.
District           -     Also called by its local name Zila, which is an
administrative unit comprising a number of Thanas, in
the charge of a Deputy Commissioner.
Haor               -     Shallow lake or a large low-lying depression in a
flood-plain that may be reduced during the dry season
to a series of beels.
Khal               -     Natural channel.
Khalasi            -     Staff employed by BWDB to inspect embankments or
operate sluices.
Kharif             -     Summer cropping season (May through November).
Monsoon            -     Period of rains starting in June and ending in
October.
Polder             -     An area of low-lying land encircled by embankments for
protection against flooding
Project Proforma  -      An internal Government document required to obtain
authorization for inclusion of a project in the
investment budget.
Rabi               -     Winter cropping season  (November through F
T.                 -     When preceding a crop means transplanted.
Thana              -     Subdistrict, an administrative unit between the Union
and the District.
Union              -     Smallest administrative unit of local government.
Union Parishad    -      Elected council governing the Union.

BANGLADESH
COASTAL EMBANKMENT REHABILITATION PROJECT
I.  PROJECT AND SECTOR BACKGROUND
A. Project Background
1.1       Natural calamities are a recurring phenomenon in Bangladesh.  The
most severe occur in the form of cyclonic storms which affect the coastal
districts. The country has over 700 km of coastline on the mainland and
several offshore islands in the Bay of Bengal. Vulnerable areas include the
important port towns of Chittagong and Khulna, and the heavily populated
islands of Sandwip, Kutubdiya and Hatiya. During the last 125 years, over 42
cyclones hit the coastal belt; 14 occurred during the last 25 years. Cyclones
often cause major destruction of human life, livestock, crops, properties, and
physical infrastructure. Such cyclones also tax the country's institutional
and financial capabilities, since resources have to be diverted to provide
immediate relief to the affected population and to restore damaged or
destroyed physical facilities.
1.2       Recent cyclones causing widespread devastation and loss of life
occurred in November 1970, May 1985, November 1988, and April 1991. After the
cyclone of November 1970, the Government of Bangladesh (GOB), assisted by IDA
under the Coastal Area Rehabilitation Project (Cr. 339-BD) and other donors,
initiated a reconstruction program aimed at (a) rehabilitation of the economy
of the affected areas, and (b) provision of protection from future cyclones.
The benefits of these investments were demonstrated during the cyclone of May
1985. For example, the coastal embankment withstood the tidal surge at the
peak period and although the embankment was overtopped or breached in several
places, the energy of the tidal surge was dissipated and, by and large, houses
remained in site. The radio system also worked satisfactorily and conveyed
warnings of the advent of the cyclone, and the cyclone shelters and other
facilities were used effectively. Due to these improvements, loss of life and
damage to infrastructure were relatively limited. However, destruction was
almost total on the unembanked islands and the newly accreted parts of the
mainland where most people, with their houses and livestock, were swept away
by the tidal wave. By the time of the April 1991 cyclone, the embankments
were in a poor state of maintenance, and were breached in many locations. The
cyclone caused about 140,000 deaths, and severe damage throughout the eastern
coast of the Bay of Bengal. Had the embankments been in better condition, the
damage would have been much less severe.
1.3       In 1986 GOB formulated a National Cyclone Protection Program
including proposed programs for improving the existing coastal embankment;
protecting newly accreted lands; developing forests; improving
telecommunications, roads and coastal transport; and providing improved and
less costly cyclone shelters, to be used also as schools and health
facilities. Improvements to the coastal embankments and protection of newly
accreted land were to be undertaken in two phases. The mid-term program would
strengthen existing coastal embankments, while the long-term program would
include the construction of embankments and associated structures for the



protection of newly accreted lands. After various delays, caused in part by
the 1987 and 1988 floods, a contract was signed with consultants (a joint
venture led by KAMPSAX International) financed by the European Community (EC),
which included provision for preparation of the mid-term program and
developing TOR for a study of the long-term program.
1.4       The consultants' studies were in progress when the April 1991
cyclone struck. At GOB's request, the World Bank agreed to coordinate donor
assistance. The Government also requested the consultants to prepare plans
and designs for a Priority Works Program (PWP) to provide for the most
urgently needed embankment repair and rehabilitation work. Meanwhile, the
World Food Programme (WFP) financed earthworks to provide temporary protection
until the full embankments can be constructed. Funding was arranged from
Saudi Arabia (about US$13.3 million equivalent), a Japanese grant (about
US$23 million equivalent, under IDA administration), EC (about US$4 million),
and US$29 million from amendments to IDA credits 1467, 1784, 1870, and 2048,
for a program including about 135 km of embankment rehabilitation and about 55
km of new retired embankments. Work has been substantially completed except
for one polder where there were objections to the planned alignment, and a
second where additional drainage improvements have been found necessary. A
new alignment has now been agreed and about $3 million equivalent for
completion of the work for the two polders is proposed for financing under
this proposed credit.
1.5       Following the April 1991 cyclone, there was increased recognition
that reliable protection of human lives can be provided at the most economic
cost through a program of construction of additional cyclone shelters combined
with establishment of an appropriate cyclone disaster preparedness system.
Proposals for such a project are being prepared separately for consideration
in FY97. A component to improve road access to facilitate post-cyclone relief
operations, originally proposed as part of this project, is now being financed
by the Asian Development Bank (ADB). The proposed cyclone embankment
rehabilitation works under this project would complement the above efforts by
providing partial protection of crops and infrastructure, as well as
additional protection of human lives even after the construction of an
appropriate number of shelters. Even in areas of adequate shelters, a portion
of the population does not leave their homes (since most cyclones do not turn
out to be severe and leaving home risks theft of possessions). Moreover,
embankments provide inhabitants more time to reach shelters safely.
1.6       While the project was originally planned primarily in terms of
cyclone protection, it has important benefits in increasing agricultural
production, through protecting coastal agricultural areas from saline
inundation during normal annual monsoons and improved cropping patterns due to
reduced cyclone risks, and better drainage. Most of the proposed project
works would be economically viable on the basis of such increases in
agricultural production, even without the benefits of the project in reducing
cyclone damages and saving lives.



B. The Agriculture Sector
The Setting and Recent Trends
1.7       Agriculture, consisting of crops, fisheries, livestock, and
forestry subsectors, is still the predominant sector of Bangladesh's economy.
Its relative share of GDP, even though on a declining trend, remains high.
The share has fallen from about 53% in FY73 to 43% in FY83 and to 36% in FY92
(at constant 1984/85 prices). Processed and unprocessed agricultural
products, though also declining in relative importance, account for about 32%
of the country's export earnings. Agriculture also has significant forward
and backward linkages with other sectors. It supplies raw materials for the
most important single manufacturing subsector, jute, and for smaller
industries like food processing, tobacco manufacturing, leather tanning, and
pulp and paper. It is the market for the growing fertilizer industry. Trade
and transport of agricultural goods and inputs account for a large percentage
of these sectors' contribution to GDP. Agriculture employs over 60* of the
country's civilian population, provides the principal livelihood for most of
the poor.
1.8       The sector is dominated by the crop subsector, especially rice.
Crop production accounts for almost 70% of the value of agricultural output
while fisheries, livestock, and forestry each account for about 10%. The
share of foodgrains in the value of agricultural output is about 50%. Within
the foodgrains, rice accounts for about 90% of the value of output while wheat
accounts for 6%.
1.9       Overall, growth in agricultural production slowed in the 1970s due
to war, floods, drought, and inappropriate policy decisions. Growth increased
moderately in the early 1980s. There was a substantial upsurge in growth in
the late 1980s, based on trends in irrigation and fertilizer usage, which were
not immediately visible in increased crop production due to severe floods in
FY88 and FY89. Based upon these underlying trends, crop production jumped by
9W in FY90. Rice production, which had been growing at about 2% per annum
between FY61 and FY89, increased by almost 15% between FY89 and FY90. Policy
reforms involving privatization of the distribution of fertilizer and minor
irrigation equipment and resulting in rapid expansion of minor irrigation
(permitting multicropping and growing high yielding variety rice in the dry
season) accounted for the jump in rice production. However, this led to
unprecedented declines in rice prices leading to reduced incentives for
further rapid expansion. During the period FY90 through FY93 crop production
grew at only slightly more than 1% per year. The fisheries subsector, in
contrast, has been growing at over 6% per year during the 1990s.
1.10      As a result of the acceleration in rice production, Bangladesh,
which had long been dependent upon food aid to meet minimal foodgrain
requirements, achieved rice self-sufficiency in years of normal weather and
even began to export small quantities of rice. (In years of poor weather such
as FY94/95, shortages of rice still occur.) If Bangladesh is to continue to
expand agricultural production and take advantage of opportunities for
expansion of low-cost irrigation, it must begin to diversify production
(including export quality rice as well as other crops.) This requires the



spread of knowledge of appropriate production methods, through improved
effectiveness of research and extension (both public and private sector).
Nevertheless, some expansion of rice production in line with Bangladesh's
comparative advantage will still be required to meet the needs of the growing
population.
Aaricultural Development Obiectives and Strateav
1.11      In light of the country's heavy dependence on food aid, GOB's main
objective for agriculture since independence has been to achieve food security
through a policy of food self-sufficiency, the latter being interpreted
largely as a drive to achieve self-sufficiency in the production of rice, the
staple foodgrain. GOB has set two major goals for the agriculture sector in
the current Five-Year Plan (FY91-95): (a) attaining foodgrain (rice)
self-sufficiency by the end of the plan period; and (b) increasing the
production of minor but nutritionally important crops to improve the quality
of food consumption. Attainment of these objectives would also reduce
poverty.
1.12      Since independence, the primary emphasis of development efforts in
agriculture has been to replace the traditional cultivation methods,
characterized by low and sharply fluctuating yields, by more modern techniques
leading to higher production and sustained growth. The poor performance of
the sector was identified with the low level of technology characterizing
traditional agriculture and overwhelming dependence on weather. A
breakthrough in agriculture was considered primarily dependent on a strategy
of a rapid expansion of irrigation-fertilizer-seed technology. The current
Five-Year Plan articulates a strategy for achieving more rapid agricultural
growth, emphasizing the need to shift from high risk, monsoon-dependent
production to lower risk, irrigated production. This is to be achieved
primarily through expanding minor irrigation coverage, stimulating crop
diversification, relying more on competitive markets for input supply to
farmers, improving rural transport infrastructure, strengthening agricultural
research and extension systems to develop new technologies and diffuse the
proven technologies to farmers, making adequate credit available to finance
needed investments, and improving the macroeconomic policy environment.
1.13      The strategy for the sector, as spelled out in the current Five-
Year Plan, is basically consistent with the consensus arising from intensive
dialogue within GOB and between GOB and donor agencies in general, and IDA,
the Asian Development Bank, the European Community, the Overseas Development
Administration of the U.K., and the U.S. Agency for International Development
(USAID), in particular. The dialogue has been based on the findings of sector
work that has been carried out by IDA and other donors. It has been
demonstrated that minor irrigation equipment (shallow tubewells, deep
tubewells, and low lift pumps) has to be the method of choice for expanded
irrigation on grounds of cost per irrigated hectare, ease of management,
minimum reliance on public resources, and affordability by farmers. Expansion
of minor irrigation is also the only alternative that would bring quick
increases in production over the short and medium term. Moreover, the
strategy would not be constrained by availability of irrigable land and water
during this decade. GOB, with donor support, is therefore pursuing private



- 5 -
sector-led minor irrigation expansion as the main pillar of its agricultural
development strategy for this decade. As part of this strategy, it aims to
liberalize further the trade and regulatory framework for agricultural inputs
with a view to allowing a major role for market forces and private
initiatives. While a lot has been achieved with respect to agricultural
policy reforms, further action is needed to: (a) remove remaining policy
bottlenecks (e.g., formal legal actions to make recent reforms in seed policy
and deregulation of foodgrain marketing permanent are needed to give the
private sector confidence to invest in these fields); (b) make the
agricultural credit system work; and (c) improve weak project administration
and the poor implementation record.
1.14      The overall strategy has been fairly successful.  However, the
progress made in the sector has been almost totally the result of increased
irrigation. Average yields per hectare per season for irrigated and non-
irrigated production have not increased, indicating the need for increased
attention to improving agricultural extension and research programs. The
increase in rice production means that Bangladesh may soon have a new problem
of dealing with surplus rice in years of good weather. This requires a
substantial change in the thinking of the Government and the donor community,
with increased emphasis either on policies for encouraging crop
diversification or for the possibly more difficult task of improving a portion
of the crop to exportable quality. Nevertheless, given continued rapid
population growth (which has been reduced somewhat to 2.2t per annum) and
severe shortage of cultivable land, Bangladesh must continue substantial
efforts to accelerate agricultural growth to keep ahead of the population
growth. Moreover, with 601 of the labor force still engaged in agriculture,
any strategy for economic growth and poverty alleviation must include
continued increases in the productivity of the agricultural sector.
C. IDA's Involvement in Agricultural DeveloDment
1.15      IDA has been involved in Bangladesh's agricultural sector for many
years. The involvement has been in the form of lending operations, sector
work and special studies, policy dialogue, and coordination of donor
activities in the sector. The lending program for Bangladesh has emphasized
agriculture, especially deve] )ing water resources, strengthening agricultural
support services, and developing complementary infrastructure. Sector work
and special studies have been used by IDA as the basis for the lending program
and policy dialogue with GOB. IDA has also been working closely with the aid
community to encourage consistency of approach and avoid duplication of
effort.
1.16      Consistent with GOB's priorities, increased food production,
especially rice, has been the main target of IDA's lending in agriculture with
emphasis given to minor irrigation development and flood control and drainage.
IDA is also assisting in the exploitation of the yet unexploited potential
that Bangladesh has in the fisheries subsector. Given that fuel shortage and
population pressure are encouraging encroachment on forests, IDA is also
playing an important role in preserving forests through its lending program,
e.g., financing agro-forestry activities. In addition, IDA is financing



- 6 -
support services (e.g., research and extension) and technical assistance
(e.g., in flood control). Ongoing IDA-supported agricultural projects
include: Small Scale Flood Control, Drainage and Irrigation II (Cr. 1870-BD),
BWDB Systems Rehabilitation (Cr. 2099-BD), Third Fisheries (Cr. 2146-BD),
Agricultural Support Services (Cr. 2233-BD), National Minor Irrigation
Development (Cr. 2246-BD), and Forest Resources Management (Cr. 2397-BD). A
major theme in Bangladesh's agenda for encouraging sector development is
expansion of private sector services to enhance efficiency. IDA has used its
lending program to promote policy reforms in this direction. For example, in
close collaboration with the Asian Development Bank, IDA assisted GOB in 1991
to develop a list of investments and input requirements linked to an action
program for policy and institutional reforms. IDA supported this package
through three projects: Shallow Tubewells and Low Lift Pumps (Cr. 2253-BD,
now closed), National Minor Irrigation Development and Agricultural Support
Services. IDA has also been coordinating an international effort aimed at
formulating a plan to improve Bangladesh's ability to deal with flood hazards.
This included coordinating the work of a panel of experts assigned to oversee
the implementation of a series of studies and pilot projects which will
recommend an appropriate Flood Action Plan (FAP). As this work draws to a
close at the end of 1995, IDA is assisting GOB in developing permanent
institutions for improved water sector planning, preserving the institutional
capacity developed under FAP and making multi-disciplinary planning approaches
part of Bangladesh's normal water sector planning procedures.
1.17      The proposed project is part of IDA's efforts to improve
Bangladesh's ability to deal with flood hazards, but reflects the unique
characteristics of the project area. The problems of saline intrusion affect
only a small portion of the country, and the opportunities for similar yield
increases through keeping out saline water are limited. The high rates of
return available for certain project investments also reflect the inherent
advantages of a rehabilitation project, able to take advantage of the sunk
costs of prior investments. However, the opportunities for similar coastal
embankments to protect newly accreted lands are well worth studying as part of
the longer term post-FAP water sector planning activities described above.
While the proposed project as a whole can be justified based upon its
quantifiable agricultural production benefits, there are significant non-
quantifiable benefits in the savings of human lives during future cyclones.
Therefore, this project should not be considered solely in terms of IDA's
agricultural sector strategy.
D. Institutions Involved in the Prolect
1.18      Two institutions, Bangladesh Water Development Board (BWDB) and
Forest Department (FD), are involved in the preparation and implementation of
the project.
Bangladesh Water DeveloDment Board
1.19      BWDB was established in 1972, when the former East Pakistan Water
and Power Development Authority (EPWAPDA) was reorganized into two separate
agencies -- the BWDB and the Power Development Board (PDB). Under the



- 7 -
supervision of the Ministry of Water Resources (MOWR), BWDB's mandate is to
plan, construct, operate and maintain projects for surface water irrigation,
flood control, and drainage, including town protection and river training,
throughout the country. It is also responsible for operating and maintaining
about 400 completed projects ranging in size from small single-structure
schemes to large multipurpose flood control, drainage, and irrigation
projects. Its main sources of funding are: (a) GOB's Development Budget for
the construction of new projects and rehabilitation of a large number of
existing schemes; (b) the Revenue Budget for operation and maintenance (O&M)
of completed projects (including an allocation for debt service, taxes and
arrears); and (c) the Food for Work Programme (FFW) with an allocation of
wheat utilized for the repair and maintenance of existing projects, and for
the construction of new schemes. Since 1972 IDA has supported BWDB's program
with 15 credits, totalling about US$440 million for 11 new projects and four
flood rehabilitation operations.
1.20      BWDB is managed by a six member Board comprising a Chairman, and
five Members for Planning, Implementation, Operation and Maintenance (O&M),
Administration, and Finance, respectively. The headquarters organization is
primarily responsible for administration, finance, planning and design while
construction and O&M are the responsibility of the field organization. BWDB
has about 16,300 staff, of whom about 1,500 are Class I managers, engineers,
and administrators, and about 14,800 are Class II, III and IV middle and lower
level technical and support staff. About one third of BWDB employees are
assigned to O&M. However, these staff have also been responsible for
construction and rehabilitation of small projects.
1.21      Project Performance Audit Reports (PPAR) prepared for five
completed IDA-financed BWDB projectsl indicate that three have been
successful, one project (the Barisal Irrigation Project) has not reached its
full potential, and one (Drainage and Flood Control I) is questionable. For
the Drainage Project, the PPAR indicated that the assumption that incremental
dry season production would not have taken place in the absence of the flood
control embankment "has not yet been substantiated with appropriate impact
studies." However, the PPAR also noted that the largest of the project's
investments included significant non-quantifiable benefits of protecting
population from periodic flooding. (It should be noted that the benefits of
the proposed project, providing protection from saline high tide inundation
and reduction in damage from cyclones, are different from the more typical
inland flood control projects.) All the completed projects experienced
1.    (a) Chandpur Irrigation Project, Cr. 340-BD, PPAR Report No. 3436,
dated April 30, 1981.
(b) Karnafuli Irrigation Project, Cr. 605-BD, and Barisal Irrigation
Project, Cr. 542-BD, PPAR Report No. 6521, dated November 21,
1986.
(c) Muhuri Irrigation Project, Cr. 725-BD, PPAR Report No. 8654, dated
May 16, 1990.
(d) Drainage and Flood Control Project, Cr. 864-BD, PPAR Report No.
8805, dated June 29, 1990.



serious construction delays. On average, it took about eight years to
complete the schemes instead of five years as originally planned. Projects
currently under construction and those more recently completed experienced
similar delays, most of which occur at the pre-construction stage. There have
been significant delays due to time consuming administrative procedures for
appointing consultants to prepare feasibility studies and designs. Procedures
for acquiring land for construction have been long and complicated and actual
acquisition of land often has been delayed because of shortage of local funds.
Delays have occurred during the construction stage due to procurement
problems, insufficient budget allocations, or delays in release of funds.
Poor quality of construction due to inadequate supervision of works by BWDB
staff and use of inexperienced local contractors was a major problem in the
past. In recent years, BWDB has employed local consultants on several
projects to monitor construction works and help maintain quality standards.
Quality of works has improved but there is still room for significant further
improvement. There has also been improvement in the funding allocations for
the IDA-assisted BWDB projects, which have benefitted from dialogue in
connection with the Public Resource Management Adjustment Credit (Cr. 2361-
BD).
1.22      Potential benefits of past investments in BWDB's projects have not
been fully realized due to weaknesses in O&M resulting in deterioration of the
systems. The underlying reasons for these weaknesses include: (a) inadequate
budget allocations; (b) inefficient use of available funds; (c) deficiencies
in BWDB's organizational arrangements, procedures and staff capabilities for
O&M; and (d) insufficient farmer participation. Actions to resolve these
issues have been agreed under the BWDB Systems Rehabilitation Project (SRP)
(Cr. 2099-BD). Of particular importance are agreements for increasing
budgetary allocations for O&M, improvements in BWDB's organization for O&M,
training programs for BWDB staff and beneficiaries, and measures to increase
beneficiary participation in financing irrigation O&M activities. While there
have been some notable improvements (expenditures on maintenance doubled from
FY89 to FY94), progress on institutional issues has been less than expected.
However, recent steps, such as the approval in August 1994 of Guidelines for
Peoples' Participation in Water Development Projects, the assignment of staff
to implement these Guidelines, and the development of a training program for
such staff, appear to indicate renewed commitment to addressing the key issues
in this field.
1.23      Since mid-1994 substantial progress has been made on long-standing
institutional issues affecting BWDB. Permanent members have been appointed to
BWDB's Board (previously staffed largely through "acting" assignments) and the
Board's original mandate and authority as an autonomous body has been
restored. Acting upon this renewed authority, the Board filled all vacant
Chief Engineer positions and delegated increased authority to lower levels of
BWDB staff to permit project managers to expedite project procurement and
expenditure decisions. A new accounting system, designed to make BWDB's
financial transactions more transparent, has now been introduced. (The new
accounting system had been designed in 1990 by consultants financed by the
Canadian International Development Agency (CIDA) as part of co-financing the
Second Small-Scale Flood Control, Drainage and Irrigation Project (Cr. 1870-
BD). However, implementation of the new system had been resisted until mid-



- 9 -
1994). As noted above, Guidelines for Peoples' Participation have been
approved, and are being implemented. Finally, BWDB established five task
forces on institutional reform, whose recommendations provided the basis for
reaching agreement with IDA in September 1994 on a short-term BWDB reform
program. This program concentrated on what could be accomplished over the
following year, without altering the current allocation of responsibilities
between BWDB and other institutions. The reform program included measures
affecting all aspects of BWDB activities, including implementation, O&M,
accounting, planning, and administration. Key measures, beyond those
indicated above, include a phased program to separate staff and
responsibilities for O&M and construction in order to focus a portion of the
staff on O&M, reforms in personnel planning, evaluation, and promotion
policies, and introduction of multi-disciplinary project planning teams.
Progress to-date in implementing the reform program is on schedule.
1.24      The reform program concentrates mainly on BWDB and leaves issues
involving other institutions in the water sector for further analysis.
However, the program includes agreement to undertake studies on such issues,
including appropriate institutional arrangements for sector analysis, project
evaluation and planning, and mechanisms for decentralization of responsibility
and increasing peoples' participation in project formulation and O&M. The FAP
reports' institutional recommendations include a merger of the temporary
organization established for FAP with an existing, but weak, sectoral planning
organization. Under the proposed River Bank Protection Project (RBPP), IDA
expects to finance technical assistance to improve sectoral planning capacity
(see para 1.16) and carry-out the above referred medium-term studies.
1.25      The proposed project design addresses some of the problems
described above, inter alia, through requiring the use of experienced,
internationally recruited consultants for supervision of works, using
prequalified contractors selected through ICB procedures for the major civil
works, using "Class A" contractors (or the equivalent) and agreed minimum
contract sizes for the minor works, and incorporating community participation
and other measures designed to improve maintenance of project works.
Forest Deoartment
1.26      Formerly part of the Ministry of Agriculture, the Forest Department
(FD) is now under Ministry of Environment and Forests (MOEF) which was
established in 1989. MOEF is in the process of being fully established, but
is still weak, especially in the areas of monitoring and evaluating the
operations of its executing agencies, interagency coordination, and resolution
with other ministries of important intersectoral issues, including land
allocation, wood prices, and human resource development.
1.27      FD, headed by a Chief Conservator of Forests (CCF), is the primary
GOB agency responsible for the day-to-day management of forest resources in
the country. At its headquarters, the CCF is assisted by three Deputy Chief
Conservators of Forests (DCCF), responsible for development planning, forest
extension, and forest management planning, respectively. Each DCCF is
supported by an Assistant Chief Conservator of Forests (ACCF). Reporting to
the CCF is also a Conservator of Forests (CF), who with the assistance of two



- 10 -
ACCFs, is responsible for general administration and wildlife. Also, the
Directors of both FD's Forest Development and Training Center and the Thana
Afforestation and Nursery Development Project are directly responsible to the
CCF.
1.28      FD's field operations, consisting of six Circles headed by CFs and
concerned with territorial forestry, also come under the CCF's direction. The
CF of each Circle is in charge of several forest divisions, which are normally
within the boundary of an administrative district, and under the charge of a
Divisional Forest Officer (DFO). There are 37 forest divisions including one
covering the National Botanical Garden. The divisions are divided into
several forest ranges which are controlled by Forest Range Officers, who in
turn are in charge of several beats, each under a Deputy Ranger or Forester.
The field staff is responsible for the establishment and maintenance of
plantations, supervision of state forest lands, provision of industrial wood
supplies and extension and public relations at the local level.
1.29      FD's total staff is close to 10,000 including about 4,850
professional and technical staff by the end of 1991. In addition, FD employs
a labor force of about 500,000 staff-days/year. The FD has long been a
traditional administration-based organization with accountability focussing
mainly on adherence to budgetary allocations.  Because work quality and
effectiveness are not systematically measured, the performance of FD's field
staff varies widely. The organization was not sufficiently developed along
functional lines, with inadequate specialization of staff. Headquarters'
capacity for planning, programming, monitoring and evaluating, and
environmental management has been weak. Promotion within FD is almost purely
based on seniority with very little attention to merit and managerial and
technical capacity. Top managers, especially CCFs, are frequently promoted to
their post shortly before retirement, reducing their interest in policy and
institutional reform. FD has furthermore been slow to introduce people-
oriented forestry development, which, in view of the country's extreme
population pressure, is crucial for the establishment, protection, and
utilization of forests. While a change in attitude towards people's
participation is already evident, clear policies, institutional changes and
suitable programs are yet to be developed. Some of these issues are being
addressed under the Forest Resources Management Project (Cr. 2397-BD, approved
June 1992). In particular, the organization of FD has recently been reshaped
along functional lines, in accordance with a revised organizational structure
agreed under the credit. However, it would take some time for the new
organizational arrangements to affect long-established administrative
practices.
1.30      Despite the weaknesses noted above, the overall experience with the
two prior IDA-assisted forestry projects has been good. The Mangrove
Afforestation Project (Cr. 1042-BD, approved in 1980) achieved an 18% economic
rate of return and established over 30,000 ha of new mangrove plantations.
The Second Forestry Project (Cr. 1634-ED, approved in November 1985) closed in
June 1992, one year later than originally scheduled. Provisions for
establishment of 40,000 ha of new mangrove plantations, establishment of
23,000 ha of industrial plantations and maintenance of mangrove plantations
have shown good progress. Progress was behind expectation, however, on



- 11 -
components for thinning industrial plantations, improving the forestry data
base, and improving in-service training of FD personnel. IDA assistance to
the forestry sector continues under the Forest Resources Management Project,
which became operational in 1992. This project supports a seven-year
comprehensive forestry development program. After a considerable delay in
procuring technical assistance and contracting for new aerial photographs, the
performance of the project at present is generally satisfactory. However,
there are still some policy and institutional reform issues concerning which
discussions with the Government are continuing. In addition to IDA-financed
projects, FD has undertaken a number of ongoing pilot operations in community
participation in forestry, including projects on private and public wastelands
and the slopes of railway and road rights-of-way. These projects have
involved several non-governmental organizations (NGOs) and have been assisted
by such external agencies as ADB and WFP. The proposed project takes
advantage of some of the community participation models developed in the
inland areas, combined with FD experience in saline tolerant mangrove
plantation in the coastal areas.
II. THE PROJECT
A. Proiect Objectives and Concept
Obiectives
2.1       The objectives of the proposed project are:  (a) cyclone
protection, including improving the security of persons living in the
protected areas, reducing damage to houses and other buildings and
infrastructure and minimizing the loss of crops and livestock; (b) improved
agricultural production, through preventing saline inundation during normal
weather and improved cropping patterns due to reduced cyclone risks; and
(c) introduction of improved technology in the design and construction of
protection works, and improved methods of embankment maintenance.
Project Concept
2.2       The proposed project was originally intended to finance the Mid-
Term Program to strengthen existing coastal embankments, while a second-stage
long-term program was being developed for the construction of embankments and
associated structures for the protection of newly accreted lands. In response
to the April 1991 cyclone, a portion of the originally intended project has
been financed separately under the PWP (see para 1.4) to provide for the most
urgently needed embankment repair and rehabilitation work. However, the PWP
works are still conceptually part of the Mid-Term Program for coastal
embankment restoration. The proposed project would finance in addition to the
rehabilitation and improvement of 12 sea-facing embankments, ancillary
investments for improved O&M, afforestation, and minor polder improvements
covering the ten PWP embankments as well as the 12 embankments referred to



- 12 -
above.2 It would also finance small amounts of work required to complete the
PWP embankments in one polder where there had been delays due to disputes
concerning the alignment and in another where improvements in drainage are
required. For reasonable economy in construction, designs for embankments to
protect agricultural land allow for moderate overtopping of embankments during
severe cyclones, but not to a degree which would lead to breaching of the
embankments or to very high flood levels in the polders. Adequate drainage is
also to be provided. Afforestation is provided both to help absorb wave
energy and tidal surges during cyclones and to reduce embankment maintenance
costs. A community participation approach is to be employed for afforestation
and routine embankment maintenance.
B. Rationale for IDA Involvement
2.3       In light of IDA's past efforts in assisting Bangladesh to develop
programs for dealing with the issue of recurrent cyclones, GOB requested IDA
to coordinate donor assistance following the April 1991 cyclone. IDA agreed
and assembled the financing package for the PWP (para 1.4). The proposed
project would extend the PWP program to additional areas, as well as provide
investments to complement those financed under the PWP. The project should
also enable IDA to maintain the ongoing dialogue with GOB on appropriate
disaster control policies and institutional support. Finally, the proposed
project would protect past and ongoing IDA investments in the coastal belt,
specifically the Coastal Area Rehabilitation Project (Cr. 339-BD) completed in
1980, Flood Rehabilitation Project (Cr. 1587-BD) completed in 1986, which
included repair of damages caused by the May 1985 cyclone; Second Flood
Rehabilitation (Cr. 1876-BD) completed in 1989, which included repair of
damage during the 1987 floods; and the ongoing Second Small Scale Flood
Control and Drainage Project (Cr. 1870-BD). A revised Country Assistance
Strategy (CAS) is scheduled to be presented at the same time as this
operation. The CAS emphasizes, inter alia, the importance of agricultural
growth, development of rural infrastructure, and the need to reduce the
negative impact of flooding on people's lives and livelihood.
C. Prolect Components
2.4       The project includes the following components:
(a) rehabilitation and improvements to 12 sea-facing empbankments, plus
small amounts to complete similar works in two polders of the PWP
program;
(b) minor rehabilitation and improvements to the non-coastal sections
of the polders in the Mid-Term Program, including re-excavation of
drainage canals (khals), repair or replacement of
2.    There is a total of 21 polders, since the sea-facing embankments of one
polder are financed partially under PWP and partially under this
project.



- 13 -
regulators/surface sluices or minor embankment repairs where
appropriate to assure agricultural benefits;
(c)  afforestation of embankment slopes and a strip of 50 to 200 meters
of foreshore to reduce cyclone damage and embankment maintenance
costs;
(d) improved O&M of all polders in the Mid-Term Program;
(e) a program for compensation, rehabilitation and resettlement of
people displaced by the project;
(f) environmental monitoring and provision for appropriate mitigating
measures;
(g) studies to investigate the possibility of cost-effective town
protection works for Sandwip;
(h) technical assistance for implementation support for components (a)
through (g) above, and for coordination between the project and
other projects in the area supporting increased agricultural
production, and NGO activities to organize and support community
participation in afforestation and maintenance of embankments;
(i) training of staff of implementing agencies and participating NGOs,
and for community participants; and
(j) vehicles and minor equipment required for GOB supervision of
proiect implementation, and for afforestation.
In addition the EC grant supporting this project includes a Community
Development Fund to enable NGOs to implement such poverty alleviation
activities complementary to the Project as may be agreed between GOB and EC.
The proposed project would be implemented over a seven year period beginning
in late 1995 or early 1996.
2.5       A community participation approach would be used for dealing with
the issues of afforestation, routine embankment maintenance, and resettlement
of families displaced by project works (predominantly squatters on the
sections of the embankment rehabilitated under the project). NGOs would be
employed to assist in mobilizing the community, provide training of
participants and facilitate community implementation of these project
activities. The project design, including embankment parameters, use of
afforestation and community participation in maintenance, reflects the results
of studies to determine a cost-effective design with reduced maintenance
costs.



- 14 -
D. Proiect Area
2.6       The polders (Annex 2) covered in the project are spread over the
coastal belt surrounding the Bay of Bengal, from Teknaf on the southeast tip
of Bangladesh to Sharankhola at the southeast border of Bagerhat District. Of
the 21 polders covered in the project, 18 are in the southeastern part of the
country and 3 are in the southwest. The number of inhabitants in the project
area is about 1.2 million or about 200,000 households. About 70% are farmers.
The gross area is about 240,000 ha of which about 80% is under cultivation.
Agricultural productivity is low due to salinity and threat of cyclones.
Industrial activity is concentrated in the Chittagong area (included in the
PWP).
E. Detailed Features
Rehabilitation and ImDrovement Works
2.7       Maior Civil Works (base cost US$35.1 million).  The civil works
program would include: (a) major resectioning and/or retirement of embankments
(including resectioning of about 78 km of embankments and about 73 km of new
retired embankments) in areas where protection of embankments at the existing
locations is technically or economically not feasible; (b) about 7 km of new
or repaired protection works in the form of block revetments to protect
embankment slopes against damage due to cyclonic storms; (c) replacement or
repair of existing drainage structures (regulators or drainage sluices); and
(d) new drainage structures. The above program includes about US$3.4 million
of works to complete the PWP embankments. It also includes about US$2 million
of works, to be designed during the first year of project implementation, to
deal with recent erosion in a 6 km portion of one of the polders (Sandwip).
All works under this category would be carried out on contracts awarded
following ICB procedures.
2.8       Armored embankments for protection of agricultural land and the
population living in the protected area are not economically feasible or
justifiable. Therefore, a cost-effective design with lower maintenance cost
is adopted in the project. The sea-facing embankments are designed as
compacted earthfill dams with a protective layer of a meter of clay cover
along the outer slopes and crest, slopes of 1 on 7 on the sea-facing side and
1 on 3 on the country side, a 50 meter to 200 meter wide strip of foreshore
afforestation and a crest level established according to the following
criteria: for normal monsoons and cyclones of the intensity expected once in
five years, no significant overtopping would occur; for more severe cyclonic
storms expected once in 20 years, wave overtopping should not result in an
average water depth in the polder exceeding 1 meter; and for storms of the
intensity expected once in 40 years, crest level should not be lower than the
still water level. (For the Chittagong industrial areas financed under the
PWP, the design criteria used provide for no significant overtopping, even for
storms expected only once in 40 years). With these design criteria and
particularly the clay layer, the flatter slopes and the protection afforded by
afforestation and forest cover of the slopes, annual erosion of earthfill
would be limited to about 900 m3 per km. A very limited quantity of armored



- 15 -
protection is provided in places where retirement is not possible and no
foreshore area is available for afforestation. In such areas, revetment
blocks of sufficient weight and thickness and placed on geotextile filters are
provided. The regulators and surface sluices are designed according to BWDB
design criteria.
2.9       Minor Civil Works (base cost US$9.5 million).  The civil works
program would include: (a) minor embankment repairs mainly on the non-coastal
sections including any minor repairs to the sea-facing sections;3 (b) re-
excavation of the drainage network within the protected area and the outfall
canals; (c) minor repairs to existing revetment works; (d) repair or
replacement of drainage regulators in areas not included in the program of
coastal embankment repairs; (e) new surface sluices to drain isolated drainage
congested pockets within the protected area; and (f) surveying and
investigations limited to minor civil works. The slopes and crest width of
interior and marginal embankments of the minor works program would conform to
the Coastal Embankment Project design criteria, prepared by Leedshill-DeLeuw
Engineers and approved by BWDB. Crest levels would conform to the design
criteria of the major civil works. Works other than re-excavation of drainage
canals would be carried out on contracts awarded following NCB procedures.
Re-excavation of khals (base cost US$3.1 million) are expected to be carried
out under the Food For Work (FFW) program.
2.10      BWDB Engineering and Administration (base cost US$2.7 million).
Project costs include provision for BWDB engineering and administrative costs
directly related to the implementation of rehabilitation and improvement
works. The provision is estimated at 5% and 10% of the investment costs of
major and minor civil works respectively.
Afforestation (base cost US$5.3 million, plus US$1.8 million for leasing
private foreshore land)
2.11      The afforestation program includes: the development of forestry
models, nursery development and planting of appropriate species of trees and
shrubs on the embankment slopes and a 50 to 200 meter wide strip of forest on
the foreshore. This program forms an integral part of the embankment design.
The afforestation program covers about 1,900 ha of embankment and about 4,700
ha of foreshore development. The component would be implemented on a
community participation basis. FD would be directly responsible for the
foreshore afforestation component and would provide assistance to BWDB for the
embankmnent afforestation component. An agreement has been signed between BWDB
and FD, satisfactory to IDA, setting forth specific responsibilities and
arrangements for coordination. It was agreed at negotiations that maintenance
of such an agreement, satisfactory to IDA, would continue until Proiect
completion. NGOs active in the respective project areas would be employed to
mobilize the local people to participate in the project and provide training
in planting and maintenance of trees. FD would provide necessary technical
training to NGO staff, and possibly directly to community participants, and
3.    For one polder (Sonagazi) where only 2 km of sea-facing embankment
requires repair, this work is included in the minor works program.



- 16 -
technical supervision of forestry activities. In areas where private sector
produced seedlings of non-mangrove species are available and of suitable
quality, advantage would be taken of such availability. This is estimated to
cover about 30% of non-mangrove species. For the remainder, FD would have
overall responsibility for nursery operation and provision of seedlings. FD
would directly operate the nurseries for mangrove species and provide
technical supervision and funding support for establishment of NGO-operated
nurseries for the non-mangrove species. FD staff would also provide technical
assistance, if requested, for the private sector nurseries.
2.12      Squatters and those made landless by land acquisition and other
landless people (to the extent of available space) would be resettled at the
toe of the embankment on the countryside and allocated a homestead plot of
10 m by 20 m and an area of the embankment of about 1 ha.4 for each family.
Such settlers would be given long-term contracts (of the order of 15 to 30
years) allowing them to reside on this allotment and obtain the produce of
appropriate tree species planted in return for providing routine maintenance
of their section of the embankment and carrying out and maintaining an
afforestation program for their part of the embankment. NGOs would be
employed to help organize this process, while FD would provide technical
guidance and quality control for the embankment afforestation operations.
Such families would be paid a subsistence income for about four years until
the trees on the embankment produce sufficient income, but the payments would
decrease as the income from the trees increases. Participants would be paid
on a periodic schedule, based upon NGO certification that the embankment
maintenance and afforestation activities were adequately performed in
accordance with project requirements. NGOs would also supply participants
with fertilizer and pesticides for the first three years. The declining scale
of payments to be specified in the settlers' lease agreements has been set to
ensure that such payments, when added to the increasing income from the
homestead and the afforested embankment, would produce an adequate incentive
to cover both routine embankment maintenance and afforestation activities.
NGOs would monitor actual earnings from embankment afforestation and homestead
cultivation. In case of major disruptions in a polder from the assumed
afforestation program, which may occur due to significant storm damage or a
disease outbreak, NGOs would recommend appropriate adjustments in the agreed
4.    For two polders (out of the 21 in the project) there are too many
squatter families for the normal pattern to work. In portions of these
polders a variation of the arrangements described above for squatter
resettlement and embankment afforestation and maintenance would have to
be used. Groups would be organized to take responsibility for
embankment afforestation and maintenance, with each family responsible
for the maintenance and receiving the benefit of the produce of the
trees for a much smaller section of the embankment than in the standard
model (perhaps 1/4 to 1/8 ha). This would not be expected to provide
subsistence income but (together with the produce from the homestead
plot) would supplement whatever sources of income had been maintaining
the families up until now. In portions of the two polders, a
continuous berm would be constructed at the countryside toe of the
embankment for squatter resettlement.



- 17 -
scale of payments to the settlers. FD and BWDB would provide periodic quality
control checking, on a sample basis, of afforestation and embankment
maintenance activities respectively. The contracts with the settlers would
provide for an increasing scale of penalties for families who do not
adequately perform these duties, ranging from non-payment of cash income
through revoking the right to the produce from embankment afforestation, with
the possibility of eviction as a last resort if all other sanctions fail.
NGOs would recommend to BWDB appropriate sanctions, when necessary. (For
additional details see Annex 3). Model lease agreements with settlers were
agreed at negotiations.
2.13      Community groups organized by NGOs under FD supervision would be
responsible for the foreshore afforestation on publicly owned land. They
would be paid for their planting activities. Thereafter Landless Contracting
Societies (LCS) would be granted contracts under which they would share 2/3 of
the income from the trees in return for their handling of minimal maintenance
activities, guarding of the trees against poachers, harvesting, and replanting
of harvested areas. The remaining 1/3 of the income would go to the Union
Parishad for financing of local investments. Models of such agreements were
agreed at negotiations. An order has been issued allocating appropriate
publicly owned land for foreshore afforestation purposes. In areas where the
foreshore is privately owned, FD would hire community groups, organized and
supervised by NGOs, to plant the seedlings at no charge to the landowners, in
return for the landowners' agreement to maintain the area under trees. The
agreement with private owners of foreshore land would also provide that
landowners would receive in the case of any land not yet too saline for crop
production, the estimated value of the net income from foregone crop
production until income from the trees is available (about 8 years). The
agreement would permit landowners to use forest products according to sound
forestry practices, with the landowner obligated to replant trees in areas of
harvesting. Models of such agreements were agreed at negotiations. Foreshore
afforestation development is based on the premise that land would not require
acquisition. This is a realistic assumption as most of the foreshore is
public land and where the foreshore is privately owned, areas can be selected
which are relatively unproductive due to high salinity levels. (In only about
1,000 ha out of about 4,700 ha planned for foreshore afforestation are crops
currently grown.) In addition, after the recent cyclones, communities now
appreciate the importance of the coastal embankments in saving their lives and
property. The locally elected officials of the Union Parishad and influential
members of the community and the community at large should be able to settle
any problem of non-cooperation without being forced to resort to land
acquisition.
2.14      Models for embankment slope afforestation and foreshore development
have been prepared. Models for the embankment take account of the need for
species which are resistant to cyclonic winds and (on the outer slope)
salinity, have root structures which will strengthen the embankment, and which
would produce adequate income. Within these constraints, those allotted
sections of the embankment would be permitted some choice of species in



- 18 -
response to personal preferences and local market opportunities.5 Options
for suitable species for foreshore development take into consideration
variations in soils and tidal conditions. It is estimated that, due to loss
of trees, an average of two plantings would be required on the foreshore.
Based on this, it is estimated that about Tk 16,800 per ha would be required
for afforestation of embankment slopes, Tk 10,700 per ha for foreshore
afforestation with mangroves and Tk 30,200 per ha for foreshore afforestation
with upland species. An additional Tk 56.5 million is provided for
establishment and operation of nurseries. (For details see Annex 4). These
figures exclude the costs of technical assistance, FD staff and vehicles. The
subsistence payments to settlers until the trees provide adequate income are
budgeted under embankment O&M costs. (Annex 4).
Improved Operation and Maintenance (base cost US$5.4 million)
2.15      The project would support an improved O&M program for the 21
polders included under the project. All polder infrastructure, including the
sea-facing and non-coastal reaches of the embankment, drainage regulators,
surface sluices and the drainage network would be included in the program. In
the past, O&M activities were not clearly defined, allocations under the
recurrent budget were inadequate, BWDB field staff were not sufficiently
experienced, and the benefiting community was not involved in maintenance
activities. The severe damage caused by cyclonic storms and river erosion
aggravated the situation which forced the displaced families whose land had
been eroded to resettle on the embankment. Cuts on the embankment to
accommodate houses and cultivation of the slopes with annual crops weakened
the embankment. In these conditions, no effective system of O&M could be
carried out. The improved O&M program has been designed taking into
consideration these issues. The major change is in the maintenance system and
the involvement of the community in the simple maintenance tasks. Operational
activities are adequately spelled out and BWDB staff are sufficiently
experienced to carry out these tasks once the works are rehabilitated and the
community has been mobilized to handle routine embankment maintenance. The
improved O&M program would include: (a) routine or preventive maintenance;
(b) periodic maintenance; and (c) repairs caused by cyclonic storms, monsoon
floods, and scours to embankment slopes caused by wave action. The project
provides funds for these activities, including the costs required for
involvement of the community in the maintenance program. Further details are
provided in Annex 5.
2.16      Community involvement is crucial to the sustainability of the
proposed system. The families resettled on the toe of the embankment (as
described in para 2.12 above) would be responsible for routine maintenance of
their allotted section of the embankment. NGOs involved with the
5.    Hedge rows of vetiver grass would be planted just below the crest of
the embankment in a small pilot area. Depending upon the results,
vetiver or other hedge row species would be used in the general model
along the crest of the embankment. Longer term experiments will
determine whether the tree canopies allow sufficient light to penetrate
to permit vetiver to grow on other portions of the embankment.



- 19 -
afforestation component would also help organize the community participation
in routine embankment maintenance. BWDB would provide technical guidance and
quality control for the embankment maintenance operations. The income
payments to the participants have been set on a declining scale over four
years (taking account of anticipated increases in income from the embankment
and homestead over this period) to provide adequate incentive (see para 2.12),
and would cover the routine embankment maintenance responsibilities as well as
the work in the initial years of planting trees on the embankment. This would
be budgeted as embankment O&M expenditure (which is financed on a declining
percentage basis under the proposed credit). However, payment to participants
would be contingent on NGO certification that both embankment maintenance and
afforestation responsibilities were being carried out adequately. Routine
earthwork maintenance on portions of embankments not being rehabilitated under
the project would be carried out under labor contracts by the community in
groups of about 25 persons on average, organized by NGOs. Periodic
maintenance of all earthworks, which would be carried out annually or
biannually, would be undertaken under labor contracts by the community or by
small scale contracts awarded following NCB procedures. Controlling
activities at the structures, presently the responsibility of Khalashis, would
gradually be transferred to the community. Committees in each Union Parishad
would be formed to assist in supervising maintenance activities and preventing
future squatters. BWDB would continue to provide technical supervision and
financial control of all maintenance activities.
2.17      Project costs include the routine and periodic maintenance
activities for polders included in the project. The project would also
provide for repairs to damages caused by cyclonic storms occurring during the
project period. (This would include allowance for payments for routine
maintenance to resume temporarily if the cyclones damaged trees on the
embankment to the extent that they could no longer provide adequate
subsistence income.) Repairs to damages due to monsoon floods and embankment
slope scours would also be included. (Repairs of damages to embankment slopes
caused by river bank erosion is not included in the Project.) Disbursements
by IDA for maintenance activities would be on a declining percentage basis.
The Government agreed to include in its budget annual allocations for
operation and maintenance of proiect facilities in accordance with a timetable
satisfactory to IDA. Tentative estimates of annual budgetary requirements for
O&M were agreed at negotiations, subject to further review based upon
experience in maintaining these embankments. These estimates cover routine
and periodic maintenance activities, and repairs following "normal" floods and
cyclones of an intensity occurring with a frequency of once in five years.
The O&M budgeting and monitoring procedures being developed in four pilot
subprojects under the SRP project would be introduced in CERP polders to
assure adequate provision for routine and periodic O&M.
Land Acauisition and Resettlement (base cost US$3.3 million for Land
Acquisition, US$0.4 million for Resettlement)
2.18      A total of about 675 ha of land would be required for providing
project facilities. For reconstruction work, about 270 ha are required for
retirements, about 175 ha are required for widening of existing embankments
(including paying for land under one existing embankment where the land had



- 20 -
been previously made available on a voluntary basis, without transfer of
ownership), about 55 ha are required for structures, and about 35 ha for minor
works (excluding Khal excavation). For resettlement of embankment squatters
and other landless people who will participate in embankment O&M and
afforestation, about 35 ha would be required. For the temporary acquisition
of spoil dump areas needed for the re-excavation of khals, about 105 ha would
be required. (Cost of temporary acquisition is estimated at about a third of
the land value). Acquisition is kept to a minimum by limiting areas to only
those actually required for the embankment sections being constructed and for
homestead allotments. Payment of royalty for the fill materials would be the
responsibility of contractors. According to procedures adopted for the FFW
program, no provision is made for land acquisition for re-excavation of khals
except for the spoil dump areas. Takeover of acquired land could be timed in
most cases after the aman harvest to avoid damage to standing crops.
2.19      A Resettlement Action Plan acceptable to IDA has been adopted by
the Government. Agreement was reached during negotiations that resettlement
and rehabilitation of persons adversely affected by the prolect would be
carried out in accordance with the provisions of the Resettlement Action Plan.
About 700 families who are presently squatting on embankment sections to be
rehabilitated, and about 1300 other landless families would be resettled under
the project on new homestead plots at the toe of the embankment. The
embankment squatters on sections being rehabilitated would have to move prior
to the start of construction. It is expected that most would opt to move
immediately to new homes at the back of the homestead plots, which would still
allow contractors enough room on the berm for construction activities.6
Alternatively, they would be given the option of a rental allowance for about
a year to cover the costs of temporarily renting a plot or a house for a year
during construction. During the construction period, such settlers would be
given priority by FD for employment in the afforestation of the foreshore, to
the extent that they are not absorbed in project construction activities. The
above arrangements allow people displaced by the project to remain in their
communities, a short distance from their previous homes, and to earn income,
following project construction activities, from the produce of the trees to be
planted on the embankment. Landowners losing a portion of their property due
to land acquisition for the project (about 1,800 families) would be given cash
compensation sufficient to purchase replacement land of similar earning
capacity, including the value of trees, fish ponds, and other assets. All
families being resettled (the squatters and about 200 families who would have
to move their houses to other parts of their property plus the four families
losing all their property) would receive funds equivalent to the replacement
costs of the houses being demolished, taking into account the costs for moving
their homes and household belongings and the cost of new materials for
rebuilding their homes (deducting the value of salvagable materials). Similar
compensation for relocation of shops or factories would be provided for about
22 families. Any landowner receiving compensation less than three months
6.    About 80 squatter families who will have to relocate during the first
year of the project will be paid a rental allowance for about a year to
allow time for acquisition of land for their new homestead plots, plus
moving costs.



- 21 -
prior to eviction would be paid additional compensation to cover estimated
loss of income until new land or other assets can be purchased. Any farmer
using non-owned land under arrangements of duration greater than a year would
also receive compensation for loss of income and any advance payment he had
made for the land. The timing of takeover of land would be set to minimize
loss of standing crops. However, anyone losing a standing crop would be
compensated for the estimated value at harvest. Project costs include the
cost of relocation of the squatters and those losing their homes due to land
acquisition as resettlement costs and the costs of other compensation as land
acquisition costs. Consultants (including NGOs) and vehicles for land
acquisition purposes are included within the technical assistance and vehicles
categories. Further details are provided in Annex 9.
Surveys. Investigations and Studies (base cost US$0.5 million)
2.20      This category would include (a) surveys required prior to minor
civil works construction; and (b) studies to investigate the possibility of
cost-effective town protection works for Sandwip. The surveys under (a) above
would be carried out by BWDB. The major part of the work under (b) would be
carried out by the Ministry of Water Resources' River Research Institute, with
some input from BWDB and the project consultants described in para. 2.21
below.
Technical Assistance (base cost US$10.6 million)
2.21      Technical assistance would be provided for Project support
(including implementation supervision and assistance) and training. A
substantial amount is required for project support to ensure effective
contract management and supervision of civil works, taking account of the
scattered nature of the construction sites. Prior projects directly
supervised by BWDB have had problems with construction quality due in part to
inadequate site supervision staff and lack of proper training in these
aspects. Proiect support (base cost US$9.6 million) would include:
(a) contract management and supervision of all major civil works, including
any inputs for additional designs required during the construction period;
(b) review of designs of all minor civil works, including making amendments as
required, and assistance in preparing tender documents, evaluation of bids and
supervision of construction of such works; (c) technical advice and assistance
for afforestation development (including pilot operations testing use of
vetiver on embankments); (d) assistance in planning and introducing effective
systems for operating and maintaining the completed polders; (e) assistance in
involving the community in planting of trees under the afforestation program
and routine embankment maintenance activities; (f) assistance in periodically
monitoring the physical and social impact of the project, including
identifying adverse environmental effects and proposing mitigating measures to
minimize or eliminate these effects; (g) coordination between this project and
on-going efforts under other GOB and NGO projects in the project area
(particularly the IDA/ODA supported Agriculture Support Services Project) to
assist farmers in the project area to take advantage of the opportunities for
increased production resulting from the project; (h) assistance in
implementation and monitoring of land acquisition/resettlement; (i) assistance
to the Sandwip town protection studies referred to in para 2.20(b); and



- 22 -
(j) assistance to BWDB during 1995 for pre-construction activities. Item
(e) above would be carried out largely by NGOs active in the project area.
Item (h) is likely to be carried out by the local firm which prepared the
resettlement plan, using NGOs active in individual polders as subcontractors.
Services for item (j) are being carried out by the PWP consultants and the
local resettlement plan consultants. The remaining items above (as well as
provision of some assistance to NGOs involved in item (e) above) would be
provided by a firm of consultants with terms of reference satisfactory to IDA.
Consultancy inputs by the main project consultants are estimated at 326 staff
months of internationally recruited senior specialists, 736 staff months of
locally recruited senior specialists, 568 staff months of locally recruited
junior professionals, and 1172 staff months of locally recruited technical
assistants. In addition, there would be about 140 incremental NGO staff
participating under the project. Further details of the consultancy and NGO
arrangements are given in Annexes 6 and 3 respectively, with item (h) above
covered in Annex 9.  Agreement was reached during negotiations that
consultants, with terms of reference acceptable to IDA, would be appointed
prior to siqnature of civil work contracts.  The framework for contracts
between BWDB and NGOs was agreed during negotiations and agreement reached
that the BWDB would enter into such contracts with NGOs satisfactory to IDA by
February 15, 1996.
2.22      Training (base cost US$1.0 million) would be directed specifically
at: (a) improving the skills of BWDB subdivisional office staff in
construction supervision and O&M and in the social aspects of community
involvement and dealing with displaced people; (b) improving the awareness of
environmental issues within BWDB (which is intended to have impact on project
planning and implementation beyond the present project); (c) providing
training to FD and NGO staff in community development activities, nursery
development and forestry planting and maintenance, NGO staff in routine
embankment maintenance, and community participants in afforestation and
routine embankment maintenance. In addition, the project would include
continuation of a program initiated under the PWP for training of senior and
mid-level BWDB staff in all aspects of contract management and supervision.
The same consulting firm providing implementation support assistance, as
described in para. 2.21 above, would also be responsible for designing and
coordinating the training programs and recruiting a team of appropriate local
trainers. Requirements have been estimated at about 12 consultant months of
an internationally recruited specialist and 228 consultant months of locally
recruited senior trainers. Further details on requirements and activities to
be performed for all project components are given in Annex 7.
Vehicles and Eciuipment (base cost of procurement US$0.9 million, operating
costs US$0.4 million)
2.23      The project would provide vehicles and minor equipment for the use
of the Project Implementation Unit (PIU) (see para 3.1), BWDB field divisions,
and forestry field staff. Miscellaneous minor equipment such as off-ce and
drafting equipment, environmental monitoring equipment, and a limited number
of computers are also provided. It was agreed at negotiations that all
vehicles and eauipment. other than replacement motorcycles or bicycles for
field staff, would be Procured by December 31, 2000. The estimated costs for



- 23 -
the running of such vehicles and equipment are included as part of the
Government's contribution to the project. Vehicles and equipment to be used
by the consultants and NGOs are included in the cost estimates for technical
assistance services.
Community Development Fund (base cost of US$0.4 million)
2.24      This Fund is not part of the IDA-financed project but is expected
to be included in the EC grant and would finance poverty allieviation
activities complementary to the project. The proposed Community Development
Fund would provide resources for NGOs to implement poverty alleviation
activities aimed at strengthening project operations and group cohesion. This
could involve, for example, water supplies and sanitary facilities for
embankment settlers, community training and education programs, and income
generating activities. The latter could in particular aim at processing and
marketing of project outputs such as coconuts. The fund might also be used to
provide credit for the purchase of hand tools by community groups engaged in
embankment maintenance. The fund would be administered by the Project
Implementation Unit (PIU) described in para 3.1. NGOs would submit proposals
to the PIU for activities to be funded under eligibility criteria agreed
between GOB and EC. Implementation of approved activities would be the
responsibility of NGOs contracted for project implementation. The PIU would
closely monitor activities funded to ensure that they directly support key
project objectives, and do not divert NGO staff time from the support and
implementation of afforestation and embankment O&M.
F. Status of Preparation and Implementation Schedule
2.25      Preparation.  Detailed designs and tender documents have been
prepared for the major reconstruction works of the project (other than one
small contract for completion of a PWP polder and a second to deal with a six
km section of another polder affected by recent erosion). Prequalified
contractors were invited in August 1995 to bid for these major works. Review
of tenders and award of contracts is expected to be completed by January 1996.
The preliminary designs and estimates for all minor works have been prepared
by BWDB. It is estimated that by the end of FY96, the project consultants
could prepare detailed designs and tender documents for all the minor works.
Proposals and estimates for embankment afforestation and improved O&M
components have been prepared. Given the pioneering nature of the community
participation approach for embankment afforestation and a portion of the O&M
work, provision is being made for two mid-term reviews (see para 3.9) to
permit adjustments based on experience.
2.26      Implementation Schedule (Annex 1). The total project
implementation period would be seven years (FY96-FY02). However,
implementation after December 31, 2000 would be limited to the afforestation
and improved O&M components, together with limited inputs for associated
project support and training, and activities supported by the Community
Development Fund. Contracts for reconstruction work for most of the sea-
facing embankments would be awarded following ICB procedures in time to permit
construction to start in March 1996. Work would be completed in three



- 24 -
construction seasons (ending June 1998). The contracts for completion of the
sea-facing embankment in one of the PWP polders, major structures in another
PWP polder and works in the section affected by erosion would be tendered a
year later. Minor civil works would commence in FY97 and be completed within
a period of five years. The longer implementation period is required due to
implementation of portions through the FFW program, limited number of local
contractors able to produce quality work, and utilization of community groups
for earthworks. It was agreed at neqotiations that the Proposed credit would
only finance civil works expenditures incurred throucrh December 31. 2000. As
soon as construction conditions permit, sections of the ermibankment and the
foreshore would be afforested. It is estimated that the planting of trees
would commence in FY97 for the PWP embankments and the foreshore, and in FY99
for the other major work embankments. Initial planting would be substantially
completed by FY00. (Replanting where required and planting on minor works
embankments would continue through the end of the project.) Forestry trials
