and pilot schemes on about 40 ha of embankment and 120 ha of foreshore would
be taken up in FY96. These would produce data useful for successful
implementation of the afforestation program and the program for community
participation in embankment maintenance. The embankment maintenance program
would commence for sections of embankment when contractor's one year defects
liability period is completed. The schedule for the reconstruction program
indicates that the maintenance program would start for some embankment
sections (particularly sections completed under the PWP) by FY96, and that all
works would be included in the maintenance program during FY01 and FY02.
Project support and training would commence in FY96 and continue throughout
the project period, with limited inputs during the last two years.
G. Environmental Aspects
2.27      The proposed project has been placed in environmental screening
Category B. Most physical works consist of rehabilitation and improvements of
existing infrastructure in the polders. The project would improve the
environmental conditions by providing additional security against cyclone and
monsoon damage, ensuring that salinity intrusion is controlled, increasing
forested areas and reducing or eliminating drainage congestion with consequent
reductions in health hazards. The rehabilitation program would not have
adverse effects on fisheries, since the polders of the Mid-Term Program have
no permanent water bodies (beels or haors), and since the embankments do not
close up major or medium rivers or shoot channels of these rivers which are
the main routes for migration of fish to the spawning grounds in flood plains.
Regulator operations on the khals would be designed to facilitate fishing.
Shrimp inlets have been included where necessary. Freshwater pond fish
cultivation (dominated by carp) would be benefited by protection from cyclone
surges and saline intrusion into ponds. The borrow pits inside the
embankments could also be used to increase pond fish cultivation. There would
be some increase in the use of fertilizer and pesticide in the project area,
since the project would enable farmers to switch from local to high yielding
varieties. However, over-use of fertilizer and pesticides is not generally a
problem in Bangladesh. Farmer training in proper fertilizer and pesticide use
is included under the ongoing IDA/ODA financed Agricultural Support Services
Project (Cr. 2233-BD). The proposed project provides for the resettlement of



- 25 -
squatters and others who may become landless as a result of land acquisition
(see paras 2.12 and 2.19). The technical assistance component includes funds
for monitoring the effects of project works during the implementation period
and training to increase environmental awareness within BWDB. Financing for
mitigating measures required to remedy adverse effects would be available
under the rehabilitation and improvement component. Further details on
environmental aspects are in Annex 10.
H. Project Cost
2.28      The total project cost is estimated at US$87.8 million equivalent,
with a foreign exchange component of US$26.2 million or about 30% of the total
cost (Table 2.1). The civil works cost estimates are based on prices obtained
in 1992 in the tenders for the major civil works under the PWP, adjusted for
inflation and differences between the proposed Coastal Embankment
Rehabilitation Project (CERP) and PWP, and results of tenders under other
ongoing IDA projects. The rates for recent NCB tenders are used for cost
estimates for the minor civil works. Estimates for quantities are taken from
the preliminary designs prepared by the PWP consultant and BWDB. The
estimates for the afforestation program are based on the PWP consultant's
proposals, FD estimates and an assessment carried out by the appraisal team.
For O&M, estimates are based on the PWP consultant's proposals with
adjustments made based on estimates of length of project embankment affected
by cyclonic storms (to take account of the fact that the non-coastal
embankments would require less maintenance), and for the cost of declining
scale payments to families engaged in embankment maintenance and afforestation
until the income of the produce from trees planted on the embankment is
sufficient to maintain the family (see para 2.12).
2.29      The project cost includes US$7.0 million in taxes and duties.
Physical contingencies are estimated at 15% for major civil works and range
between 10% and 15% for the minor civil works, depending on the nature of the
works. Allowance for physical contingencies is not provided for afforestation
or other goods and services. Price contingencies and foreign exchange rates
are estimated on the basis of expected rates of local and foreign inflation.
Local inflation is estimated at rates ranging from 4% to 5% per annum during
the project period while foreign inflation is estimated at 2.2% per annum.

for: embankment rehabilitation and improvement works; vehicles and equipment
for GOB agencies; BWDB engineering and administration costs; surveys and
studies carried out by GOB staff; and the recurrent costs for improved O&M (at
a declining percentage). An EC grant of ECU 15 million (US$20.1 million
equivalent at June 1995 exchange rates) would cover about 25% of the project
cost excluding taxes, plus the Community Development Fund. EC financing for
the project would be used to finance afforestation, private foreshore leasing,

2.32      All major works on the sea-facing embankments, estimated to cost
$42.0 million equivalent, (involving retired or resectioned embankments or
block revetment protection works, and repairs or replacements of drainage
structures in areas of embankment improvement) would be carried out under
contracts awarded following ICB procedures. In bid evaluations under ICB
procedures, eligible domestic contractors would receive a preference of 7.5'.
All of the above works in a polder would be offered as a package. Where such
a package would be too small to interest international bidding, works in two
polders would be combined in one package. One large minor works contract for
3 relatively large structures for Chakoria polder, estimated to cost about
$1.6 million equivalent, would also be procured through ICB. The other minor
civil works, estimated to cost $6.4 million equivalent and to average about
$0.2 million per contract, are relatively small, scattered and located in
places of difficult access and in addition most are labor-intensive. Such
works would therefore be carried out under contracts let through standard
National competitive bidding (NCB) procedures acceptable to IDA. For drainage
structures included in the minor works program, a separate tender would be
invited for all structures in a polder. The packages would include new and
replacement structures, and structures requiring repairs. Such packages would
include the supply and installation of gates and lifting mechanisms. For NCB
contracts for minor works. it was agreed at neQotiations that particivation
would be limited to bidders of Class A registration (or foreicrn firms meetinci
eauivalent standards).  In addition Pre-aualification of bidders in a manner
satisfactory to IDA would be recauired for contracts for structures.
Earthworks would be aroused to provide a minimum contract size of US$150,000
eauivalent.
2.33      For civil works estimated to cost more than US$150,000, bid
documents would be submitted to IDA for prior review, and IDA's concurrence
would be obtained before contracts are awarded. This would cover all
investment costs on civil works. It is estimated that the prior review
exercise would involve about 40 contract packages (9 for major works and 30
for minor works). Contracts less than US$150,000 would be limited to the
maintenance works for the embankments and structures. Periodic maintenance
works would either be awarded to community maintenance groups at the
engineer's estimated prices or implemented through contracts awarded following
NCB procedures to small local contractors. For contracts estimated to cost
US$150,000 or less, bid documents and evaluations would be retained by BWDB
and made available to IDA representatives upon request. Force account work in
respect of earthworks would be carried out only in exceptional circumstances,
such as an emergency operation, and would be restricted to amounts under
US$10,000 for each case up to a aggregate of US$500,000. Maintenance work
following a cyclone, monsoon storms or floods, for which partial financing is
available under the project, would be carried out generally in packages
costing less than US$150,000, through contracts awarded following NCB
procedures. In addition community groups could be awarded some of these
works. The procedure for contracting and reviewing of bid documents would be
similar to that described above for periodic maintenance. Routine maintenance
would be carried out by individual families (see para 2.16).
2.34      Earthworks included in the investment costs for re-excavation of
khals, valued at US$3.6 million equivalent (including contingencies), are



- 30 -
expected to be executed under FFW programs according to procedures agreed
between GOB and WFP. Procurement of vehicles and equipment valued at US$0.7
million would be through NCB procedures. Contracts of US$100,000 or more
would be subject to prior review. The procurement in this category is spread
over seven different types of vehicles (4 wheel drive vehicles, motorcycles,
bicycles, boats, rickshaw vans, etc.) Foreign firms with appropriate vehicle
maintenance facilities to be eligible to bid would have local agents in
Bangladesh. Small items and a small quantity of vehicles required for
facilitating an early start to implementation would be procured on a prudent
shopping basis and limited to US$50,000 for a package, up to an aggregate of
US$200,000. Consultants, training, the services of NGOs, and resettlement
costs would be financed by the European Community and would be procured
following EC guidelines. The contracts for consultants and NGO services would
include the costs of vehicles and equipment for their use. BWDB/FD would
select NGOs based on criteria acceptable to IDA/EC which would include the
NGOs' experience in the respective project areas, demonstrated leadership and
staff capabilities, and ability to work with target groups, local government
representatives and implementing agencies. NGO contracts would be subject to
IDA and EC a2roval.
K. Disbursements
2.35      Disbursements of the IDA Credit would extend over the period 1995-
2002. However, disbursements after December 31, 2000 would be limited to
improved O&M activities and replacement motorcycles or bicycles for field
staff supervising O&M and afforestation. IDA disbursements would cover:
(a) 90% of civil works expenditures, including construction materials
but excluding khal excavation;
(b) 100% of BWDB estimated expenditure on administration and
engineering of project works, based on 5% and 10% of the cost of
major and minor civil works;
(c) 90% of costs of surveys, investigations and studies carried out by
GOB agencies;
(d) an average of about 65% of expenditures on improved O&M activities,
to be disbursed at 85% of expenditures through FY99, 65% for FY00
and FY01, and 45% thereafter; and
(e) 100% of foreign expenditures on directly imported vehicles and
equipment, 100% of the ex-factory price of such goods manufactured
in Bangladesh, and 70% of expenditures for off the shelf locally
procured goods.
2.36      Full documentation would be sent to IDA in support of withdrawal
applications, except for items (b), (c), and (d) above, small civil work
contracts costing less than the equivalent of US$150,000 each, and contracts
for vehicles or equipment costing less than the equivalent of $100,000 each.
For these items, applications for the BWDB components would be supported by

statements of expenditure certified by the Project Director of the PIU. The
relevant documentation would be retained at the PIU office and should be
available for review by IDA representatives on request. This documentation

2.37      The disbursement schedule (Annex 1) reflects the anticipated
sequence of activities in implementation and has been based on the assumption
that disbursement for works in one quarter would be completed within the next
two quarters. The average disbursement period for water development projects
in Bangladesh has been nine years. However, a five-year implementation period
for civil works construction is considered feasible in view of the intended
tendering for the major works and commencement of land acquisition procedures
before Board presentation, use of consultants and ICB procurement for the
major works, and community pressure for expeditious action based upon the
experience of the 1991 cyclone.
2.38      Timely settlement of progress payments to contractors is crucial to
the speedy implementation of project works. To facilitate payments, ensure
the availability of funds, and reduce the number of withdrawal applications
submitted to IDA, GOB would open and thereafter maintain a special account in
U.S. dollars with the Bangladesh Bank in Dhaka.  The authorized allocation for
the special account would be US$4.0 million equivalent. This allocation
represents an estimated four months of IDA-financed expenditure during the
peak construction period. The special account would be replenished through
reimbursements on a monthly basis and would be used exclusively for IDA's
share of financing.
L. Accounts and Audits
2.39      BWDB and FD would maintain adequate records, in line with
guidelines on financial reporting and auditing of World Bank projects, to
identify physical progress and financial transactions relating to the project.
Individually identifiable accounts would also be kept for: (a) all
expenditure for which credit withdrawals are made on the basis of statements
of expenditure (para 2.36); and (b) the flow of funds from the Special
Account. Separate project accounts would be kept by BWDB and FD. Auditors
acceptable to IDA would audit these accounts, including statements of
expenditure and operation of the Special Account. Audited accounts, together
with auditors' comments, would be sent to IDA within six months of the close
of the fiscal year. At present, accounts of BWDB and the FD are audited by
the Comptroller and Auditor-General of Bangladesh. BWDB accounts are also
audited by its Internal Directorate of Audits. These arrangements are
satisfactory to IDA.  Agreement on the above arrangements was confirmed during
negotiations.

III. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEIHNT
3.1       A Project Implementation Unit (PIU) would be established in
Chittagong, under the Member implementation, with a Project Director of the
rank of a Chief or Additional Chief Engineer responsible for the overall
management for all BWDB components of the project and preparing and submitting
quarterly progress reports (including sections to be submitted by FD on
afforestation activities). The Project Director would be assisted by two
Superintending Engineers, who would be Deputy Project Directors for
Implementation and O&M, a Deputy Director Resettlement who would head the
Project Resettlement Unit, and the project consultants. FD would appoint a
full-time Project Director Afforestation (para 3.4), based in Chittagong, who
would head a small separate Forestry Project Implementation Unit (FPIU) and
would, inter alia, liaise with the PIU to ensure coordination between the
activities of the civil works and afforestation components and the respective
maintenance activities. A Project Coordinator, based in Dhaka, at the
Superintending Engineer level, will assist the Project Director in monitoring
and coordination. The PIU would be responsible, inter alia for:
(a) planning the implementation of the project, in cooperation with the
field divisions, including reviewing and consolidating annual
budget requests prepared by the field divisions, monitoring
progress of project implementation and taking action required to
eliminate bottlenecks, and keeping track of implementation
schedules to facilitate coordination among construction,
afforestation, land acquisition and resettlement, and O&M
activities;
(b) coordinating the activities of the main project consultants and
contracting with and coordinating the work of the resettlement
consultants and NGOs assisting in implementation of the embankment
maintenance/afforestation components;
(c) overseeing the preparation and processing of tender documents for
minor civil works by field divisions and award of contracts;
(d) preparing and processing of tender documents and the award of
contracts for procurement of BWDB equipment and vehicles, and for
completion of portions of major civil works not completed under
PWP, and additional works to cope with recent erosion at the
southern end of Sandwip;
(e) monitoring project environmental and social impact and organizing
implementation of appropriate mitigating measures;
(f) overseeing project accounts and ensuring timely submission of
disbursement requests to IDA;
(g) assisting the Training Directorate in preparing and executing O&M
training programs for the CERP polders;



- 33 -
(h) coordinating preparation of proposals for utilization of the
Community Development Fund and implementation of approved
activities under this Fund by NGOs; and
(i) preparing six-monthly reports on the progress of the project.
A PIU, with terms of reference satisfactory to IDA, has been established and
the Project Director and the two Deputy Project Directors and the Deputy
Director Resettlement have been appointed. The maintenance of the PIU until
prolect completion was acqreed at negotiations.
3.2       Field operations for the rehabilitation and improvement components
would be carried out through: (i) two new and one existing implementation
circles, located at Cox's Bazaar, Noahkhali and Barisal respectively, headed
by Superintending Engineers; (ii) an implementation unit for the works in
Chittagong area, directly controlled by PIU, and headed by the Deputy Project
Director, Implementation; (iii) seven implementation divisions, headed by
Executive Engineers, two located at Cox's Bazaar, one each at Chittagong,
Sonapur, Ramgati, Barguna, and Sharankhola; and (iv) thirteen implementation
subdivisions one for each polder with ICB works. By adjustments to the areas
of jurisdiction of the existing O&M divisions and the transfer of some of the
O&M divisions to implementation, it is proposed that the divisional set up
would consist of three new divisions and four divisions transferred from the
existing O&M set up. Project Consultants would provide substantial assistance
(described in more detail in Annex 6). Responsibilities of the implementation
divisions, in association with the divisional and site staff of the
consultant, would include: the construction supervision and quality control
of civil works; preparation of tender documents and selection of contractors
for minor works; accounting and financial control of construction contracts;
coordinating with the Project Resettlement Units to ensure that land
acquisition is accomplished as required by the contract schedules; and
coordination with the O&M divisions to ensure that maintenance work starts as
soon as appropriate sections of construction work have been completed.
3.3       Six existing divisions, located at Cox's Bazaar, Chittagong,
Noahkhali, Lokhipur, Barguna, and Bagerhat, would function under the direction
of the existing O&M Zonal Chief Engineers and Circle Superintending Engineers
and would carry out all operations and maintenance activities in the PWP and
CERP polders. The Superintending Engineer of the O&M Circle at Chittagong
would, in addition to his other duties, serve as Deputy Project Director O&M,
and would assist the Project Director in coordinating project O&M activities.
O&M works would be carried out under five existing O&M circles located at
Cox's Bazaar, Chittagong, Feni, Faridpur and Khulna, which would continue to
be responsible for their current work in addition to the work under the
project. Eighteen subdivisions would be set up covering one or two polders.
This would be achieved through a combination of changing the jurisdiction of
some existing subdivisions and creating new subdivisions where necessary.
Responsibilities of the O&M wing would include: (a) operation of regulators
and sluices; (b) preparation of tender documents and award and supervision of
contracts for periodic maintenance of embankments and drainage canals, and
routine maintenance by Contractors; (c) assistance tc NGOs and quality control
of routine maintenance by embankment settlers; and (d) accounting and



- 34 -
financial control of O&M activities, including payments to embankment settlers
and NGOs for activities concerning embankment maintenance and afforestation.
3.4       Forest Department (FD) activities would be coordinated by a FPIU in
Chittagong, under the direction of a senior officer (of Division Forest
Officer or higher rank) who would be designated Project Director
Afforestation. The project responsibilities of BWDB and FD and the
implementation arrangements for afforestation has been set out in an agreement
between the two organizations. FD would be responsible for foreshore
afforestation (including mangrove nurseries), technical assistance and funding
support for NGO establishment of nurseries for non-mangrove species (for both
embankment slopes and foreshore), and providing assistance to BWDB for
embankment afforestation activities (mainly providing advice and training to
NGOs organizing these activities). The agreement includes provision for close
coordination between the PIU and the FPIU in the implementation of the
project. Work would be carried out by three units headed by Assistant
Conservators of Forests assigned to the project by FD. These three units
would be established at Chittagong, Noahkhali, and Patuakhali respectively.
Nursery operations for the mangroves and assistance to NGOs in nursery
operations for other species would be carried out by regular staff of the
divisions participating in the project. The extension wing would contract
services with local NGOs to organize and train community participants to
engage in the afforestation components of the project (as described in paras
2.12 and 2.13), and supervise their work. FD would be responsible for the
procurement of the vehicles included in the project for this component. FD
would keep separate accounts for foreshore afforestation, its own nursery
activities, and advances to NGOs for nursery establishment. The FPIU would
maintain adequate accounting staff for this purpose. The FPIU would also
supply information to the PIU for inclusion in consolidated quarterly progress
reports. The FPIU has been established with terms of reference satisfactory
to IDA, and the Project Director Afforestation has been appointed. The
maintenance of FPIU until proliect completion was agreed at negotiations.
3.5       Land acauisition and resettlement activities would be carried out
by the Project Resettlement Unit of the PIU, headed by a Deputy Director
Resettlement located in Chittagong, and work would be carried out by four
subunits established at Cox's Bazaar, Chittagong, Noahkhali, and Barguna.
Each subunit would have a Regional Project Resettlement Officer, a Revenue
Officer, an Accountant, and a Surveyor. The consultants who prepared the
draft resettlement plan with regard to land to be acquired for embankment

assist the Deputy Commissioners and the Project Resettlement Unit to establish
entitlements for land required for the first year of construction.14
Entitlements specified under the law would be paid by the Deputy
Commissioners, while additional entitlements agreed under the project (Annex
9) would be paid by the Project Resettlement Unit. Special Land Acquisition
Officers deputed to the Deputy Commissioners would be appointed at Cox's
Bazaar, Chittagong, and Laxmipur to assist the Deputy Commissioners in dealing

site offices at each polder headed by Assistant Resident Engineers. The site

3.7       It is envisaged that between 10 to 20 NGOs would be involved in the

responsible for presenting NGO views to the Government, identifying training
needs, and coordinating the sharing of experience of NGOs. This

coordinating committees described below. Each participating NGO would appoint
a senior officer as its project director, responsible for contractual matters
and overall supervision of its project-related activities.
Coordinating Committees
3.8       A Project Coordinating Committee, chaired by the Project Director,
would meet at a minimum of every three months to review progress and problems
and issue necessary policy decisions and directives. This committee would
include as members the Project Director Afforestation, the Deputy Project
Directors for Implementation and O&M, the Deputy Director Resettlement,
Director Field Services Division, Department of Agriculture and Extension

Deputy Commissioner and would include as members two Executive Engineers

would be set up in each Thana in the Project Area, headed by the Thana Nirbani
Officer and including as members the Union Parishad chairmen for all Unions in
the Thana included in the Project Area, the BWDB Subdivisional Engineer, the

beneficiary comments and suggestions, assist in land acquisition and

activities, supervise the adequacy of the embankment O&M and afforestation
activities (organizing community pressure in case of problems), and provide
community support in preventing encroachment on the embankment by squatters.
Agreement was reached during negotiations that the committees, as described
above, would be established by November 15. 1995 and that such committees
would be maintained throughout the prolect implementation period.
Mid-Term Reviews
3.9       To examine project progress, GOB would carry out two mid-term
reviews, at the end of the second and fourth project year. PIU and FD would
organize the reviews and prepare terms of reference in consultation with IDA.
The reviews would focus on resolving any remaining bottlenecks in the civil
work programs and adjusting the project in response to lessons drawn from
experience under the O&M and afforestation components. The first of the two
reviews would benefit from the initial experience in applying the O&M and
afforestation components in the PWP polders, while the second review would
provide opportunities for mid-term corrections based upon the experience with
the polders with major civil works financed under this project. The reviews
would provide a detailed examination of problems encountered and make
recommendations for improving project implementation and modifying the
project, if found appropriate. The project consultants and participating NGOs
would participate in these reviews. AQreement was reached with GOB durinQ
neaotiations that it would undertake these reviews, discuss the findings with
IDA. and revise the Project Proforma. as necessary, to oromptlv reflect
proiect revisions agreed with IDA.
IV.   PRODUCTION. MARKETING. AND FARM INCOMES
A. Agricultural Production
4.1       Estimation of the project's expected impact on agricultural
production and farm incomes has been based on a detailed analysis of farming
practices and agro-ecological conditions in each of the 11 polders with major
works financed under the project. The methodology, major assumptions, and
15.   Other members could be added if GOB so wishes.



- 37 -
detailed results are presented in Annex 8. The project is expected to lead to
increased agricultural production due to three major factors: (a) prevention
of salinity intrusions and storm surges during normal monsoons and cyclones;
(b) adoption of high yielding varieties (HYVs) because the increased security
provided against periodic devastation during severe cyclones would encourage
the required investment for HYV production; and (c) improvement of drainage
conditions in the polders. These factors would lead to increased agricultural
output through higher crop yields per ha, increased cropping intensity,
shifting cropping patterns from local to HYVs, and accelerated crop
diversification.
4.2       It is anticipated that yield per ha per cropping season would
increase by 10-15% for the same crop variety due to protection against
intrusion of saline water, improved drainage conditions in the kharif
season16, and protection against flooding caused by cyclones and storm
surges or heavy rainfall during the monsoon. It is also expected that in the
absence of the project, increased salinity due to existing or imminent
breaches in the embankments, poor drainage and flooding would, over time,
reduce yields to the level observed outside the embankments. All current HYV
production would switch to local varieties within two years and the average
yield of local varieties of rice would be reduced over a period of five years
to about one ton per ha, which is the average yield of T. Aman under saline
conditions.
4.3       Cropping intensity in the project area ranges from 108% (Polder
35/1) to 174% (Polder 72), the average being 136%. The national average is
about 171%. Saline soil conditions limit crop cultivation during the dry
season.   Cropping intensity in the "with project" situation is expected to
increase by about 20%. The area under kharif crops would increase due to
protection from floods as well as improved drainage and the area under rabi
crops17 would increase as a result of soil salinity reduction. With the
project, the cropping intensity is expected to range from about 130% (Polder
73/1A&B) to 210% (Polder 66/1), the average being 155%.
4.4        Adoption of HYVs of rice is limited in these areas due to high
soil and water salinity, as these varieties have little or no tolerance to
salinity. With protection against saline water and improvement of drainage
conditions, increased cultivation of HYVs of rice is expected to occur.
Depending upon the degree of sharecropping in each polder, between 20 and 40%
of T. Aman area is expected to shift to HYV in the kharif season. During the
rabi season, HYV boro cultivation is expected to increase only in Polder 66/1
where fresh underground water is available for irrigation. Cultivation of
HYVs of other rabi crops such as watermelon and betel leaf will also increase
in the "with project" situation, especially in the polders in Cox's Bazaar
district.
16.   Summer cropping season (May through November).
17.    Crops grown during the winter cropping season (November through May).



- 38 -
4.5       More land would come under diversified crops such as pulses,
oilseeds, cowpeas, chillies, winter vegetables, and sweet potatoes in the rabi
season due to reduced soil salinity. The changes in cropping pattern
resulting from the project are expected to occur in a period of seven years.
4.6       Projected annual incremental crop production is summarized in
Table 4.1. Total annual production, at full development, with the project is
expected to increase by about 87,700 m tons (or 56%) on average over the
"without project" situation. This consists of the sum of 70,400 m tons of
additional production over the present situation plus avoidance of an
anticipated 17,300 m tons decrease in production without the project, due to
increasing salinity. This production estimate is based on the net cultivable
land of about 49,900 ha in 11 of the 12 polders under the project16 and
excludes the cultivable land protected under the PWP. It is expected that the
target production would be achieved in seven years' time when the projected
changes in cropping pattern would be completed. The expected increase in
production would be higher if not for the constraints that the farmers will
still have to face. These constraints include limited fresh water for
irrigation during the dry season, unfavorable tenancy agreements19 that
discourage the tenant farmers from investing in land improvement and intensive
cultivation, existence of absentee/large landowners who do not pay much
attention to crop intensification, and poor quality of draft animals and
shortage of mechanical cultivators for land preparation. These constraints
have been taken into account when projecting increase in production.
B. Marketing and Prices
4.7       Farmers in the project area sell their surplus crop production
largely through the traditional marketing system in which prices are
determined by market forces. The system seems to work fairly efficiently.
Many farmers sell some portion of the output immediately after harvest to meet
their cash requirements, and then buy some of the same products later in the
season to meet their consumption needs. The increased crop production is not
expected to create any major marketing difficulty despite the record
performance in rice production at the national level in recent years. Rice
shortages exist in the areas covered by the project. The expected increase in
production is not likely to have a substantial impact on the national level.
Bangladesh still imports some of the foodgrains required for domestic
consumption, especially in years of bad weather. Actual foodgrain imports
declined from 2.9 million metric tons in FY88 to 0.8 million metric tons (food
18.   Polder 60, where project civil works include only 2 km of embankment
strengthening plus khal excavation, is excluded from the analysis.
More time is required to test the effectiveness of recently completed
anti-erosion works before firm estimates can be justified.
19.   Thirty to forty percent of rural households operate land under
different tenancy arrangements, the most widespread being sharecropping
under which the tenant agrees to bear all costs and pays 50* of gross
output to the landlord. The agreement is usually for one crop season.



- 39 -
aid plus commercial imports) in FY93. Imports increased in FY95 due to a 7%
reduction in foodgrain production resulting from drought and fertilizer
distribution problems. While continued growth of rice production could create
surpluses on a national level (in years of good weather), Bangladesh has the
option of either reducing wheat imports or taking measures to encourage crop
diversification. Farmers have the choice of selling their surplus foodgrains
under GOB's procurement program, but they are most likely to sell in the local
markets since local market prices tend to be higher than GOB procurement
prices. No downward pressure on market prices is expected.

4.8       GOB has taken actions in recent years to minimize distortions in
agricultural input prices and the distribution system in order to promote an
improved allocation of resources and maximize access to a wide range of inputs
to give farmers the flexibility to respond quickly to changing incentives. In
particular, the distribution of fertilizers and pesticides is now
predominantly handled by the private sector and restrictions on importation
and distribution of power tillers and minor irrigation equipment have been
removed. Private sector participation is also being allowed in seed
marketing. Consequently, farmers are not expected to face serious
difficulties with respect to availability of these inputs. However, limited
access to agricultural credit may constrain production. There has also been
backtracking on some of the fertilizer pricing and distribution reforms during
the 1994/95 season. Unusually high world market prices for urea led to higher
than normal exports. This happened at a time when domestic demand was higher,
resulting from higher price of rice. The additional exports created scarcity



- 40 -
in urea and the government's response was an array of distribution controls,
including a maximum retail price, quotas on lifting from the factories, and
intervention of district administrators. It is understood that the
backtracking on distribution liberalization was only a temporary reaction to
this situation.
C. Farm Incomes
4.9       Farm budget analysis shows that reduction of salinity intrusions
and storm surges and improvement of the drainage system would have a
significant impact on farm incomes. The effect of the project on annual farm
incomes is analyzed on the basis of a farm budget model for the "present" and
"with project" situation for each polder. The budget is presented on a one ha
farm since most households in the area operate farms of about that size. The
analysis is done separately for each polder because of differences such as
soil quality and cropping patterns between polders. Cost of production, gross
income, and net income per ha are estimated for each crop under the "present"
and "with project" assumptions using constant 1991 farmgate prices (see Annex
11, Table 1 for the computation of input and output prices). A summary of the
farm income is presented in Table 4.2 while detailed farm budgets for each
polder are presented in Annex 8.
4.10      The analysis shows that the investment in the project would have a
significant impact on the incomes of the farm families in the area (Table
4.2). The average net increase in income from one ha farms would vary from
about 20% (Polder 59/2) to 1151 (Polder 35/1). In value terms, the range
would be from about Tk 2,100 (Polder 73/lA&B) to Tk 11,300 (Polder 66/1). The
average increase in net income from a one-ha farm for all the polders would be
about Tk 5,200 or 47%. The increase in average farm income is expected to be
higher in the polders in Cox's Bazaar district, where intensive cropping is
practiced, than in the polders in Burguna or Bagerhat districts.
4.11      The additional farm incomes would also benefit tenants and landless
laborers, in addition to benefiting owner-operators. The percentage share of
the benefits is not expected to differ substantially from the present
arrangements. Everybody would gain in proportion to present income levels.
Thus, most of the people in the project area are expected to have strong
incentives to support the project.



- 41 -

(Taka/ha)

a/ Gross production value minus production cost.
D. Afforestation: Production. Incomes and Marketing
4.12      Forestry models and farm budgets are discussed in detail in
Annex 4.  Estimated annual production from the proposed afforestation
component is summarized in Annex 4, Table 7. The main outputs that would come
from afforestation are fruits, vegetables, timber, and fuelwood. About
227,600 m tons of fruits and vegetables and 224 million cubic feet of timber
and fuelwood are projected to be produced over the project life of 30 years.
While about 90% of the output of fruits and vegetables is projected to come
from the embankment slope and the rest from the homesteads, about 95% of the
timber output is projected to come from the foreshore.
4.13      Fruits and timber would be the two major products expected from the
afforestation on embankment slopes and foreshore. The incremental output as a
result of the project is not likely to create marketing problems. Fruits are
in short supply in the coastal belt as well as in the rest of the country for
most of the year. Timber and fuelwood are also in short supply. The timber
is used in constructing houses and building boats. There is a large fishing
community in the project area. Given access to other coastal areas by water
and, to some extent, by road, any surplus output in a given area would be
transported to deficit areas without much difficulty.
4.14      Estimated net incomes from afforestation on the embankment slope,
foreshore, and homestead land are summarized in Annex 4, Tables 8, 9, and 10,
respectively. Projected net financial benefits from the afforestation over a
30-year period is Tk 5,000 million of which about 20%, 75*, and 2% would come
from the embankment slope, foreshore, and homestead plantation, respectively.
Net positive incomes from the afforestation, if all inputs including family
labor were valued at market prices, would begin to be realized from the first,



- 42 -
fourth, and ninth year of afforestation on the homestead land, embankment
slope, and foreshore, respectively. However, given that the Government would
provide the seedlings and the first three years' requirements of fertilizer
and pesticides, and that embankment settlers would be using their own family
labor, settlers on the embankment would be receiving a positive income from
the embankment and homestead from year one. The payments the families receive
for routine embankment maintenance would be set to provide sufficient
supplementary income to produce an annual income of Tk 9,000 per family2O.
Such payments would decrease progressively as production from the embankment
and homestead increase, with settlers expected to achieve an annual income of
about Tk 9,000 per family without further Government payment in the fifth
year. (For safety sake, the project cost estimates include an allowance for
the possibility of delays or other problems resulting in some families
requiring a continued payment in Year five). Similarly for the foreshore
plantation, the costs of seedlings, fertilizer, and pesticides for three years
is a Government cost covered under the project. For the foreshore, the
Government would also cover the cost of labor for planting. Thus the only
costs prior to production through year eight are small costs for guarding
against poachers. (These costs are assumed to be met by family labor in the
case of community management on Government land.)
V. BENEFITS. JUSTIFICATION AND RISKS
A. Benefits and Beneficiaries
5.1       The main project benefits would be fewer human lives lost and less
property destroyed during cyclones, and prevention of salinity intrusions and
storm surges during normal monsoons, leading to increased agricultural
production and employment. Complementary works under the project would
improve drainage, further increasing agricultural production. Social forestry
for cyclone protection and embankment maintenance would have ancillary
benefits in poverty alleviation, environmental improvement, and increased
output of wood and other forestry products.
5.2       The operation would directly benefit about 1.2 million people
living in the project area. This includes about 600,000 people living where
the sea-facing embankments would be rehabilitated and another 600,000 people
benefitting from improved embankment O&M, afforestation, and minor embankment
improvements for polders for which the rehabilitation of the sea-facing
embankments was financed under PWP.
20.   There would be an exception for two polders as discussed in the
footnote to para 2.12.



- 43 -
Protection of Human Lives
5.3       Benefits in human lives saved are impossible to quantify and are
not included in the economic analysis. However, it is estimated that about
140,000 people were killed in the project area during the 1991 cyclone. The
embankments would help save lives during future cyclones. Although
overtopping could still occur after the construction of the new embankments,
the flooding of the polders would be slowed and reduced. The massive sudden
flooding of 1991, which happened due to breaches in the embankments or missing
sections, would not happen in a "with project" situation. Delayed flooding
would give more time for the people to escape to safer areas or cyclone
shelters.
Production Benefits
5.4       Most of the quantifiable benefits would come from increased crop
production in normal years. The embankments would protect a gross area of
about 191,000 ha (excluding the area protected by sea-facing embankments
financed under PWP). The project would directly benefit about 66,500 ha of
net cultivable land21 through protection from saline inundation and through
improved drainage. This would increase annual crop production at full
development from about 157,400 m tons in the "without project" scenario to
245,100 m tons in the "with project" scenario (an increase of about 40W and
56W over the present and "without project" situation, respectively).
5.5       Beyond the benefits afforded during monsoons and normal cyclones,
the embankments would provide additional benefits from protecting crops and
livestock during major cyclones. It is conservatively estimated that about
50* and 40% of the crops would be saved in the case of cyclonic surges with a
return period of 10 and 20 years, respectively. Over 50,000 head of cattle
were lost during the 1991 cyclone; many would have been saved had the
embankments not breached. Improved embankments and sluices would also
preserve shrimp farms, culture fisheries and salt production.
Protection of Property and Infrastructure
5.6       The project would protect houses, schools and administrative and
commercial buildings. About 864,000 houses were destroyed and another 774,000
were damaged during the 1991 cyclone. Protection would also be provided to
public infrastructure such as roads, bridges and culverts. Regulators and
other infrastructure in shrimp farms would also be saved, especially from
cyclones with a return period of 10 and 20 years.
Afforestation Benefits
5.7       Afforestation of embankment slopes and a belt on the foreshore
would reduce cyclone damage and embankment O&M costs (Annex 4, para 1).
Afforestation would provide fruits, timber, fuelwood, and fodder to workers in
embankment maintenance, to community groups in foreshore maintenance, and to
21.   Excluding Polder 60 and the PWP polders.



- 44 -
landowners on the foreshore whose land would be planted with trees (para
4.12). The use of mangrove areas as a fish and shrimp nursery is another
unquantified but substantial benefit.
Emplovment Benefits
5.8       The project would generate additional employment during
construction and through increased agricultural production made possible by
the embankments. It is estimated that demand for agricultural labor would
increase from about 190 days/ha per year to about 280 days/ha at full
development, for a total increase in agricultural employment of about 26,700
years. The additional demand for labor would come from greater area under
crops, adoption of HYVs, higher cropping intensity and extra labor needs for
harvesting. Additional labor would also be engaged in the routine maintenance
of the embankments and in the afforestation belt on the foreshore.
Other Benefits
5.9       The project would give other, unquantified, benefits in:
(i) savings on emergency relief. While the embankments would afford little
protection from high winds, they would provide protection against cyclonic
surge and would reduce post-cyclone relief costs for those affected by the
surge22; and (ii) environmental benefits: afforestation would also provide
environmental benefits as reducing salinity and lessened soil erosion
Distribution of Benefits and Poverty Alleviation
5.10      The project's benefits would be widely distributed and have a
positive impact on poverty. Estimated annual per capita income from a one-ha
farm in the project area is now about US$50 per annum. The project is
projected to raise this to about US$70 per annum at full development, an
increase of about 40%. The distribution of direct agricultural benefits would
be determined largely by landownership and tenurial arrangements. Women and
landless laborers would gain employment in land preparation, planting, weeding
and harvesting resulting from increased agricultural activities in the area.
Those groups would also get income from maintenance of the embankments (wage
income during the early years and the products of the trees on the embankments
and the afforestation belt during the later years) and wage income from
periodic maintenance jobs. While the project is not specifically poverty
focused, the loss of life and property during cyclones affects
disproportionately the poor, with weakly constructed houses and least ability
to recuperate.
22.   Such benefits are difficult to quantify and are not included in the
economic analysis of the project due to scarcity of data.



- 45 -
B. Economic Analysis
5.11            Economic analysis was done for each of 11 polders23 having
major works financed under this project. The overall economic rate of return
(ERR) for the project, excluding the value for human lives saved, is estimated
at 19.2%. The net present value (NPV) of net incremental benefits is
estimated at US$27.0 million, at an opportunity cost of capital of 12%.
Individual polder ERRs range from 6.6* (Polder 70, Matherbari) to 28.1%
(Polder 35/1, Sharankhola and Polder 59/3B, Sudharam) and all but three
polders have ERRs greater than 121.
5.12      Kuakata (Polder 48), Ramgati (Polder 59/2), and Matherbari (Polder
70) have ERRs less than 12% (7.9%, 6.9% and 6.6%, respectively). Even though
Polders 48, 59/2, and 70 cannot be justified on the ERR criterion, they are
fully justified taking account of the additional benefit of the savings in
human lives. For example, during the 1991 cyclone about 10,000 people were
killed in Polder 70 in a population of 23,000.
5.13            No calculations have been done on the PWP polders for which
this project is providing only ancillary investments in improved O&M,
afforestation, and minor embankment improvements. Given that the major cost
of the polder improvements are for the sea-facing embankments, the
complementary investments to improve embankment life and remove minor
embankment deficiencies are inherently attractive.
C. Risks
5.14      The project confronts several important risks.
(a)  Insufficient community participation. A major risk is the possible
failure to engender sufficient community participation for
afforestation and routine embankment maintenance. Community
participation merges pieces of other programs, but is untried in
its proposed form. The cost of routine embankment maintenance
would increase substantially if the afforestation component failed
and future rehabilitation costs would also rise. The proposed
component has, nevertheless, a better probability of success than
traditional means of embankment maintenance precisely because it
involves beneficiary participation.
(b) Poor inter-institutional coordination. The community participation
component requires coordination between BWDB and FD. Poor
coordination could delay implementation and cause benefits to be
23.    Annex 11 presents the methodology and assumptions of the analysis.  A
twelfth polder (Polder 60: Sonagazi) was excluded from the economic
analysis because the project finances only minor works there. About $2
million of remaining work to complete PWP embankments in Anowara
(Polder 63/1A) has also been excluded from the analysis (See para 1.4).



- 46 -
foregone during the waiting period. The agreement between BWDB and
FD (para 2.11) is intended to reduce such delays. Two mid-term
reviews would help to adjust the operation if necessary, in light
of project experience.
(c) Inadecauate Government funding. The investment and O&M costs of
this project can be met within available budgetary resources,
without distorting priorities. Nevertheless, inadequate Government
funding remains a risk, despite recent improvements. Inadequate
funding, like poor inter-institutional coordination, could delay
implementation and postpone benefits. However, the devastation
caused by the April 1991 cyclone is expected to lead to political
pressure to overcome problems which would delay the proposed
project. Inadequate financing of O&M would reduce the life of the
investments requiring premature rehabilitation. The project, by
replacing existing deteriorated embankments, does not add to the
costs of adequate embankment maintenance. In fact the planting of
trees on the embankment and the use of settlers on the toe of the
embankment for routine maintenance (in return for the produce of
the trees) should reduce maintenance costs. Indicative budget
requirements for O&M were agreed at negotiations. Finally, the
project has been designed to elicit greater community participation
and this is expected to bring additional pressure on the Government
to provide adequate funding.
(d)  ManaQement and institutional weaknesses. IDA experience in
Bangladesh shows that there is a risk of implementation delays, and
resulting cost increases, due to management/institutional
weaknesses. The risk of implementation delays and poor quality
construction is being addressed through the use of internationally
recruited consultants for supervision of construction, using
contractors for the sea-facing embankments selected through ICB,
after prequalification, and initiating tendering for the major
works and land acquisition for the first year's construction
program prior to Board presentation.
(e)  Overestimation of yields or farmer response.  The estimated yields
and rate of conversion to HYV are based on experience from other
areas in Bangladesh, adjusted to take account of limited
availability of dry season water supply and other constraints (para
4.6). While the resulting estimates are believed to be realistic,
all such estimates are subject to a margin of error.
D. Sensitivity Analysis
5.15            The economic analysis was supplemented with a sensitivity
analysis based on the identified risks. The sensitivity of the ERRs was
measured in each polder with respect to adverse changes in key variables (para
5.14 and Annex 11, para. 15). Those variables are: (i) a failure of community
participation in embankment maintenance, causing O&M cost to increase above
projected levels; (ii) implementation delays (due to institutional weaknesses,



- 47 -
inadequate counterpart funds, and land acquisition problems) leading to
escalation in investment costs and deferral of benefits; and (iii) less than
expected increases in agricultural production. Adverse changes in the
sensitivity variables needed to reduce the project ERR to 12%, are larger than
considered likely.
5.16      Effect of insufficient community participation. The potential
adverse effects of insufficient community participation would be to lower
benefits and to raise both OM and investment costs. If the embankments are
not maintained properly and there is intrusion of saline water, then
agriculture benefits would fall, the life of the embankments would be shorter
and benefits from infrastructure protection would be less. If the life of the
embankments is reduced due to inadequate maintenance, major additional
expenditure on embankment rehabilitation would be incurred in year 10 and
again in year 27 after project completion. Major expenditure on embankment
rehabilitation in year 10 and 27 after project completion (a proxy for the
cost effects of inadequate maintenance) would reduce the average ERR for the
11 polders from 19.2% to 17.1%.
5.17      Effect of implementation delays or overestimation of production
increases: Implementation delays would defer benefits. Overestimation of
yields or of the rate of farmer conversion to HYV would also reduce benefits.
A reduction in the major benefits has been included in the sensitivity
analysis. Switching values have been calculated at a discount rate of 12% for
individual benefit items in the NPV/ERR calculations to show the potential
effect of the risks for each of the 8 polders having estimated ERR on
quantifiable benefits above 12%. The variables that have been tested in the
risk and sensitivity analysis include delays in completion of construction and
decrease in major quantifiable project benefits (agricultural, afforestation,
and non-agricultural benefits).
5.18      Table 5.1 shows that to bring the ERR on quantifiable benefits
below 12% would require a combination of a two year delay in project
completion and a decrease in project benefits exceeding 30%, or a three year
delay in project completion and a decrease in project benefits of about 20%.
Other combinations which would reduce the ERR to 12% would include a two year
delay in project completion and an increase in O&M costs of over 30%, or a
three year delay and an increase in O&M costs of about 15%. Such combinations
of unfavorable developments are considered unlikely. Even then, the
additional non-quantifyable benefits of saving human lives would still make
the project worth doing.



- 48 -

VI. AGREEMENTS REACEED AND RECOMMENDATION
6.1       During negotiations, agreements were reached with GOB on
the following points:
(a)  maintenance of an agreement, satisfactory to IDA, between
BWDB and FD, governing implementation of the Project until
completion of the Project (para 2.11);
(b)  model agreement with embankment settlers, foreshore
afforestation groups, and private owners of foreshore land
(paras 2.12, 2.13);
(c)  provision of adequate funds for O&M of project facilities
(para 2.17);
(d)  resettlement and rehabilitation of persons adversely
affected by the project would be carried out in accordance
with a Resettlement Action Plan, which is considered
acceptable to IDA (para 2.19);
(e)  appointment of consultants, with terms of reference
acceptable to IDA, prior to signature of civil work
contracts (para 2.21);
(f)  contract framework and schedule for BWDB and Forest
Department entering into contracts, satisfactory to IDA,
with participating NGOs (paras 2.21, 2.34);
(g)  all vehicles and equipment, other than field staff
replacement motorcycles or bicycles to be procured by
December 31, 2000 (para 2.23);
(h)  only civil works expenditures incurred through December
31, 2000 would be financed (para 2.26);



- 49 -
(i) participiation of contractors under NCB tenders for minor
works to be limited to prequalified class A contractors
(or the equivalent), and minimum contract sizes for
earthworks (para 2.32);
(j) withdrawals for civil works to be supported by
certification by implementation support consultants (para
2.36);
(k)  arrangements with respect to project accounting and
auditing (para 2.39);
(1) maintenance of the PIU and FPIU (paras 3.1, 3.4) until
project completion;
(m)  establishment and maintenance of the project coordinating
committees (para 3.8); and
(n) arrangements and schedules for mid-term reviews (para
3.9).
6.2       Conditions of effectiveness would be as follows:
(a) effectiveness of the cofinancing agreement between GOB and
EC (para 2.30); and
(b) approval of the Project Proforma (para 2.30).
6.3       With the above conditions and agreements, the project
would be suitable for an IDA Credit to the Peoples Republic of
Bangladesh of SDR 34.1 (US$53.0 million equivalent) on standard IDA
terms with a maturity of 40 years.
