-62-                              ANNEX 3
Page 1 of 11
BANGLADESH
COASTAL EMBANKMENT REHABILITATION PROJECT
Community Participation and Role of NGOs
General
1.        Objectives.  A community participation approach to afforestation
and embankment maintenance would be adopted in order to maximize the benefits
of the project for local disadvantaged groups and in order to provide long
term low cost O&M of coastal flood control and drainage systems.
2.        The approach requires the involvement of NGOs who would assist in
training and supervising landless people in O&M, construction and
afforestation activities. Specifically, the NGOs would (a) facilitate the
resettlement of families displaced by project works to the toe of the
embankment where they would be responsible for routine maintenance and
afforestation of their allotted sections of embankment, (b) involve landless,
and particularly female headed households, in routine embankment maintenance
for the entire length of the selected coastal embankments, irrespective of
whether rehabilitation works have been carried out, (c) carry out protective
afforestation of the foreshore, and (d) mobilize local people to take
advantage of other employment opportunities arising from the project
(construction works, periodic maintenance, forestry nurseries, agricultural
and non-farm activities). In addition NGOs would be involved in monitoring
the land acquisition process and in ensuring the Project Affected Persons
receive fair and timely compensation. This aspect of NGO activities is
described in Annex 9 - Resettlement.
3.        NGO Capabilities and Experience.  There are over 12,000 NGOs
operating in Bangladesh of which some 150 have been identified by the IDA as
having development programs for the poor. Their size, capacity and areas of
expertise diverge greatly. Many NGOs are members of the umbrella
organization, the Association of Development Agencies in Bangladesh (ADAB),
which is mandated to improve NGO coordination. ADAB also provides
secretariats for fora or sub-groups of NGOs working in specific fields. One
such group is the Forum on Forestry and Environment (the forum), which has
some 95 members working in the area of community or social forestry.
4.        The experience of community forestry has been mainly limited to
arrangements on government-owned lands with bodies such as the railways and
roads authorities and the BWDB for inland embankments. These lands are leased
for short periods to the NGOs, who organize and pay groups to plant trees and
receive a share of the produce upon harvesting. A caretaker from the group is
paid for by the NGO. The trees are often slow yielding timber species and the
land remains the property of the government agency, which generally also
receives part of the harvested produce. An exception to this model occurs in
the coastal areas where small groups are involved in planting of palms on land
around cyclone shelter sites, often donated by the community. There has been
no experience to date of the private lease-type arrangements proposed for this
project.



-63-                             ANNEX 3
Page 2 of 11
5.        The NGOs with the most experience in the forestry sector include
Proshika (which has worked with winrock International in developing community
forestry training modules), Dinajpur and Rangpur Development Scheme (who have
a well established and successful social forestry programme in north-western
Bangladesh), and BRAC (who have experience in the planting of mulberry trees
for sericulture on road and river embankments). In addition, POUSH, CARITAS,
MCC (Mennonite Central Committee), CARE and CONCERN have been involved in
social forestry along roads and railways lines.
6.        At least 40 NGOs are working in the 21 polders covered by this
project. They are largely involved in emergency relief, building and
maintenance of cyclone protection shelters and land reform. There is a
growing experience through programs such as the SRP, and through NGOs' own
Land Reform Programs, in working with landless women to form Earthworks
Maintenance Groups (EMGs) to carry out routine embankment maintenance for
BWDB, and in forming Landless Contracting Societies (LCSs) for road building
and maintenance. Many NGOs are also involved in social forestry programs,
although these do not appear to involve afforestation of coastal embankments.
One NGO (Oiskar International) is believed to be active in community planting
of mangroves.
7.        Interest of NGOs.  Based on discussions between the mission, ADAB
and individual NGOs at the national and the local level, there appears to be
general support for the approach of the project. In particular many NGOs
welcomed the opportunity to transform a major infrastructure project into an
opportunity to use a scarce land resource for resettlement of vulnerable
landless groups and to treat embankments as income earning assets. All of the
NGOs consulted expressed an interest in participating in activities ranging
from project design to implementation and monitoring. NGOs wish to be
considered as partners, rather than simply contractors, in the project,
actively involved from the outset of project implementation in discussions on
the preparation of proforma contracts and consideration of proposals for
implementation of the project. NGOs stressed that they would prefer to work
in areas where they are already established, which highlights the need to
identify locally active NGOs to be included on the shortlist of NGOs to be
invited to provide services.
Activities for Community Participation
8.        Areas in which community participation would occur are:
(a) Routine embankment maintenance;
(b)  Foreshore afforestation;
(c) Embankment afforestation; and
(d) Periodic embankment maintenance.
9.        Routine Earthwork Maintenance would be carried out on the total
length of embankments rehabilitated and improved under CERP and PWP and
limited sections of non-rehabilitated embankments unimpeded by human
settlements and plantations (see para 13 (b)). Routine O&M and planting and
management of trees on the rehabilitated embankments would be carried out on
about 530 km of embankment by squatter and other selected landless households,
with priority given to those affected by the project. The selected landless
families would be resettled at the toe of the embankment and would be



-64 -                            ANNEX 3
Page 3 of 11
responsible for the maintenance of a specific strip corresponding to the
length of embankment on which they would have the usufructuary rights for the
trees. This is expected to be about one hectare (approximately 250m-300m
depending on the height and width specifications of the embankment). In
portions of Sandwip and Ramgati, due to the high density of squatters on
embankments, they would be organized in groups of about 5-10 families, with
each group being allotted a 1 ha section of embankment. Families would have a
contract with BWDB and receive direct payment for the first 4 years of
operation (from the first year of settlement) at a declining rate as the
produce of the trees on the embankment increases.1 Failure to meet their O&M
and afforestation responsibilities would lead to sanctions, including in the
worst cases, eviction and resettlement of new families on the homestead plot.
After a period of 4 years the families would continue to carry out routine O&M
but would no longer receive payment (except that if the embankment trees are
destroyed in a future cyclone, payment would resume until sufficient
subsistence income could be obtained from the trees.) It has been calculated
that about 1,800 families, plus about 30 groups of 5-10 families each in
Sandwip and Ramgati, would be resettled in this way.
10.       Foreshore Afforestation.  Afforestation would be carried out on
some 4,700 ha of the foreshore by groups of local people trained and
supervised by NGOs. Mangroves would be planted on low lying foreshore areas,
while other species (see Annex 4) have been identified for higher foreshore
areas. Foreshore afforestation would be phased and would be carried out from
year 1 of the project, before the completion of construction works. Planting
would be carried out in the months of June to August, with mangroves also
being planted in December to February. It is expected that groups would have
an average of about 15 members who would plant some 12 ha (equivalent to an
0.8 km strip of 100 m or a 1.6 km strip of 50 m)2 However different NGOs
would be permitted to use larger or smaller group sizes based on their
experience and preferences. Replacement of failed seedling would be carried
out in the subsequent 2 years. This implies that NGOs and FD would need to
mobilize some 400 groups for foreshore afforestation over the whole project
area. Average earnings of group members, on the basis of a Tk 60 day rate,
are estimated at Tk 3,600 per season, equivalent to earnings from agricultural
labor. For the publicly owned areas to be afforested (expected to be most of
the foreshore afforestation program) protection of foreshore forests would be
the responsibility of the community. Distribution of benefits from
harvesting, carried out from year 8 onwards, under the supervision of the FD,
would be the responsibility of the community.
11.       Embankment Afforestation would be carried out by resettled
households. A combination of timber, fuelwood, fruit and other species, such
as grasses, would be provided gratis to families from nurseries established in
collaboration with the FD. Such families would also be supplied with
fertilizer and pesticides during the first three years of afforestation.
Trained field officers of the NGOs would assist beneficiaries in preparing a
1.    Details of payment rates are in Annex 5, para 17.
2.    Based on the assumption of 3,000 seedlings per hectare, with an average
labor input of 75 person-days per hectare, for a five day week over a
three month season.



- 65 -                            ANNX 3
Page 4 of 11
planting strategy which is appropriate to embankment protection, provide a
steady subsistence income from year 5 onwards and meet fuelwood, fodder and
survival needs. Training in planting, maintenance, harvesting and marketing
would be provided to groups of households. Protection of tree crops would be
individual household's responsibility with the support of the local project
coordination committee.
12.       Periodic Embankment Maintenance. Re-excavation of Khals and the
Periodic Maintenance of Khals. Periodic embankment maintenance would consist
of earthwork in resectioning to bring the embankment to design profile,
repairs of earth slips, grassing of slopes, etc. The re-excavation of khals,
which is included as a rehabilitation and improvement work, would consist of
removal of water hyacinth, clearing debris and man made obstructions, and
earthwork excavation in bringing the khal to its designed profile and bed
level. The periodic maintenance of khals, which is estimated to be carried
out once every two years, would consist of removal of water hyacinth, clearing
of debris and man-made obstructions, and desilting to the designed bed level
and bed width. The periodic maintenance activities on embankments would be
carried out on about 770 km. About 390 km of khals are to be re-excavated
under the rehabilitation and improvement program, and the work is expected to
be carried out under the Food For Work Program. These khals are also included
under the routine and periodic maintenance program. Whilst the routine
maintenance of embankments being rehabilitated and improved under the project
are provided for, the project does not expect routine maintenance of
embankments to be carried out in most of the 230 km of embankments where no
rehabilitation and improvement works are proposed under the project.
Conditions prevailing in these embankments (extensive human settlements and
plantations) do not permit an organized and effective method to carry out
routine maintenance. However, the periodic maintenance program would ensure
the safety and security of the embankment. In addition, where conditions
permit, funds from the periodic maintenance program could be utilized to carry
out routine maintenance. During the project period, BWDB and project
consultants would identify sections of khals requiring routine maintenance,
and also the sections of embankment where no rehabilitation and improvement
works are included in CERP and PWP where routine maintenance is possible.
These sections would be contracted out to Earthwork Maintenance Groups as
detailed in para 13(b). Wherever possible periodic maintenance work would be
awarded to Landless Contracting Societies (LCSs) for a guaranteed minimum wage
rate, comparable with Food For Work rates for a fixed amount of work.
Alternatively, such work could be awarded through NCB procedures.
13.       Minimum contract size for major construction works would preclude
Landless Contracting Societies from being awarded contracts directly by BWDB,
but it is anticipated that international and local contractors would budget
some 10% of overall contracts for local manual labor, implying an average
local labor demand of at least 100 labor-days per km over a 3 month
construction period. It is anticipated that a total of about 60 LCS would be
formed for periodic maintenance work.
Models for Community Participation. Three basic models for community
participation are proposed.
(a) Resettled Households would have individual contracts which allow
settlement on a 10 m by 20 m homestead plot on the toe of the



-66 -                             ANNEX 3
Page 5 of 11
embankment and the usufructuary rights of the trees that they plant
on the embankment. Resettlers would receive a cash payment for
relocation expenses and payment in kind of materials for building
of a katcha house on the homestead plot. The length of the
contract for settlement at the homestead should be based on the
lifetime of the embankment which, with the exception of river
sections prone to erosion which may require future realignment, is
expected to be at least 30 years. In the event of retirement, the
lease holders would be resettled on the toe of the new embankment
and would receive a similar package of assistance to the original.
The contract would be between the household and the BWDB and it
must be clearly understood that rights of abode would be tied to
the performance of O&M services. In order to reduce risk of
seasonal migration of males and non-performance of O&M duties and
evictions, it may be appropriate to assign the contract to the
female head of the family.
(b)  Earthwork Maintenance Groups (EMGs) for embankments and khals would
have direct annual contracts with the BWDB for: (i) sections of
embankments (limited to sections unimpeded by human settlement and
plantations and identified by BWDB and project consultants) where
no rehabilitation and improvement works have been carried out; and
(b) sections of khals requiring regular clearing operations to
facilitate rapid drainage. Preference for membership to these
groups would be given to landless households, and particularly
female headed households, living close to the embankment. EMGs
would typically have about 25 members with an elected chairperson
and secretary. Maintenance responsibilities would be throughout
the year based on a renewable annual contract (estimated value of
Tk 15,600 per km/pa) paid directly to the group's bank account.
Two signatories would be required for withdrawals. BWDB would
continue to pay for such routine maintenance services after IDA has
withdrawn from the project. Currently EMGs established for the SRP
and Early Implementation Project do not have legal entity but are
organized and certified by NGOs.
Some of the existing EMGs have a compulsory group savings scheme
whereby 10W (Tk 3 per day) of the wages is saved for group or
individual projects. NGOs should discuss this approach with
members in order to generate savings for other income generating
projects such as livestock, horticulture, cottage industries,
nurseries and afforestation contracts on unsettled sections of the
embankment.
(c)  Landless Contracting Societies (LCSs) would be established by the
NGOs in order to carry out foreshore afforestation, some earthworks
during resectioning and appropriate periodic maintenance work.
During the construction period preference would be given to
households resettled on the toe of the embankment, who would not
yet be able to plant on the embankment or their homesteads.
Landless Contracting Societies would be registered by NGOs who
would assist in the necessary paperwork for the contracting process
with the BWDB. Since the groups do not have their own legal
entity, it is suggested that, if they wish, NGOs assist both EMGs



-67-                             ANNEX 3
Page 6 of 11
and LCSs to register formally under the Society's Act in order to
ensure sustainability after the expiry of the contract between BWDB
and the NGOs.
Collaborative Framework
14.       Scope of NGO activities.  Terms of Reference for the NGOs would be
prepared by BWDB and FD in agreement with IDA and EU missions and in close
collaboration with the ADAB forum on forestry and environment. The main
activities are expected to include the following:
(a)      Planning of embankment afforestation and maintenance and foreshore
afforestation. NGO staff would visit project polders to explain
the project concept, mobilize support for the project, assess the
interest and attitudes of local people towards participating in the
programme, and identify suitable participants. Together with
forestry experts and local people, an appropriate mix of trees
species would be identified, taking account of the requirements of
BWDB for trees suitable for planting on an embankment. The NGOs
would assist FD in delineating areas of foreshore suitable for
planting. Based on this preliminary work, NGOs would formulate a
plp-n for discus,.on and approval in the PIU and FPIU.  Following
approval, NGOs would mobilize project participants and initiate
forestry activities.
(b)      I=mlementation of embankment forestrv.  Assist embankment settlers
(ES) to occupy homestead plots allocated in the Resettlement Action
Plan, and in negotiating contracts with BWDB. In conjunction with
the FD, provide appropriate advice on planting schemes and methods.
Supply each settler with tree seedlings, hedge seeds, fertilizers,
stakes and pesticides. Together with local FD staff, provide
advice and any training required on planting methods, and ensure
trees are correctly planted. After planting, monitor survival and
growth of trees, and provide any advice required. Continue to
provide fertilizer and pesticides, and replace any seedlings that
die, for a three year period.
(c)      SuPDlV of tree seedlings.  At the start of the project, in
conjunction with the FD, identify existing supplies of tree
seedlings and calculate what additional supplies, if any, would be
needed for both the embankment and the foreshore. In conjunction
with the FD, formulate a plan to expand seedling production, with
FD taking the lead in mangrove seedlings, and the NGO organizing
production of other species, preferably by supporting nurseries run
by landless and poor people.
(d)      ImDlementation of foreshore forestry.  Organize people (with
preference for the landless/poor) into Foreshore Forestry Groups
(FFG). If group members wish, assist them in registering as a LCS.
Supply seedlings and other input materials and, together with the
FD, provide technical advice and training. Monitor the survival
rate of seedling and organize replacement planting as well as
weeding and other maintenance activities. Pay foreshore groups
(from funds provided by FD) at agreed rates for planting.



-68 -                             ANNEX 3
Page 7 of 11
(e)      Forest protection.  Motivate and organize communities to protect
both newly planted and existing areas of foreshore forest. Assist
groups in negotiating a share of firewood and poles produced in
these forests.
(f)      Routine maintenance of rehabilitated embankments.  Provide advice
to ES on the routine maintenance of embankments and assist them in
maintaining exclusive occupation of their leased sections. Certify
to BWDB the quality of the work carried out as the basis for
payments to ES from BWDB. Monitor these payments and level of
income from tree crops and homestead plots. Recommend appropriate
sanctions for inadequate performance of responsibilities, including
eviction as a last resort.
(g)      Routine maintenance of sections of existincq embankments and khals.
Organize groups of landless people in EMGs to contract with BWDB to
provide routine maintenance of khals and identified sections of
embankment that are not rehabilitated or afforested by the project.
Give training to group leaders in earthwork methods, group
organization, basic accounting and tendering procedures. Assist in
negotiations with BWDB, and monitor payments to the group from
BWDB. Assist in setting up a group bank account. Provide, on a
credit basis, any hand tools the group may require.
(h)      Periodic embankment and khal maintenance.  Organize groups of
landless people as LCS to carry out contracts given by BWDB for
periodic maintenance work on embankments. Give training to group
leaders in earthwork methods, group organization, basic accounting
and tendering procedures. Assist in negotiations with BWDB, and
monitor payments to the group from BWDB. Assist in setting up a
group bank account. Provide, on a credit basis, any hand tools the
group may require.
(i)      Homestead Dlots.  Provide assistance to ES in making productive use
of homestead plots, and in developing other sources of income that
are compatible with the good maintenance of the embankment and
trees.
(j)      Submit proposals to the PIU for use of a Community Development Fund
provided by the project and implement approved activities. The
Community Development Fund would provide resources to implement
supporting poverty alleviation activities aimed at strengthening
project operation and group cohesion. This may involve water
supplies and sanitary facilities for embankment settlers, training
and education programmes, and income generating activities. The
latter could in particular aim at processing and marketing of
project outputs such as coconuts. The fund may also be used to
provide credit for the purchase of hand tools by EMG/LCS.
(j)      Participate in Prolect Coordinating Committees at the polder level
and elect representatives to participate at district and national
levels.

*(k)     Community suDDort.  Hold discussions with the Union Parishad and

involved it is likely that NGOs for the pilot activities would be employed as

selection. Coordination and Monitoring of NGOs
17.       Selection of NGOs.  Given the diversity of activities in which NGOs
would be involved (mobilization, organization of groups and individuals for
O&M, afforestation and tree maintenance) and the diversity of coastal areas
and conditions, it is anticipated a separate contract would be required for
each polder. It would be highly advantageous for BWDB and FD to select the
same NGO in a particular polder. Given the large number of NGOs involved,
standardization of contracts and coordination would be required to guarantee
quality and continuity of services provided.
18.       Surveys for the resettlement plan have furnished a list of active
NGOs by polders. From this, and other information that ADAB may provide, and
consultation with the polder level coordinating committees headed by the Union
Parishad Chairmen, a short list would be compiled and NGOs would be invited to
submit proposals for providing services for specific polders. The proposed
selections would be made by a joint BWDB/FD Committee subject to approval by
the PIU and the FPIU, and whatever higher level approvals may be required.
Selection would be made on the basis of the following criteria:
(a)      several years of experience of working in the coastal area,
especially in the specific project polders;
(b)      proven management and implementation ability, particularly for
field staff in remote areas;
(c)      experience in mobilization, group formation and organization of the
landless;



- 70 -                            ANNEX 3
Page 9 of 11
(d)      experience in implementation of social forestry projects;
(e)      experience in organization of EWG or LCS for public works
activities; and
(f)      pragmatic approach shown through a willingness to work with local
government representatives (Union Parishad), local elites, and
government implementing agencies;
19.       Ideally the polder level coordinating committees headed by the
Union Parishad Chairmen would review the NGO proposals and send their
recommendations to the PIU and FPIU, who would review them to ensure that they
were appropriately based on the selection criteria and that the total
selections of a particular NGO by the various Union's did not exceed that
NGO's implementation capacity. In making the selection, a cross section of
local representatives including members of the target group, Union Parishad
members, and staff of other development agencies should also be consulted to
verify the record and suitability of the NGO for the specific area. The
proposed selections would be subject to IDA/EU review and a statement of no
objection.
Staff Requirements
20.       NGOs would utilize full time field workers and supervisors to work
exclusively on the project. For calculation purposes it is assumed that there
would be one field worker per 8 EMGs/LCS, one worker per 10 foreshore forestry
groups, and one field worker per 30 ES during the period of embankment
afforestation, reducing to one per 50 ES once trees are established.
Individual NGOs may choose to vary group size and number of groups covered by
a field worker based, on their experience and preferences. ES may be
organized into groups of 10 to 15 for mutual support, and to facilitate
supervision and training. There would be an average of about one supervisor
per five field workers. Attachment 1 shows the anticipated NGO staff
requirements relative to project embankment maintenance and forestry
activities, on these assumptions. NGO staff numbers for all polders would
total about 140. In addition each NGO would appoint a Project Director from
amongst its senior staff at a regional or national headquarters level. The
NGO's Project Director would be responsible, on a part time basis, for the
overall direction of the NGO's activities, and for contractual matters.
21.       Management and Coordination.  Within each polder the NGO's senior
field supervisor would be responsible for NGO activities, and would coordinate
with local BWDB and FD staff. The NGO supervisors would report to the BWDB
zonal XEN (O&M) and to the CERP ACF in the FD who would be responsible for O&M
and forestry activities for a group of polders. NGOs would draw up, for
approval by the PIU/FPIU, a detailed work plan for a three year period,
together with cost budgets. The plan would be updated annually. The project
consultants would recruit four Community Development Officers, who would be
based at a zonal level, together with a Community Development Advisor based in
the PIU. They would assist NGOs in their relations with BWDB and FD, arrange
for NGO staff training, and provide overall communication and coordination
between project NGOs.

22.       RePresentation.  NGOs would be represented on committees with the
implementing agencies and consultants at a number of levels.  A senior member

system for each polder would feed into a computerized MIS. In addition a

surveys of beneficiaries. If an NGO fails to meet the terms of its contract
then the contract would be terminated and another organization would be
sought.
Timing and CoveraQe of Pronosed Actions
24.       Solicitation of NGO participation should be initiated in June 1995,
on the basis of an agreed terms of reference prepared by BWDB and FD, with
advice and assistance from the World Bank resident mission, EU Consultants of
the PWP and the ADAB forum. Contracts should be negotiated and signed for NGO
participation in all 21 polders covered by the project by December 1995.
25.       Improved O&M would be introduced on a gradual basis from the first
project year in about 300 km of CERP embankment where no rehabilitation works
are proposed, and on completed PWP embankments. Full coverage would be
reached by year four, after rehabilitation has been completed and the one-year
maintenance period by contractors has expired. Foreshore afforestation may
also start on 4700 ha of foreshore and PWP embankments from project year one.
Afforestation of CERP embankments can take place from project year two or
three and would be completed by year four.
26.       An attempt is being made to organize a pilot afforestation
programme in 1995 in four selected polders where priority works have already
been completed. Each pilot area would consist of a 2.5 km strip and would
involve the following activities:
(a) selection of a different NGO in each area for mobilization and
organization of project participants;
(b) resettlement of 10 squatter or displaced households per trial area
in order to give each a 1 ha plot of embankment;
(c) selection of tree species in collaboration with consultants, FD,
BWDB and beneficiaries based on agro-ecological conditions and
economic needs;
(d) seedlings for pilot planting would be purchased from existing FD,
NGO and private nurseries. In addition two nurseries (one
mangrove, one for upland trees) would be established in each area
to produce seedlings for planting in subsequent years;



-72 -                            ANNEX 3
Page 11 of 11
(e)  training of participants in routine O&M by BWDB based on
development of a one day training module in the field;
(f)  mobilization of three or four groups of about 10 members for
foreshore afforestation of a 30 ha strip in front of the embankment
with training and seedlings provided by FD.
27.       Continual monitoring of the pilot areas would be carried out by the
PIU and consultant Community Development Adviser and the model for community

suitability of vetiver grass for embankment stabilization in different
conditions.

and participation would be financed by the EU for the seven years of the
project. Payments for routine and periodic O&M would be financed by the BWDB
with reimbursement on a declining scale under the IDA credit. Full costs
would be covered under the GOB budget after credit completion. Provision of
seedlings for afforestation on the foreshore and embankments, together with
fertilizer and pesticides for the first three years of afforestation and
payments for foreshore planting work would be covered by the EU grant.

BANGLADESH
COASTAL EMBANKMENT REHABILITATION PROJECT
Forestry Models
A. Introduction
1.          Afforestation of embankment slopes and a belt on the foreshore
would reduce cyclone damage and embankment operation and maintenance (O&M)
costs. The coastal embankments are submerged up to a fair depth during normal
tides, with greater submersion occurring during spring tides and cyclones.
The eroding actions by the sea waves and cyclone surges cause constant
scouring of the embankment base and slopes resulting in damage or, at times,
breaches in the embankment. However, it has been observed that wherever
groves of standing trees exist on the foreshore, such damage is minimal. The
trees, acting as a physical barrier, directly intercept the waves and storm
surges and reduce their speed and erosive action. A belt of standing trees of
appreciable width along the coastline functions as a wall absorbing and
dissipating the first onslaught of storm surges and provides protection of the
embankment as well as lives and property of the people living in the area.
Also, trees of appropriate species planted on the embankment help hold the
embankment soil firmly through their root network. Finally, as a last resort,
many people in the affected area survived cyclones clinging to the top of
appropriate tree species (without thorns and able to bend in the wind). In
addition to reducing cyclone damage and embankment O&M costs, the trees would
provide direct economic benefits in terms of their produce and would produce
environmental benefits. The foreshore afforestation would also accelerate
accretion of new land.
2.          The proposed area for afforestation consists of embankment slopes
and a 50 to 200 meter wide strip of foreshore (depending on land suitability
and availability) of the 21 polders included in the project. Total length of
embankment available for afforestation is about 575 km, covering an area of
about 1900 hectares on the slopes. On the foreshore, a length of about 440
km, covering an area of approximately 4,700 hectares, would be available for
afforestation. (Details of length and area by polder for the embankment slope
and the foreshore are given in Tables 1 and 2 respectively.) In addition, an
area of 30 hectares out of about 35 hectares provided as homestead land for
the participants in embankment maintenance would be available for planting
trees or growing crops. Foreshore land, according to a BWDB survey (which
covered 20 out of the 21 CERP polders), includes 1,090 ha in the inter-tidal
zone and almost 11,000 ha of raised foreshore between the high tide mark and
embankment alignment. About half of the raised foreshore is privately owned
and used for cropping. There are limited areas of trees, homesteads, shrimp
pond and salt pans, but most of the remaining raised foreshore is
uncultivated. It is assumed that all the inter-tidal zone would be planted to
mangrove.   The area of raised foreshore available for planting has been
assumed to be 80% of publicly owned, non-used land, and 20% of private and
cropped land. The Forest Department (FD) has a very successful afforestation
project on an island near Polder 48 which shows that afforestation is possible
in the coastal areas as long as appropriate tree species (especially those



-75-                            ANNEX 4
Page 2 of 11
with some tolerance of salt) are selected. Planned phasing of embankment and
foreshore afforestation is given in Tables 3 and 4 respectively.
B. Imvlementation Concept
3.          A community participation approach is proposed for dealing with
the issues of afforestation and routine embankment maintenance. Families, now
squatting on embankments to be resectioned by the project, and other landless
families would be settled (to the extent of available area) on the toe of the
embankment on the inland side. They would be given contracts allowing them to
reside on this allotment and the rights to the produce of the trees on a given
section of the embankment (of about 1 ha.) in return for providing routine
maintenance and care of trees on this section. They would be paid for their
embankment maintenance activities by Bangladesh Water Development Board (BWDB)
until the trees had grown sufficiently to provide adequate income, but the
payments would be gradually reduced and eventually eliminated as the income
from the trees increased. They would also be provided seedlings, technical
assistance in planting and maintaining approved species, and fertilizer and
pesticides for the first three years after planting. In areas where
sufficient land in the foreshore is Government owned, groups of landless
people would be paid to plant appropriate species over a period of four years.
Thereafter community groups would provide the small amounts of labor required
to maintain and protect these forests, motivated both by the protection
provided by these forests to the community as well as the rights to the
produce of these forests. NGOs active in the respective project areas would
be employed to mobilize and organize local people to participate in the
project and to provide training in tree planting and maintenance and routine
embankment maintenance. BWDB and FD would provide necessary technical
training to NGO staff and possibly directly to community participants. In
areas where the foreshore is privately owned, owners would be encouraged to
grow trees on 50-200 m strips along the shoreline (which are in most cases
unsuitable for other crops). The project would plant the seedlings at no
charge to the landowners, in return for the landowners' agreement to maintain
the area under trees. The agreement would also provide that the landowners
would receive in the case of any land now used for crop production, the value
of the income foregone from crop production until income from the trees is
available (about 8 years). The agreement would permit landowners to use
forest products according to sound forestry practices, with landowner
obligated to replant trees in areas of harvesting. The community
participation effort would be supervised and encouraged by local committees
headed by the Union Parishad Chairman anu including representatives of the
local NGOs, and BWDB and FD local staff and a representative of the
consultants.
C. Plantation Models
(i) Selection of Tree Species
4.          Plantation models have been developed for the embankment slopes,
foreshore, and homestead. Figure 1 shows the model for the slopes and
foreshore while Figure 2 shows the model for the homestead. These models



-76-                            ANNEX 4
Page 3 of 11
include a wide range of species to allow flexibility in selecting species to
be grown in a given area. Guidelines for spacings and planting arrangements
are given in the following sections.
5.          Models for embankment slope afforestation take account of the need
for species which are resistant to cyclonic winds and (on the outer slope)
salinity, have root structures that will strengthen the embankment, and which
would produce adequate income. Within these constraints, those allotted
sections of the embankment would be allowed some choice of species in response
to personal preferences and local market opportunities. Since the target
group to be involved in routine embankment maintenance are mostly poor people,
the selection of trees to be planted on the embankment slopes has been
designed to generate quick returns from the plantation for the people's
subsistence. A combination of fruit trees that yield in the short term and
those that yield in the long term as well as timber trees have been selected.
Quick growing fuelwood species have also been included in the models since the
supply of firewood is inadequate in the area. Arhar or vetiver grass 1 hedge
rows would be planted along the crest of the embankment due to their advantage
as soil binders. Arhar also produces an edible pulse. The models take
account of the need for spacing of the trees to permit sunlight to reach the
embankment slopes, so that grass or other undergrowth is maintained. Planting
of annual crops that require tilling the slopes would be prohibited because
such action would loosen the soils and contribute to erosion. Growing of
bananas and papaya would also be prohibited because on harvest or natural
death of the plant, the rotting of the herbaceous stem and roots create holes
and subsequent breaches in the embankment that take place from hollowing.
options for suitable species for the foreshore take into consideration
variations in soils and tidal conditions.
(ii) Model I: Embankment SloDes
6.          A typical height of the embankment would be about four meters 2
and the inner slope (country side) would have a gradient of 1:3. The inner
slope available for tree planting would thus be about 12 meters. Two rows of
palms would be planted on the inner slope, the first row running 1 m below the
crest of the embankment and the second row running along the foot of the
embankment. In the top row date palms would be spaced at lOm with a betelnut
palm planted between the dates. In the bottom row coconuts would be spaced at
lOm with two betelnuts between each pair of coconuts. Between the two rows of
palms one row of fruit trees would be planted, that would bear fruit in two to
three years (such as guava, lemon, kul, and sharifa). The spacing between the
fruit trees should be such that sunlight reaches the ground to allow growth of
grass. A few calms of bamboo may be planted along the toe of the embankment,
in the outmost periphery. Since the height of the embankment is variable, the
1.    Vetiver will be grown on an experimental basis initially in five areas,
one km. rows on both sides of the crest.   Depending on the results,
vetiver or other hedge row species would be used in the general model.
2.    Designed crest level less distance of the base above sea level.



-77-                            ANNEX 4
Page 4 of 11
row to row distance of the palm and fruit trees would vary at different
locations.
7.          A typical outer slope (sea facing) would have a gradient of 1:7.
Given the typical embankment height of 4m, the slope would have an width of
28.8m available for afforestation. The outer slope would have four rows of
palms with, between the palm rows, four rows of timber trees. The first row
of palms would be im below the crest width with a lOm space to the second row
of palms. These two rows would comprise of alternate coconut and date palm at
a Sm spacing. The third row of palms would be positioned at 8m from the
second row of palms, with the last palm row a further 8m distant. The third
and forth palm rows would be planted with alternate date and palmyra palms at
a spacing of 5 m. The intervening space between the palm rows would be
planted with timber species with a plant to plant distance of 2 m. Timber
species would include koroi, shisoo, babul, khair, khoya babla, mahogany, etc.
As embankment height is variable, the number of rows of plants will also vary
at different locations.
8.          In order to prevent scouring of the embankment berm by sea waves,
a 2m wide strip (lm along the slope and lm flat berm) should be planted with
dholkolmi, shada akand, keya, golpata, and other suitable species. The
planting would be done by cuttings, either directly planted into the ground or
grown in polypots, at a spacing of lm x lm.
9.           Two rows each of arhar and ipil-ipil would be grown in dense
line, along the embankment length, to function as protective hedges. The
arhar rows would run O.5m below the crest width, on both sides, and the ipil-
ipil rows would run along the toe of the embankment, above the submersion
level, on the outer slope. Ipil-ipil is likely to reach a considerable height
in one growing season and so may blow over. Therefore it would need to be
regularly cut to a height of l.5m, producing a substantial quantity of
fuelwood and fodder. Arhar will produce an edible pulse and fuelwood.
Depending on the results of experiments, vetiver may replace arhar in some
locations.
(iii) Model II: Foreshore
10.         Raised foreshore areas with clay soils would be planted with
species such as raintree, koroi, khoya babla, simul, and kadam, grown in
polypots. Sandy soil and sand dune areas would be primarily planted with
jhaw, but other species like eucalyptus camaldulensis and khoya babla would be
tried in areas where some proportion of clay is observed. The plant spacing
would be 2 m x 2 m. The low lying inter-tidal areas (mud flats) would be
planted with mangrove species at a spacing of 1.5 m x 1.5 m. The choice of
species would depend on the maturity of the formations and physical
characteristics of the topsoil. While new formations would be planted with
keora, the comparatively harder soils would be planted with baen, kankra, and
gewa.



-78 -                           ANNEX 4
Page 5 of 11
(iv) Model III: Homestead
11.         Settlers would build a house at the edge of the homestead plot
furthest from the embankment facing north or east. The rest of the homestead
area would be a kitchen garden growing seasonal vegetables and fruits (banana,
papaya, mango). Fuelwood trees and betelnut could also be grown around the
plot. It is not envisaged that the project would provide any formal
assistance in the cultivation of homestead plots, but NGOs may provide advice
and other support on an ad hoc basis.
D. Estimation of PlantinQ Stock Reauirements
12.         Seedling requirements per km of embankment and foreshore are
estimated as follows:
(i) Embankment

betelnut 400)

sharifa, and jack fruit.

E. Nursery Practice and Cost of Establishment
13.         The project would aim to support the development of private sector
nurseries, both by procuring as many seedlings as possible from existing
sources of supply, and by funding the establishment of additional nurseries by
NGOs and individuals. A number of nurseries are already established in
project polders by the FD, NGOs and private individuals. Channels have also
been developed to bring seedlings in for other areas such as Bola island and
Barisal. Although it will not be possible to precisely define nursery
requirements until detailed site specific planting plans are drawn up and the
capacity of local nurseries assessed, for the purposes of overall project
planning it has been assumed that 30% of fruit and timber trees and palms will
be procured from outside sources together with 10% of hedging plants. It is
assumed that all mangrove seedlings will be produced by project nurseries.
Only the production of mangrove seedlings, which require specialist
techniques, would remain the exclusive preserve of the forestry department.
Project nurseries for other seedlings will be established by channelling funds
via NGOs who will either establish nurseries themselves or support the
establishment of nurseries by individuals or groups.
14.         One year old seedlings, especially in the case of fruit trees and
mainland timber species, have a higher rate of survival when transplanted.
The general nursery technique would be polypot raised seedlings in 25 cm x 15
cm x 0.08 mm size polypots, except coconut, betelnut, and mangroves, that
would be bare rooted, and ipil-ipil that would be raised in 15 cm x 10 cm x



- 80 -                          ANNEX 4
Page 7 of 11
0.06 mm size polypots. Seedlings of dholkolmi and sada akand may be grown in
polypots or the hedge rows may be established by direct planting or cuttings.
15.         The area required for nurseries on each polder has been calculated
from forecast seedling requirements and the nursery area required by seedlings
of different species (allowing for half the nursery area to be taken by paths
and storage areas). Allowances have been made for bought in seedlings, and a
failure rate (requiring replanting) of 40% for embankment planting and 100t
for foreshore planting. Planting is assumed to be spread over one to four
years for embankment species (depending on the embankment construction
schedule) and five years (including one year of replacement planting) for
foreshore seedlings. On this basis the numbers of nurseries required in each
polder.has been calculated in Table 5. The maximum size of a nursery is
assumed to be lha, and if the required area exceeds this, additional nurseries
will be required. A total of 29 non-mangrove nurseries (average size 0.65ha)
and 17 mangrove nurseries (average 0.15ha) would be established. In practice
NGO supported nurseries may be considerably smaller, but to split a nursery
into a number of sub-nurseries would have minimal impact on project cost
projections. Although it has been assumed that it will not be possible to
ship seedlings between nurseries on different polders, transport within a
polder should not be too much of a problem along the newly constructed
embankments.
16.         Each nursery would have a supervisors and an average of 14 workers
and guards for non-mangrove species, and 7 workers/guards for mangrove
nurseries. Each nursery would have sheds for workers' accommodation and
storage, together with fencing and tools. Non-mangrove nurseries would need a
supply of water (surface tank or tubewells), while mangrove nurseries need a
small embankment and simple sluice to control the level of tidal water.   The
cost of nurseries is estimated as follows:

Cost calculations for seedling production (excluding nursery establishment,

Table 6. Costs excluding nursery establishment, overheads and labor amount to

foreshores, and Tk 10,689 per ha for mangroves. Nursery establishment,
overheads and labor would add about Tk 9,400 per ha to these costs.
17.         Project cost allowances also include a van rickshaw (Tk 5,000
each) and bicycle (Tk 3,500 each) for each nursery. In addition FD
supervision and management costs to be funded by the project include
allowances for four ACFs responsible for each of the zones (Tk 12,000 each per
year) and 21 foresters posted to the polders (Tk 5,000 each per year),
together with four 4-wheel drive vehicles (Tk 1,000,000 each), 21 motorcycles
(Tk 100,000 each) and two speed boats (Tk 600,000 each), and vehicle running
costs and country boat rental (Tk 17,500,000 over 7 years). The above costs
exclude the costs of NGOs organizing and supervising community participation
in carrying out the afforestation program.
F. Production. Incomes and Marketing
18.         Estimated annual production from the proposed afforestation
component is summarized in Table 7. The major types of output that would come
from afforestation are fruits, vegetables, timber, and fuelwood. About
227,600 tons of fruits and vegetables and 224 million cubic feet of timber and
fuelwood are expected to be produced over the project life of 30 years. While
93% of the production of fruits and vegetables is expected to come from the
embankment slope and the rest from the homesteads, 96% of the timber
production is expected to come from the foreshore.
19.         Farm budgets for a one-hectare model farm on the embankment
slopes, a 15-hectare model farm on the foreshore, and a 200 m2 homestead farm
on the country side toe of the embankment are summarized in Tables 8, 9, and
10, respectively. The beneficiaries would begin to realize net positive
incomes from the afforestation, if all inputs including family labor were
valued at market prices,3 from the first, fourth, and ninth year of
afforestation on the homestead land, embankment slope, and foreshore,
respectively. In light of the fact that the Government would provide the
seedlings and the first three years requirements of fertilizer and pesticides,
and that embankment settlers would be using unpaid family labor, these
settlers would be receiving net positive cash incomes from the embankment
slope and homestead land from year one and the foreshore from year nine (see
Tables 5, 6, and 7). About 30% of the trees in the foreshore would be ready
for felling in the ninth year from planting. In the case of the homestead
plantation, all fruit trees would be producing and 30% of fuelwood trees would
be ready for felling in the sixth year.
3.    The seedling costs in the financial models are at market prices, which
contain an element of subsidy in that Government prices for seedlings do
not cover nursery establishment costs. These additional costs of about
Tk 7,800 per ha for nursery establishment and personnel plus the costs of
FD vehicles and vehicle operating expenditures and NGO services costs have
been added back in for the purposes of economic analysis and project cost
estimates.



-82 -                           ANNEX 4
Page 9 of 11
20.         Fruits and timber would be the two major products expected from
the afforestation on embankment slopes and foreshore. There would be adequate
demand for these products. Fruits are in short supply in the coastal belt as
well as in the rest of the country for most of the year. Hence the farmers
are not expected to face difficulties in marketing the increased production of
fruits. Fuelwood and timber are also in short supply. The timber is used in
constructing houses and building fishing boats. There is a large fishing
community in the project area. The coastal areas are well connected by rivers
and, to some extent, by roads. Surplus output of fruits, timber or fuelwood
in a given area would thus be transported to deficit areas without much
difficulty.
