- 102 -                         ANNEX 5
Page 1 of 10
BANGLADESH
COASTAL EMBANKMENT REHABILITATION PROJECT
Improved Operation and Maintenance
Introduction
1.          The project provides for improved operation and maintenance for
twenty one polders located mainly in the south eastern part of the country.
Improved O&M would cover: operational activities which need to be carried out
to ensure the safety of embankments, to check that drainage canals (khals) are
effective, and for the safety and proper operation of drainage structures; and
maintenance activities on: (a) about 505 Km of sea facing embankments, and
about 260 Km of interior and marginal embankments on the non-coastal side
(Table 1); (b) all drainage regulators and surface sluices; (c) the drainage
network within the protected area and the outfall canals; and (d) the
afforested sections of the embankment slopes. These polders provide
protection to agricultural land with the exception of Polder 62, Patenga which
protects major public and private properties including the airport, naval
academy and export processing zone. The implementing agencies, BWDB and
Forest Department assisted by consultants, NGOs and the elected members of the
Union Parishad, would carry out O&M utilizing the community particularly
landless persons, squatters on embankment, and destitute women.
Present Condition
Organization and Management.
2.          The BWDB Headquarter's Technical Unit under the overall control of
the Member O&M assisted by the Chief Engineer O&M is responsible for
rehabilitation and improvement of operational projects and the O&M of these
projects. The unit comprises an O&M Directorate and a Rehabilitation
Directorate headed by Project Directors (level of Superintendent Engineers).
The unit is required to: (a) review and consolidate the annual budget requests
prepared by field divisions covering operation, maintenance and rehabilitation
and establish priorities under budget constraints; (b) develop long term (five
years) rolling budget requirements for O&M covering operational projects and
projects expected to be completed during the plan period; (c) guide the field
divisions in preparing and updating O&M manuals; (d) participate in
maintenance reviews, monitor progress and evaluate performance; (e) assist O&M
field staff in solving new and difficult problems pertaining to O&M and
rehabilitation; and (f) assist the BWDB Training Directorate in preparing and
executing O&M training programs.
3.          At the field level, there are on average, three subdivisions
within a division and each is supervised by a Sub-divisional Engineer (SDE),
supported by three Section Officers (SOs)and a variable number of Works
Assistants (WAs). Embankment and Sluice Operators (Khalashis) are located at
each polder to carry out the operational activities and to assist in ensuring
the security of infrastructure. Job responsibilities of all field staff are
specified in the basic O&M manual prepared in 1967.for the Coastal Embankment
Project.



- 103-                          ANNEX 5
Page 2 of 10
Annual Budciets
4.          Budgetary requirements for O&M, which includes: operations,
routine and periodic maintenance, maintenance during the implementation period
of on-going projects, and flood and cyclone damage repairs are provided for
under four categories: the revenue budget, development budget, food for work
program (FFW) and the foreign exchange budget. The main sources of funding
for operations, routine and periodic maintenance are from the revenue budget
and the FFW program. The annual divisional O&M budget is based on the
following categories: (a) building repairs and construction; (b) protective
works; (c) resectioning works; (d) construction and repair of sluices;
(e) establishment; (f) transport and equipment; and (g) supply of fall boards
and gates. There is no facility for further breakdown of categories into
routine and periodic maintenance or secondary categories to enable monitoring
of physical and financial progress and establishment of realistic costs for
routine and periodic maintenance. BWDB uses up to 90% of its FFW allocations
for maintenance activities comprising: resectioning of embankments; breach
filling; and re-excavation of drainage channels. A major part (estimated at
about 67*) of the revenue budget was utilized to meet the high establishment
costs. It has been established by previous studies and as discussed in the
SAR for Systems Rehabilitation Project, Cr. 2099-BD (SRP) that the allocation
from the revenue budget for actual O&M costs was about 40% short of the actual
requirement with the main shortfall recorded in the maintenance category where
in FY89 there was an estimated shortfall of about 75%. Under SRP, the
Government agreed to increase allocations for O&M by at least 10* a year in
real terms. This agreement has been adhered to. The budget for maintenance
items (electricity, fuel and maintenance works) in FY94 was about US$12.6
million equivalent, close to double the US$6.5 million allocation in FY89.
Problems and Actions Directed to Imvrove OhM
5.          Problems could be categorized under two headings:  (1) Budgetary:
As earlier stated, activities which broadly fit into O&M are financed from
four sources. The four budgets are prepared separately and at present there
is no system to extract and develop an overall O&M work program. An overall
budget with realistic and identifiable items of work: (a) would prove and
justify annual requirements based on need; (b) adjust work programs in a
logical manner to match the allocation of scarce funds; and (c) assist in the
introduction of an effective monitoring system, identify issues and introduce
changes in a timely manner; and (2) Maintenance Activities: The present
absence of clear definitions of O&M (particularly maintenance) and its
objectives provides a severe restraint on the current annual and long term
planning of O&M activities. Planning of annual maintenance and the drawing up
of a rolling long term plan require:
(a)   the work activities such as routine and periodic maintenance, and
repairs to damages caused by floods and cyclone storms;
(b)   updating of the inventory of all BWDB facilities;
(c)   estimating rehabilitation and improvement requirements to bring
projects into a condition that routine and periodic maintenance
could commence and ensure the project's sustainability;



-104 -                         ANNEX 5
Page 3 of 10
(d)   work standards and outputs;
(e)   community participation in routine and periodic maintenance;
(f)   an assessment of resources (manpower, materials and equipment);
and
(g)   annual work programs and schedules.
6.          Operational activities in polders in the coastal belt and flood
control projects in the mainland of the country are well defined and could be
carried out satisfactorily if all the infrastructure of the polders are
completed to the required design and are in an operational condition. In
irrigation projects several issues which are constraints to effective
operations have been identified and these are being addressed adequately under
the IDA financed Second Small Scale Flood Control, Drainage and Irrigation
Project, Cr.1870-BD (SSFCDI) and the SRP. Maintenance activities are effected
adversely due to several issues which need to be addressed before an effective
and sustainable system could be introduced. The issues include: (a) squatters
on the embankments and cuts in the embankment slopes to accommodate their
homesteads; (b) cultivation of perennial cash crops on embankment slopes;
(c) unauthorized planting of trees on the embankment slopes; (d) deep runnels
on embankment slopes; (e) earth slips at structures; (f) breaches caused by
floods and cyclonic storms, being left in a state of disrepair to face the
monsoons; (g) repairs of collapsed protection works not being done in a timely
manner; (h) settlement of the embankments due to poor compaction and slopes
not done to the designed profile; (i) flap gates at structures in a state of
disrepair or in most cases missing; (j) protection works, structural aprons
and wing walls of structures, damaged due to erosion; (k) silting up of
drainage network particularly in outfall canals affected by tidal conditions.
7.          In order to establish a rationale and sustainable plan for O&M and
particularly for maintenance, the issues listed above are being addressed
under several donor assisted on-going projects such as the IDA financed SRP
(Cr.2099-BD) and the SSFCDI Project (Cr.1870-BD); the Dutch financed Land
Reclamation Project (LRP), Delta Development Project (DDP), and the Early
Implementation Project (EIP); the ADB financed Ganges-Kobadak Rehabilitation
Project (GKRP); and the IDA and CIDA financed Operation and Maintenance Cost
Cell (O&MCC) under the SSFCDI Project. The Headquarters O&M Technical unit
with the separation of rehabilitation and O&M functions and training directed
to O&M activities has already been established. The unit is presently
addressing aspects of routine and periodic maintenance including costs and the
O&MCC is as a first step establishing standards and creating a computer based
program for a country wide schedule of rates for construction works. The CKRP
Project would establish a system to provide for floods and cyclone repairs in
the O&M program. Under the SRP, models are being experimented on for
determining the effectiveness of carrying out periodic and routine maintenance
by groups of landless persons and destitute women. The CERP Project would
address the issue of squatters and cultivation of embankment slopes and direct
its effort to involve the community in carrying out all routine maintenance
and a major part of periodic maintenance.



- 105 -                          ANNEX 5
Page 4 of 10
ODeration and Maintenance Activities
8.          Operation.  Activities to be executed continuously include:
(a) patrolling of the embankment to detect weak sections, slips, signs of
squatter settlements, cultivation of perennial cash crops, cuts in the
embankment to accommodate homesteads, embankment subsidence and erosion and/or
settlement of protection works; (b) regular checks of structures to detect
slips at abutments, erosion of aprons, protection works and wing walls, and
periodic damage to flap gates and fall boards; (c) regulation of gates if
applicable (d) supervision of routine maintenance work. In addition at the
beginning of the monsoon, a condition survey (if needed an engineering survey)
of the embankment would be needed for estimating the periodic, cyclonic and
flood damage repairs to be carried out during the dry season. During the
monsoon operational activities would be stepped up to ensure timely action to
prevent damage. At the end of the monsoon, operational activities would
include: the condition survey of the embankment, and structures and an
engineering survey of the drainage network. These surveys would be required
to prepare details for carrying out periodic maintenance.
9.          Routine or Preventive Maintenance.  Activities to be executed
continuously include: (a) filling of runnels in embankment slopes and road;
(b) filling of minor slips in embankment slopes and abutments and wing walls
of structures; (c) repairing of rat holes; (d) preventive measures to stop
leaks and piping through the embankment; (e) maintaining recently planted
approved type of trees and replacement of trees, turf or grass bush;
(f) arresting settlement/erosion in aprons and protection works at structures;
(g) arresting collapse of protection works provided for prevention of river
bank erosion and wave action on embankment slopes; (h) clearing of debris and
water hyacinth in structure intake and outfall canals; (i) greasing of lifting
mechanism of regulators and surface sluices; (j) preventing cattle damage by
erecting and maintaining fences; (k) maintaining reservations along
embankments; (1) preventing new settlements on the embankment and reservations
or making of cuts in embankment slopes to accommodate homesteads; and
(m) clearing of debris and man-made obstacles, such as fish fences, in the
drainage canals.
10.         Periodic Maintenance.  Activities to be executed during the dry
season include: (a) maintaining the design profile of the embankment and
drainage canals; (b) repair of breaches and major slips caused by normal
annual monsoon and cyclonic storms; (c) repair of protection works provided
for arresting river bank erosion and damage due to wave action and providing
preventive works to minimize such damage; (d) permanent repairs to arrest
piping through the embankment; (e) permanent repairs to arrest damage to
aprons, wing walls and revetments at structures; (f) repairs or replacement of
flap gates and lifting mechanism of vertical lifting gates and replacement of
gate and sill rubber seals; (g) repairs or replacement of trashracks of
structures; (h) repairs to eroded concrete at structures; and (i) desilting of
drainage canals (frequency to be decided after a study of silting condition).
Earthworks would include compaction. Frequency of these activities would
depend on hydrological, and tidal conditions of the polder, and the soils used
in earthfill of embankments. Generally with effective routine maintenance the
frequency would be once every two years.



-106 -                           ANNEX 5
Page 5 of 10
11.         Repairs to damages caused by floods and cyclonic storms.  These
repairs are assessed after each storm and are financed under the development
budget which is funded by GOB and donor assisted emergency programs. (The
IDA, EU, Japan and Saudi funded PWP, and the several IDA and ADB assisted
Flood Rehabilitation Projects are a few examples of such works). Repairs
include: (a) filling of major breaches including retirements; (b) major
repairs to protection works or provision of new works; (c) major resectioning
of embankments; (d) repair or replacement of structures; and (e) replacement
of trees on embankment slopes.
12.         Afforestation.  With the exception of the recent introduction of
afforestation of embankment slopes on a very limited scale, afforestation has
not been introduced as a protection measure. Foreshore afforestation
development has not been systematically introduced to be effective as a
protection measure to embankments. It has now been established that embankment
and foreshore afforestation are the most economical and effective type of
protection work to ensure the security of the embankment during cyclonic
storms. Maintenance activities include: (a) upkeep of paths to facilitate
inspection of trees; (b) clearing around trees; (c) application of
fertilizers; (d) harvesting of produce from fruit trees; (e) controlled
harvesting of fuel woods and leaves; and (f) replanting according to set
guidelines and replacement of diseased or dead trees.
Proposals for Improving Operation and Maintenance
13.         The sustainability of a rehabilitated and improved polder would
depend primarily on effective routine and periodic maintenance. It could only
be ensured if the community which is safeguarded by the infrastructure
participates fully in these maintenance activities. The chairman and members
of the Union Parishad together with NGOs located in the area need to act as
facilitators to organize those participating in maintenance, particularly
routine maintenance, and help BWDB supervise the adequacy of their work. The
Project, while ensuring that technical standards are not sacrificed, provides
for a package of incentives to achieve the objective of maximum community
participation.
14          BWDB and the Forest Department would be the implementing agencies
for the embankment O&M and afforestation and the foreshore afforestation
components respectively. All operational activities would be carried out by
the staff of BWDB. Routine maintenance of the rehabilitated embankments
including the plantations on embankment slopes would be handled by individual
families who would be granted contractual rights to the produce of trees in
return for their maintenance activities. Until such time as sufficient income
is obtained from the produce of the trees, these families would be paid an
allowance sufficient to sustain them (para 16 below and Annex 4). The
planting of foreshore afforestation would be carried out by community groups
and would be financed under the project. Maintenance, harvesting, and
replacement planting of harvested trees on public foreshore land would be
carried out by community groups, who would receive 75% of the produce.
Maintenance, harvesting, and replacement planting of foreshore afforestation
on private land would be carried out by the landowners on terms and conditions
described in Annex 4. Routine and periodic maintenance of embankments not
rehabilitated under the project, including the sections with existing heavy
plantations, and the periodic maintenance of embankment sections rehabilitated



-107 -                          ANNEX 5
Page 6 of 10
under the project would be carried out either by community groups or, in the
absence of such viable groups, by contracts awarded following LCB procedures.
Funds for carrying out repairs to damages caused by floods and cyclonic storms
during the implementation period are provided for by the project. The
provision is adequate to cover storms which occur on average once every 5
years. Damage repairs would be contracted out to local contractors under
awards made following LCB procedures.
15.         The consultant responsible for implementation support would
provide advice and guidance to the implementing agencies in all technical and
social aspects of the improved O&M program. In particular, the consultant
would establish standards for all O&M activities, carry out work studies on
routine and periodic maintenance and khal re-excavation, update project rates
and work tasks, and monitor progress and evaluate the impact of the program.
During the project perod the consultant would at regular intervals update work
tasks, rates and costs. The project cost estimates include funds for seven
categories of improved O&M expenditures. These include: (i) operational
activities; (ii) routine maintenance of rehabilitated embankments inclusive of
maintenance of trees on embankment for a specified period which would be from
the end of contractual commitment of the rehabilitation work until the produce
of trees on the embankment would provide adequate income; (iii) routine and
periodic maintenance of the embankments not rehabilitated and periodic
maintenance of the embankments rehabilitated under the project; (iv) routine
and periodic maintenance of the drainage network; (v) routine and periodic
maintenance of protection works; (vi) routine and periodic maintenance of
structures; and (vii) damage repairs to earthworks, protection works and
structures, caused by floods and cyclonic storms. IDA financing of
operation/maintenance and flood and cyclone damage repairs would be at a
declining scale (85% of O&M expenditures through FY99, 65% in FYOO and FY01
and 45% in FY02. Organizational and financing aspects for the O&M activities
are given below.
16.         Operation.  BWDB's existing field divisions and sub-divisions are
adequately staffed to carry out effectively all operational activities.
However, the field staff lack a proper understanding of these activities and
their importance for proper maintenance. Training provided under the project
would improve the situation. The project would include the required costs for
activities to ensure effective operation of the polders and the work would be
executed by the existing field staff of BWDB. Operational costs are estimated
on the basis of utilizing one patrol laborer (khalashi) for 6 Km of embankment
and one gate khalashi for three structures. Provision is also made for
engineering surveys to be carried out annually and an overhead cost of 10% for
services rendered by the SO and the SDE. No separate provision is made for
patrolling drainage canals as the gate khalashi can perform this task. It is
estimated that about Tk 5,000 would be required for 1 Km;
17.         Routine or Preventive Maintenance.  The responsibility for
maintenance would be on the community. BWDB would provide the technical
advice and NGOs would be responsible for supervision. BWDB, to ensure that
standards are met, would regularly check the work and advise NGOs on measures
needed to correct any substandard work (see para 22 below). Squatters and
those made landless by land acquisition under the project (and other landless
families to the extent of available area) would be settled at the toe of the
embankment on the country side and would be given contracts allowing them to



- 108 -                            ANNEX 5
Page 7 of 10
reside on this allotment and obtain the produce of the trees on a given
section of the embankment. It is estimated that an embankment section of 250
meters (four families per Km) would produce adequate benefits to maintain a
family (Annex 4). In return, the family would provide routine maintenance of
their section of the embankment and the maintenance of trees in this reach.
They would be paid for their embankment maintenance activities by BWDB until
the trees had grown sufficiently to provide an adequate income. If hired
labor is employed, the cost of routine maintenance to ensure that the designed
profile and crest level are maintained throughout the year, and in addition
reduce periodic maintenance costs, is estimated at Tk 18,000 per Km or about
Tk 4,500 per ha of embankment for the life of the embankment. In addition to
plant and maintain trees on the embankment using hired labor would cost about
Tk 2,000 per ha on average for the first three years after embankment
completion. The arrangements under the proposed project would involve
somewhat higher costs during the initial years to sustain families until the
trees had reached sufficient maturity but would eliminate the need to pay for
routine embankment maintenance thereafter. To sustain families during the
period of maturity of trees, each family would receive Tk 9,000 in YR1,
Tk 8,000 in YR2, Tk 7,000 in YR3, Tk 6,500 in YR4. The produce of the trees
and the homestead plots should be sufficient to provide subsistence from YR5.
However, as a contingency to deal with possible delays, cost estimates provide
funds in YR5 to cover half the families receiving Tk 4,500 each. The NGOs
would monitor the actual earnings from embankment afforestation and homestead
cultivation. In case of major disruptions in a polder from the assumed
afforestation program, such as might occur due to significant storms or a
disease outbreak, the NGOs would recommend appropriate modifications to the
declining payment scale. Participating families would also be provided
seedlings and technical advice in planting approved species on the embankment
and would be reimbursed for their expenditure for fertilizer and pesticides
for the first three years. Provision would be made to remove families who do
not adequately perform their duties. Modified arrangements would apply in
Sandwip (Polder 72) and Ramgati (Polder 59/2) due to the high intensity of
squatter settlement. Groups would be organized to take responsibility for
embankment afforestation and maintenance, with each family responsible for the
maintenance and receiving the benefit of the produce of the trees for a much
smaller section of the embankment than in the standard model (perhaps 1/4 to
1/8 ha). This would not be expected to provide subsistence income but
(together with the produce from the homestead plot) would supplement whatever
sources of income had been maintaining the families up until now.
18.         Periodic Maintenance.  BWDB, assisted by consultants, would
estimate at the end of the monsoon season the requirements for the periodic
maintenance of the embankment, drainage regulators and sluices, protection
works, and the drainage network. Work plans and procurement methods would be
agreed upon and implementation would commence at the start of the dry season
and completed before the onset of the monsoons. Efforts would be made to
allocate earthwork's contracts to landless groups and destitute women groups.
In areas where such groups could not be organized, the work would be on
contracts awarded following LCB procedures. The role of NGOs is described in
para 22. Earthwork costs have been estimated based on about 800 cubic meters
of earthwork performed by labor intensive groups (Tk 30,000 per Km for
embankments, Tk 17,000 per Km for khals), protection works repairs based on
about 60 cubic meter per Km (Tk 60,000 per Km) and structure repairs at a lump
sum of Tk 10,000 per structure.  It is estimated that about 90 groups each



-109-                            ANNEX 5
Page 8 of 10
with 25 persons would be required to carry out periodic maintenance (29 groups
for areas where no rehabilitation and improvement works have been carried out
and about 60 for areas where rehabilitation and improvement has taken place).
19.         Flood or Cyclone Damacce Repairs.  Provision is made in the project
for flood and cyclone damage repairs on an annual basis. Costs estimated for
a disaster occurrence once every 5 years are calculated on a damage section of
about 3 Km (a realistic assumption as the effects of such storms would be
limited to about a reach of 3 Km). For storms occurring once every 20 and 40
years, special arrangements would be needed to fund damage repairs. It is
expected that if such storms occur after full maturity of the afforested area,
damages would be drastically reduced and GOB with its own resources would be
able to finance the works. Sections that would be affected by such cyclones
are estimated on the basis historical records and not on the full length of
the sea-facing embankments. The annual lump sums provided in the cost
estimates for damage repairs to the sections are estimated on the basis of
rates varying from a maximum of Tk 1,000,000/Km for earthwork, Tk 560,000/Km
for protection works and Tk 25,000/No for structures respectively in the first
year after completion of rehabilitation work to a minimum of Tk 60,000/Km,
Tk 460,000/Km and Tk 10,000/No in the fourth year after completion. The
reduced cost is due to the protection afforded by afforestation.
Community Participation
20.         The concept of community participation in O&M is predicated on
past experience which has shown that the maintenance of embankment,
particularly routine activities has been minimal if not non-existent. BWDB's
record in embankment maintenance is bleak and suggests that this has never
been a priority for the Board. Consequently, it is hypothesized that local
communities who stand to gain most from well maintained embankments, can be
co-opted to undertake routine maintenance on sections of the embankment
abutting or lying in close proximity to their lands. The most affected in the
local community are the squatter population living on the embankment, the
landless (people who have lost their land through erosion during cyclonic
storms and river bank erosion and people whose lands are being acquired), and
destitute women. These people would benefit most from a scheme which would
ensure a subsistence living initially and in due course an adequate income to
maintain themselves and their families. The scheme would also open the
possibility of income generating opportunities. The project also includes an
afforestation program which would be an integral part of the embankment, being
the primary source of protection against damage by cyclonic storms. It would
be difficult for the Forest Department to manage and maintain a tree planting
program on a departmental basis. This points to the desirability of community
participation in the afforestation component.
21.         Role of Locally Elected Officials and NGOs.  It is proposed to
establish locally constituted committees at each Union in the project area to
coordinate project activities in the Union. Such committees would be headed
by the Union Parishad Chairman, and would include the BWDB Subdivisional
Engineer, a Forest Department Range Officer, and representatives of the
participating NGO and DAE. This committee, particularly the Chairman of the
Union Parishad and the NGO, would be responsible, inter alia, for motivation
and organizing the individuals and their families to undertake routine
maintenance. The committee would also assist in controlling future squatter



- 110-                           ANNEX 5
Page 9 of 10
settlements on the embankment. See Annex 3 for details on the role of locally
elected officials and NGOs.
22.         The main responsibility of the NGOs for embankment maintenance
would be to organize the resettled families to carry out effectively the
routine maintenance of the embankments rehabilitated under the project.
Responsibilities would include: (a) assistance to the implementation agencies
and consultants in the resettlement of families; (b) finalizing agreements
between BWDB and the families; (c) direction and supervision of routine
maintenance activities; (d) certification of interim payments (probably
monthly), joint inspections with BWDB to check on the quality of work; and
(e) assist in disbursing monies to families. To perform their duties
effectively, particularly in the technical aspects, NGOs would receive
training in routine maintenance activities. (NGO responsibilities for
afforestation are detailed an Annex 3). For periodic maintenance, since
supervision and payments require special skills which cannot be achieved
without going through a detailed engineering course (e.g topographical
surveys, setting out etc.), NGOs would assist in the formation of groups
including the appointment of the group leader and in administration and
financial aspects of contract management such as maintaining accounting
documents and disbursement of monies to the group members. BWDB would be
responsible for awarding contracts, setting out and supervision, measurements
and payments to the group leader who would be the signatory in the contract
document.
23.         Role of the ImDlementation Support Consultant.  The scope of the
consultant services on social development activities which is directed mainly
on assistance to form effective community participation is described in detail
in Annex 6.
Imlementation Schedule
24.         Under project financing, operational activities and maintenance on
embankment sections completed under the PWP would commence in the first year
of the project (FY96), maintenance activities on the embankments rehabilitated
under the project would commence after the contractual commitments of the
civil work's contracts are fulfilled, generally in project year 3 or 4 (FY98
or FY99). Community participation in maintenance activities is described in
detail in Annex 3.
Estimated Cost
25.         The total cost over a period of seven years is estimated at
Tk 262.6 million (US$6.0 million) inclusive of price contingencies (no
allowance is made for physical contingencies) or about Tk 340,000 per Km for a
length of about 770 Km falling under the maintenance program of the project
for the project period of seven years. The annual cost of O&M based on YR4 of
the post project period would be about Tk 46 million (US$1.1 million) or
Tk 59,000 per Km (US$1,500 per Km) excluding the cost of routine maintenance
which would be carried out by families. The O&M requirements for the polders
during project implementation (yearwise) and after the project period are
given in Table 3 of this annex. (The above cost estimates exclude a total
during the project period of about $0.3 million equivalent for reimbursing
embankment settlers for fertilizer and pesticides for the first three years of



- 111 -                          ANNEX 5
Page 10 of 10
afforestation. For ease of administration these costs would be met through
the O&M budget. The fertilizer and pesticides would be financed under the
forestry component of the project.)
26.   Of the total project cost for O&M, GOB would finance about
US$2.1 million and IDA about US$3.9 million. The cost of routine maintenance
of most of the rehabilitated embankments would continue to be financed by IDA
and GOB during the project period as it is estimated that families responsible
for this maintenance activity would continue to be dependent on the
subsistence allowance until the fifth year of planting (with a contingency
provision for some families still needing continued subsistence payments in
the fifth year). However, families on PWP financed embankments would be
expected to become independent of such subsistence payments during the project
period, while families to be settled on the embankments financed under the
minor works program would require continued subsistence payments for a few
years after the project period. During the first and second post project
year, the estimated annual cost of O&M to be borne by GOB would be about Tk 53
million and Tk 49 million respectively in (FY95 prices) and when remaining
families take over the full responsibility (physical and financial) in the
third post-project year, the cost would reduce to Tk 46 million.
Acqreements to be obtained from GOB
27.         At negotiations, agreement would be reached that adequate annual
budgetary allocations would be provided for O&M, both during the project and
thereafter, including the forecast cost of repairs of damage caused by floods
and cyclonic storms of severity occurring up to once in five years, based upon
the O&M budgeting and monitoring procedures being developed by the O&M costing
and budgeting cell (under the SRP project). Current indicative estimates of
budgetary requirements are given in Table 3 of this Annex. In addition, BWDB
would be expected by June 30, 1997 to update the present O&M manual and
operational plan for the project polders to include details of community
participation in embankment routine maintenance and the concept of
afforestation as a protective measure.

1/    Routine maintenance include the area covered by the slopes and crest.
2/    The lengths specified under the column Interior/Marginal Embankments where no works are proposed are limited to
the sections to be maintained under the project. The remaining sections are in a stable condition or covered with
intensive plantations
3/    Number of families is estimated on the basis of an hectare for routine maintenance with the exception of Ramgati
and Sandwip.
4/    Groups of 25 persons would carry out the routine and periodic maintenance of the embankment reaches where no
rehabilitation and improvements works are proposed under PWP and CERP. Twenty nine groups are estimated to be
required for this purpose. Similar groups would carry out the periodic maintenance of the sections where routine
maintenance is carried out by individual families. For this work about 60 groups would be required annually.
Members of the families responsible for routine maintenance could also participate in the periodic maintenance.
5/    Periodic maintenance is estimated to be carried out on a portion of the embankment at a frequency of two years.
6/    At Ramgati 81 squatter families on the portion to be resectioned would be accommodated within this portion.  Each
family would be responsible to maintain about 0.2 of an ha. The income of the male members are reported to be
mainly from fishing. The female members would be mainly used for maintenance.
7/    At Sandwip, the project would provide for the resettlement of all squatters in the 3.7 km portion of the
resectioned embankment from ch 46.0 to 49.7, 106 families in the same reach of the embankment.  This is due to the
possible refusal of these squatters to resettle far away from the town. The squatters living in the remaining
portion of the resectioned embankment would be resettled in the completed PWP embankments. The resettlement for
the maintenance of the resectioned embankment between ch 49.7 to 53.4, and 57.77 to 58.33, the new embankment
under CERP and the PWP embankments would be on the basis of 4 per km, with each family maintaining an ha.  The
families resttled between ch 46.0 to 49.7 would maintain about 0.14 of an ha.
8/    Total number of families inclusive of members of family groups carrying out routine maintenance is 1,977.

ANNEX 6

BANGLADESH
COASTAL EMBANKMENT REHABILITATION PROJECT
Technical Assistance for Project Implementation and Evaluation
1.         This Annex provides draft Terms of Reference for consultant's
services to assist the implementing agencies in: (a) construction planning and
supervision of civil works in the rehabilitation and improvement component;
(b) the afforestation program; (c) the operation and maintenance of
infrastructure of the polders; (d) the maintenance of the foreshore
afforestation development; (e) organizing with the assistance of the NGOs for
communities to effectively carry out routine maintenance and afforestation of
the embankment and foreshore afforestation; and (f) monitoring and impact
assessment.
Background
2.         The severe cyclone, which hit the south eastern part of the country
on April 29, 1991 caused extensive damages to the coastal embankment and left
large areas virtually unprotected against tidal flooding and cyclone surges.
The Government of Bangladesh (GOB) through a Priority Works Program (PWP)
commencing in FY93 is near to completion of a program of reconstruction and
improvement works of the sea facing embankments in ten polders included in the
Mid-Term Plan which were the worst affected by the cyclone. Work includes
about 190 km of embankment resectioning, 60 km of retired embankments (new),
allied protection works and repair or replacement of drainage structures.
Under the on-going technical assistance financed by the European Community
(EC) consultants provide the project support which includes: (a) the
preparation of engineering documentation; (b) assistance in the evaluation of
bids following ICB procedures; (c) contract management and supervision of all
contracts awarded following ICB procedures; (d) training of staff in
preparation of tender documents, supervision organization, quality control,
site administration, and contractual and legal aspects of contract management;
and (e) monitoring and evaluation of the social impacts, and planning social
development programs including a system for the future Operation and
Maintenance (O&M) of the completed polders mainly directed towards the
participation of the community. In addition, consultants are responsible for
the preparation of engineering documentation inclusive of tender documents for
the eleven polders of the Coastal Embankment Rehabilitation Project (CERP)
with new major works. The social development team are also assisting in
developing programs for community participation in CERP.
3.         Project support for CERP, whilst continuing the emphasis on
supervision and contract management of major civil works contracts to be
awarded following ICB procedures, would include: (a) review, updating and, as
appropriate, revision of detailed engineering documentation of minor civil
works prepared by Bangladesh Water Development Board (BWDB) and the Systems



- 120 -
ANNEX 6
Page 2 of 26
Rehabilitation Project (SRP); (b) assistance in field supervision and quality
control of minor civil works; and (c) assistance to the effective
implementation of the afforestation component and the improved O&M component.
The program of civil works would cover: (i) major reconstruction for about 78
km of embankment resectioning, 73 km of new embankments, protection works and
allied drainage structures of the twelve polders (including work remaining in
one PWP polder); and (ii) in seventeen polders, minor works comprising about
130 km of embankment resectioning, 4 km of new interior embankments,
repairs/replacement of about 145 allied drainage structures, and about 390 km
re-excavation of drainage canals (khals). The programs of afforestation and
improved O&M would be undertaken in twenty-one polders.
Scope of Consultant Services
4.        The consultant's services would cover the following:
(a) contract management and supervision of all major civil works under
the direction of the BWDB inclusive of monitoring of progress and
quality control;
(b) assistance to BWDB in the tendering and award of two ICB contracts
(one major works and one minor works) for completion of PWP works
and one ICB major works contract of additional works (tentatively
estimated to require 6 km of retirement) to cope with recent
erosion.
(c) checking of preconstruction documentation of all minor civil works
prepared by BWDB and the SRP consultants and in association with
BWDB, making changes as required;
(d) assistance to BWDB in the field supervision and monitoring of
quality control of minor civil works;
(e) assistance to the Forest Department and BWDB, in conjunction with
NGOs, in implementing the afforestation program in all technical
and financial aspects inclusive of monitoring of progress and
quality. In addition assistance would include pilot operations
testing use of vetiver grass on project embankments.
(f) assistance to BWDB in implementing the improved O&M component;
(g) in association with NGOs, assistance in planning social development
programs, including a system for the involvement of the community
in the maintenance of completed works;
(h) undertaking monitoring of physical and financial progress and the
economic and social impact in twenty one polders of the Mid-Term
Program;



- 121 -
ANNEX 6
Page 3 of 26
(i) assistance in monitoring to identify any adverse environmental
effects and proposing appropriate mitigating measures;
(j) providing coordination between this project and programs under
other projects, including in particular, the on-going IDA assisted
Agricultural Support Services Project (ASSP), to improve
agricultural productivity in the project area; and
(k) providing specialist inputs to the training program and for studies
to identify the possibility of cost-effective town protection works
for Sandwip.
Responsibilities of the Consultant
5.        Preconstruction Activities.  The consultants supervising
implementation of the PWP project would also be responsible for preparation of
the engineering documents for the major civil works in the eleven new CERP
polders and assisting BWDB in the tendering process. These activities are
expected to be completed before the appointment of the consultants financed
under this project. The project consultants will however be responsible for
preparing new ICB tender documents for about $3 million of work for completion
of PWP works. In addition, the consultants would review alternatives and
prepare a proposed design for cost-effective works to cope with recent erosion
in one of the polders and, after review and approval of the proposal, prepare
ICB tender documents. (About $2 million has been tentatively allocated for
these works.) BWDB field divisions provided the data needed for costing the
minor works.  In addition the SRP consultant carried out feasibility studies
for most of the polders under CERP and PWP, and completed engineering
documentation suitable for tendering. However, some of the feasibility
studies and engineering designs dealt inadequately with environmental and
social conditions and cost effectiveness and do not provide a practical guide
to finalize the engineering documentation for implementation of the minor
works program. The CERP consultant, in association with BWDB field divisions,
would review the proposals, revise the feasibility studies and finalize a cost
effective and practical implementation program. Particular attention would be
paid to minimizing resettlement. After approval of the program, the
consultant would prepare the bidding documents following LCB procedures for
embankment repairs, minor retirements, and repairs/replacement of structures.
Quantities for khal re-excavation and documents suitable for carrying out the
work under the FFW program would also be prepared. Additional surveys
required would be carried by BWDB.
6.        Contract ManaQement and Supervision.  All major civil works in a
polder would be awarded as one contract package following ICB procedures. The
package shall include earthworks, protection works and allied drainage
structures. In most cases, there would be one contract for each polder, but
in a few cases two polders would be included in one package. All minor works
with the exception of Khal re-excavation would be carried out on contracts
awarded following LCB procedures or by landless or destitute women groups



- 122 -
ANNEX 6
Page 4 of 26
contracted on the basis of the engineer's estimatell. Re-excavation of khals
would be carried out on the FFW program. The consultant's field staff
(specifically recruited for the work) would assist BWDB field divisions in the
supervision of all minor works carried out by local contractors, the community
groups and the FFW program. Particularly, consultants would monitor the
quality of work and ensure that early remedial action is taken by BWDB to
rectify any substandard work.
7.        For works awarded on ICB procedures, the consultant would
responsible for the overall and on-site supervision of the contractors
undertaking construction, including supplementary designs, where needed.
Specifically the consultant would be required to:
(a) initially on receipt of the program of execution from contractors
under the terms of the works contract, prepare a program for the
mobilization of his resident site staff and submit this to BWDB for
approval, prior to the actual mobilization;
(b) set up suitable site supervision teams, with adequate staff who
shall reside at each polder, to supervise in association with BWDB
divisional and subdivisional staff and report on the works under
the contract in accordance with the works contract document. The
consultant shall ensure that field supervision are present
continuously at site throughout construction activities. Paras 17
and 18 specify guidelines to assist the consultant in setting up an
effective organization;
(c) ensure that the pre-work surveys are carried out by the contractor
during the mobilization period and plans submitted to the
consultant within two weeks of the commencement of such work. The
consultant should ensure that no work is done by the contractor
without approval of the pre-work survey plans and quantities.
(d) approve the contractor's superintendence and key personnel;
(e) approve the contractor's plant and equipment;
(f) order the removal and replacement of improper materials and work;
(g) check and approve the necessary measurements and calculations for
the contractor's interim running bills and certifying such bills;
(h) examine at site the measurement of any work which is about to be
covered up or put out of view before permanent work is placed
thereon and/or examine and attend the measuring of the completed
work;
1.        Landless  and destitute women groups  would be  limited  to minor
embankment repairs, and minor slips at structures.



- 123 -
ANNEX 6
Page 5 of 26
(i) call and keep minutes of routine site meetings between the parties
of the contract;
(j) complete supplementary details of hydraulic structures required by
the contractors. Required topographical and soil surveys would be
carried out by the contractors under the direction of the
consultant;
(k) provide the required information for the contractor to set out the
works and check the accuracy of the setting out, specifically the
setting up of reference points inclusive of establishment of bench
marks with datum levels set to the required degree of accuracy;
(1) review the contractor's work proposal, working drawings etc. in
accordance with the requirements of the works contract document,
advise modifications as necessary and recommend these proposals for
approval;
(m)  review the contractor's work program, and where necessary request
revisions to account for the current status of the works;
(n) through the chief construction engineers, resident engineers,
technical officers and other on-site field staff, supervise the day
to day operations of the contractor to ensure compliance with the
contract;
(o) supervise tests on materials to be used in the works prior to
incorporation, approve type, quality and source of materials and in
addition tests to be carried by the contractor on completed works
required for ensuring that works meet the standards specified in
the works contract. All labor, materials and equipment required
for tests would be at contractor's expense.
B.        In addition the consultant would be required to:
(a) make recommendations to BWDB on the contractor's claims for
additional payment, extensions of time and other contractual
matters, the relevant site conditions and the contractor's detailed
submissions;
(b) in the event of variations to the works being required, prepare the
necessary documents, negotiate with the contractor and submit the
variation order to BWDB for approval;
(c)  maintain detailed diaries of relevant events and activities;
(d) set up and maintain a correspondence and document storage and
retrieval system to record all relevant communications between the
parties to the contract, all measurement and quality control
details and variations to the work as they occur;



- 124 -
ANNEX 6
Page 6 of 26
(e) maintain as built drawings and documents;
(f) prepare monthly progress reports for each contract in a form
acceptable to BWDB and IDA. These reports will include as a
minimum, details of the physical and financial status of each
contract, details of delays and the budgetary effect of these and
particular problems with suggested solutions;
(g) carry out final inspections of the works and recommend the issue of
the final certificates;
(h) check and certify the contractor's progress payments and after the
final inspection, the final accounts and certify them correct for
payment;
(i) check and certify reimbursement claims to be forwarded to IDA. In
a separate correspondence and document storage and retrieval
system, record all relevant correspondence related to reimbursement
claims;
(j) prepare completion reports in a form acceptable to BWDB and IDA;
(k) during the 12 month contractor maintenance period, the consultant
would periodically check the completed work. Shortly before the
end of the maintenance period, the consultant would carry out the
necessary inspections, specify the rectification work to be done,
supervise this as required, and recommend to BWDB the issuance of
the maintenance certificate.
(1) to ensure the early resettlement of families for the afforestation
of embankment slopes and for routine maintenance the consultants
shall take over the completed section of embankment (new and
resection) and the contractor's work program shall be proposed
accordingly to meet the targets set for afforestation and
resettlement.
9.        For minor civil works such as resectioning or new embankments,
repair or replacement of structures and minor revetments, the consultant would
design records to be maintained at the consultant's site office and monitor
the keeping of such records, including all data for preparing reimbursement
claims. Consultant's would specifically be required to:
(a) finalize the detailed designs, considering both issues of the
stability of embankments and resettlement of displaced families;
(b) establish sufficient reference points set by BWDB for control of
alignment and levels to the required degree of accuracy;
(c) enforce construction procedures, and check at critical stages of
construction such as: for hydraulic structures, excavation of
foundations, fabrication and erection of formwork, reinforcement



- 125 -
ANNEX 6
Page 7 of 26
placement, concrete pours, and fabrication and erection of gates
and lifting mechanism; and for earthfill, compaction in embankment
fill and backfill at structures. In addition assist BWDB in
supervision by posting sufficient numbers of site supervisors of
the level of Works Assistants;
(d) assist BWDB in processing contractor's claims for additional
payments, extensions of time and variation orders;
(e) maintain a detailed diary of relevant events for each polder. The
diary would contain records of field observations given by
consultants staff, discussions with staff of the zonal, circle and
field divisions and subdivisions of the implementation organization
BWDB, comments on quality and progress, actions taken by BWDB and
contractors, and any relevant information that would ensure the
required construction standards and progress;
(f) prepare monthly progress reports for each polder. These reports
would include as a minimum, details of physical and financial
progress, the preconstruction activities, status of reimbursement
claims;
(g) check and certify progress payment claims and the final payment and
certifying reimbursement claims; and
(h) check work during the maintenance period and recommendation to BWDB
on the issue of the maintenance certificate and refund of the
security deposit.
10.       During the pre-construction phase of the minor civil works program,
the consultants staff of the unit in headquarters, and the field offices of
the chief construction engineer, the regional resident engineers, and the site
would, in addition to their responsibilities for the major civil works, carry
out the required pre-construction activities. During the construction period
the consultants staff at the site level (particularly the grade of technical
officers) would be increased sufficiently to ensure effective supervision,
The consultant would also during the pre-construction phase prepare a proposal
for the mobilization of his site staff, detailing the grades of staff, their
qualifications and experience and their responsibilities. This program would
be submitted to BWDB for review and discussion at least 6 months prior to the
start of construction. The agreed proposal would be implemented with staff
posted to the sites at least one month prior to the commencement of
construction.
11.       Imvroved O&M.  Past experience indicates that progressive
deterioration of embankments due to poor maintenance aggravated the collapse
of embankments during cyclonic and monsoon storms. Although BWDB has adequate
staff to carry out operational activities, they are not capable at present of
identifying and supervising maintenance activities which are critical to a
sustainable system. It is reported that in addition to budgetary constraints,
the lack of motivation and training in maintenance activities are the main



- 126 -
ANNEX 6
Page 8 of 26
reasons for the present unsatisfactory situation. The project's main focus
would be to introduce a system that would include routine or preventive
maintenance carried out throughout the year and periodic maintenance
(proposed once every two years) carried out during the dry season. In
addition, during the project period, funds are provided for repairs to damages
caused by floods and cyclonic storms of a scale expected to occur on average
up to once every five years. The Government's contribution to the repair of
such damage could be provided when a disaster occurs. It is expected that
with afforestation as the main protection measure, once the trees have grown
sufficiently, damage due to disasters would be minimized and for normal annual
cyclonic and monsoon storms, maintenance costs would be drastically reduced
and eliminated if routine maintenance is carried out effectively. Activities
for an effective program for improved O&M are described in detail in Annex 5.
Consultant's role would be to assist the staff of the implementing agencies,
and, with major input from participating NGOs, to motivate and guide the
elected members of the Union Parishad and the benefitted community, in
carrying out maintenance activities. Consultant would be required to;
(a) prepare a simplified manual for the operational staff of BWDB.
This would include activities to be performed and responsibilities;
(b) review estimates of O&M quantities and costs for each polder and
update as required. This may require a work study to determine
reaches that could be effectively carried out by a patrol laborer
for operational activities and by a laborer for routine
maintenance.  Consultant would also monitor short reaches of the
drainage canals to determine the quantity of silt and the frequency
of desilting operations;
(c) assist the implementing agencies in effectively running the routine
maintenance program, using settlers as described elsewhere in this
report. During implementation consultant would identify problems
in running the proposed program, specifically the performance of
families, their capacity to effectively maintain the allocated
sections, and requirements for additional training or supervision
and suggest changes as needed;
(d) assist BWDB and NGOs in supervision of periodic maintenance of
rehabilitated embankments, periodic and routine maintenance of
embankments not rehabilitated under the project and periodic and
routine maintenance of structures and the drainage network, which
would be carried out by community groups or on small scale
contracts awarded under LCB procedures. Consultant would identify
and cost separately routine and periodic maintenance activities and
the frequency of such operations. The proposed program for such
works should be completed before the end of FY98, and changes as
needed and updating would be carried out during the maintenance
period;



- 127 -
ANNEX 6
Page 9 of 26
(e) assist in the training of NGOs to supervise routine maintenance
activities and associate with NGOs in the certification process
proposed for payments to families by BWDB; and
(f) assist in damage assessment after flood and cyclonic storm
disasters, and prepare programs with detail engineering
documentation for implementation;
(g) prepare annual work programs for each polder and a composite
program for all polders in the Mid-Term Plan;
(h) establish a management information system (MIS) at each regional
office of the consultant and the Project Management Unit at
headquarters which would also be the headquarters's office of the
consultant. The purpose of the MIS is for monitoring of physical
and financial progress, and recording of relevant data needed for
carrying out the evaluation study; and
(i) give the necessary support to ensure that community participation
in O&M is ensured and effective. This would involve working
closely with NGOs and the elected members of the local government.
Particularly the consultant would be involved in the formation of
landless and destitute groups to undertake maintenance activities.
12.       Afforestation Development is critical to the security of the
embankment. It is cost effective and replaces the high cost option of block
revetment works which in the past have not been effective. Afforestation of
the embankment and foreshore is included in the program. The planting of
selected species of trees on the slopes of the embankment would commence after
the completion of earthworks in resectioned and new embankments. Efforts are
being made to organize on a pilot basis planting on embankments in a few PWP
polders (40 ha) and on the foreshores (80 ha). Forestry models have been
prepared. Initial nursery development would be carried out in FY96. It is
hoped that the consultant for PWP, with sub-contracted NGOs, would implement
the pilot schemes and nursery development in the second half of 1995. This
would provide an opportunity to ascertain the communities interest in the
afforestation component within a social forestry concept. The specialist
forestry team of the consultant would review the forestry models and, if
possible, in the context of experience gained from the pilot schemes and their
site experience, recommend changes to ensure effective development of
afforestation. Specifically the forestry team of the consultant would be
required to carry out the following responsibilities:
(a)  For the embankment afforestation, the forestry team would:
(i) review proposals for afforestation in the context of technical
and organizational feasibility and recommend changes as needed to
the proposed plan; (ii) make a firm schedule for afforestation of
the ten PWP polders; and (iii) review the construction schedule for
resectioning and new embankment construction and recommend changes
as needed to the afforestation schedule (Annex 4 of the Staff
Appraisal Report), updating this as construction work progresses.



- 128 -
ANNEX 6
Page 10 of 26
As embankment afforestation is to be carried out by families
(Annexes 3, 4, and 5 of the Staff Appraisal Report), the schedule
for planting would depend on the resettlement program. Given the
need to forest the slopes at the earliest opportunity to protect
the embankment from erosion, and to generate income for resettled
families prior to the physical resettlement, the selected families
should be mobilized as soon as possible to carry out planting;
(b) It is proposed to plant vetiver grass along the edges of the crest
of the embankment in a pilot area, and depending upon the results
to select vetiver or other hedge row species for use in the general
model. Longer term experiments would determine whether the canopy
would allow sufficient sunlight to penetrate to permit use of
vetiver on other portions of the embankment. The forestry team
would supervise these experiments and develop appropriate programs
based on the results of these experiments;
(c) for the foreshore afforestation, the forestry team would, using the
foreshore topographical, tidal and forestry polder maps prepared by
BWDB and Forest Department (FD) and, after site visits, review the
foreshore afforestation proposals in association with the FD and
NGOs. Changes as needed would be proposed in the implementation
schedule. During the maintenance phase, the consultants would also
observe conditions and make recommendations as appropriate;
(d) in order to prepare detailed plans for the planting of individual
embankment and foreshore sites, NGOs would undertake a
participative rural appraisal (PRA) exercise in each polder. The
consultants would assist in planning and coordinating this PRA and
will actively participate in the field work. The consultants would
review the PRA reports for each polder and assist NGOs, FD and BWDB
in formulation of detailed afforestation plans;
(e) The consultants would assess the seedling requirements for each
polder and identify existing sources of seedling supply. Together
with the FD and NGOs, they would plan the development of any
additional nurseries that are required with, as far as possible,
responsibility for nursery operation (other than for mangroves)
being given to NGOs who would assist landless people to establish
nursery operations. The consultants would also advise on
appropriate nursery methods and identify sources of improved tree
seed.
(f) The consultant's forestry team would identify training needs in
forestry for NGO staff and project participants. Together with the
consultant's training coordinator, they would specify the content
of training courses and help select appropriate training personnel.
(g) The consultants would review applications for disbursement for
afforestation and certify the quantity and quality of work
performed.



- 129 -
ANNEX 6
Page 11 of 26
13.       Community Involvement.  The main facilitator in involving the
community in afforestation and improved O&M would be NGOs operating at the
polder level. Given the diversity of activities in which NGOs will be
involved (mobilization, organization of groups and individuals for O&M,
afforestation and tree maintenance) and the diversity of coastal areas and
conditions, it is anticipated that BWDB and FD would each sign a separate
contract for each polder for BWDB's responsibilities for embankment
maintenance and afforestation and FD's responsibilities for foreshore
afforestation and assistance in nursery establishment. This implies that up
to 42 different NGOs could be involved, although FD and BWDB would attempt to
the extent possible to select the same NGO in a particular polder.
Standardization of contracts and coordination will be required to guarantee
quality and continuity of services provided. The consultant's rural
development team, with inputs from the engineering team, would provide close
support for these NGOs, as well as assisting the implementing agencies in
community development efforts. Specifically, the consultant would be required
to:
(a) assist in the selection and contracting of NGOs to participate in
project implementation. In discussion with BWDB, FD and
prospective NGOs, refine the scope of activities and
responsibilities. Review and make recommendations on applications
from NGOs, and on the criteria used to select NGOs.
(b) coordinate and assist NGOs in planning and carrying out a
participative rural appraisal (PRA) exercise in each polder to plan
in detail the implementation of the afforestation component.
Advise on the methodology for PRA, and on how social and cultural
factors can be assessed and incorporated into the afforestation
plan. Participate in as many PRA teams as manpower allows.
(c) review the results of the PRA, and assist NGOs in developing a
programme to support afforestation, O&M and other activities.
Together with NGOs draw up a detailed work plan covering all
aspects of NGOs' activities on individual polders, and including
cost budgets. Assist in establishing community groups to carry out
periodic maintenance activities and foreshore afforestation.
(d) help NGOs prepare proposals for activities to be financed from a
Community Development Fund (CDF) to be established in association
with the project, monitor the impact of the fund and make
recommendations concerning future disbursements. This fund is
intended to provide resources to implement supporting poverty
alleviation programs aimed at strengthening project activities and
group cohesion. This may involve water supplies and sanitary
facilities for embankment settlers, training and education
programmes, and income generating activities. The latter could in
particular aim at processing and marketing of project outputs such
as coconuts.



- 130 -
ANNEX 6
Page 12 of 26
(e) monitor the progress of: (i) land acquisition and the payment of
compensation to project affected persons; and (ii) the resettlement
of embankment squatters at the toe of the embankment.
(f) identify training requirements for NGO staff, and assist in the
training of NGOs in project planning, community mobilization and
group formation activities;
(g) monitor and coordinate NGO activities and assist in their relations
with BWDB and FD. The consultant will establish a monitoring
system involving a simple monthly reporting form for individual
polders linked to a computerized MIS at the zonal or headquarters
level. This will record NGO activities, measure progress against
agreed workplans, and identify constraints and bottlenecks. If an
NGO fails to perform satisfactorily, then the consultant will
recommend remedial measures which may include termination of its
contract.
14.       Agricultural Development. The consultants would aim to facilitate
changes in farming practice to take advantages of the improved conditions
created by the construction of project embankments. They would liaise with
the Agricultural Support Services Project (ASSP) and other programs in the
area working on improving agricultural production. In the first year of
implementation an in depth study of the project area would be carried out and,
in consultation with the specialists in the ASSP unit, a detailed proposal for
assisting farmers in the area to take advantage of new opportunities resulting
from the project would be prepared. The program would include the work to be
done during project years two to four, and may include adaptive research
trials and changes to extension messages. The consultants would supervise
implementation of the programme during this period.
15.       Monitoring and Impact Assessment.  Engineering, agricultural,
socio-economic and environmental aspects would be investigated with a view to
assessing the project's impact, identifying any issues and introducing any
changes (specifically for community involvement in O&M and afforestation)
during the implementation phase. This would also be useful in the design of
future projects. Specifically, the consultant would be required to carry out
the following responsibilities:
(a) Environmental assessment: the consultant would work with those
carrying out environmental monitoring activities under the ASSP and
the National Minor Irrigation Development Project to ensure that
their activities are appropriate to meet the needs of this project.
The consultants will review the findings of these and other
environmental monitoring activities and recommend any further
monitoring or mitigation measures that may be appropriate.
(b) Civil works: the monitoring of the civil works program and all
aspects of O&M are adequately covered under contract management and
supervision and improved O&M (paras 6 through 9 & para 12).



- 131 -
ANNEX 6
Page 13 of 26
(c) Impact assessment: The bench mark studies of seventeen polders
under the CERP and PWP have been completed under the on going
System Rehabilitation Project. The conditions in the studied
polders are similar to conditions in three polders where no studies
have been done. No study was done for Patenga (polder 62) where
the conditions are not similar to the other polders. The
consultants shall review the completed studies, and collect any
additional information needed from other secondary sources and from
direct field investigations. Given the importance of the impact of
Patenga in protecting public and private properties, it would be
necessary for the consultants to carry out a bench mark survey. In
addition, consultants would liaise with the resettlement
consultants to ensure adequate monitoring of the socio-economic
status of people losing property, housing, business or employment
as a result of the project.
(d) The consultant would then undertake sample surveys in order to
assess the effectiveness of project activities and their impact on:
(i) agriculture, farm incomes, farm level employment for different
categories of people (land owners, share croppers, landless,
destitute women); (ii) income of people involved in embankment
maintenance activities, and in embankment and foreshore
afforestation; and (iii) beneficiaries of activities funded by the
Community Development Fund. Surveys will also aim to get an
insight into: (i) any problems of squatters and unauthorized
cultivation of embankment slopes; and (ii) access by all sections
of the community (particularly women) to the participative
decision-making process within project groups and local
organizations.
(e) Questionnaire surveys would cover about 60 to 100 respondents per
polder, with the objective of making statistically valid
comparisons between polders or between groups of beneficiaries
(such as settlers, farmers, and earthwork laborers). If about 10
to 12 polders were covered by a survey (i.e. all PWP or all CERP
polders), then a survey would involve about 1000 respondents. It
is suggested that four such surveys are done during the project
period, with possibly three or four smaller ones (with 200 to 400
respondents) focusing more narrowly on key issues.
16.       SDecialists InDuts for SandwiD Town Protection Studies.  In
Sandwip, Polder 72, the proposed alignment for the retired embankment will not
provide protection for the town. This reflects substantial erosion which
threatens to destroy not only the existing embankment but also much of the
current area of the town. In view of this, provision is made for carrying out
studies to identify possible cost effective town protection methods. These
studies would be carried out by BWDB with assistance from the project
consultants. The consultant's responsibilities would include:
(i) preparation of a terms of reference which would involve site
investigations and surveys, modelling at the river research institute, and
estimates of the specialist staff months required and the duration and cost of



- 132 -
ANNEX 6
Page 14 of 26
the study; (ii) specialist inputs for supervision, and technical guidance
during the study; and (iii) assistance to BWDB in preparing detailed proposals
(engineering designs and cost estimates) for implementation of the study's
recommendations.
Orcaanizational Set Up. Composition of Team and Period of Service
17.       The Headquarters's consultant team would be located in the Project
Implementation Unit's office in Chittagong, with three regional offices, four
divisional offices, and site offices at each polder. The consultant's
engineering team would be headed by the team leader, the zonal offices by
chief construction engineers, the regional offices by resident engineers and
the site offices by assistant resident engineers. The site offices would be
staffed by technical officers, with technicians to supervise construction work
and work in the field laboratories. The field laboratories would be fully
equipped with testing equipment with funds provided under the project. Where
major civil works are being executed on contracts awarded following ICB
procedures, the site offices at each polder, field laboratories, and quarters
for consultant's field staff would be provided by the contractor. The
contractor shall also provide residential buildings for the use of the
regional chief construction engineer. In polders where only minor civil works
are being executed, office space would be provided by BWDB in their existing
buildings.
18.       The team would include specialists in construction and contract
management, sociology, forestry, environmental science and agriculture. There
would be mix of internationally recruited and locally recruited personnel in
the senior management positions. All positions at the divisional and site
offices would be limited to local recruitment. It is estimated that 326 staff
months of internationally recruited and 736 staff months of locally recruited
senior specialists, 568 staff months of locally recruited junior specialists
and 1172 staff months of locally recruited technical staff would be required.
The consultant would also: (i) recruit the required field and support staff;
(ii) procure vehicles (including those for use by NGOs), boats, computers and
office equipment; and (iii) provide for incidentals such as running costs of
vehicles, maintenance of office and field equipment, office rental including
space at Headquarters for the PIU, local travelling, subsistence allowances
whilst on field visits, and consumables required in running the office.
Consultancy services are estimated to commence in early 1996 and continue June
30, 2002. Total requirements of senior and mid level staff are estimated as
follows:

Reporting Reauirements
20.       The consultant would submit the following reports:
(a)  an inception report within two months after the start of the
services. The report shall include (i) comments on the terms of
reference and any proposed changes; (ii) proposals and work plans
for each activity, including inputs to the training program; (iii)
any issues that have been identified during the reconstruction
works of the PWP and suggestions for any changes in the approach to
similar works in CERP; (iv) comments on the benchmark studies of
the polders of the Medium Term Plan carried out under the SRP; and
(v) the program for deployment of staff;
(b)  brief monthly progress reports and detailed quarterly reports
including separate annex for each major civil work's contract
awarded following ICB procedures;
(c)  a completion report for each polder including as built plans;
(d)  annual reports on the impact assessment study and a final report,
two months prior to completion of the assignment;
(e)  annual reports on social and agriculture development inclusive of
the progress of community participation in O&M activities; and
(f)  reports as appropriate on the environmental impact of the project
and a final report at the end of the assignment.
21.       The inception reports and all final reports shall be produced in
draft and final forms (20 copies of draft and 50 copies of final).  The
completion report of polders could be summarized in a main report with
annexure for each polder giving the polder data and the as built plans (20
copies).
22.       Specialists Inputs to the TraininQ Proaram.  The services of the
internationally recruited specialists particularly the services of the senior
engineering professionals, the community development adviser, and the forester
would be used for the design of training programs including curriculum and
classroom training etc. The approach is considered as the least cost option
whilst ensuring a practical approach to training. The training coordinator
would work under the direction of consultant's team leader, which would ensure

that the services of specialists of the implementation unit are available for
the agreed schedule of the training program. Aside from the consultants'
working on both project support and training, the staff months for the
training program are costed separately. (See Annex 7).
