A. Encineerinc
Initial Team Leader (57 months)
1.        The Initial Team Leader should have a civil engineering degree with
a chartership in at least one internationally recognized civil engineering
institute. As the project is multi-disciplinary, besides having extensive
experience in contract management of major civil engineering works involving a
mix of labor and mechanical equipment, he should have a minimum of 20 years
experience with at least a five year period as the Team Leader of a project
involving major construction, O&M particularly where community participation
was heavily involved, and social forestry. He should have proven experience
in leading a multi-disciplinary team of specialists, including guidance and
advice in training activities similar to that proposed under the project.
Works proposed under the project are widely scattered and extensive sea and
road traveling would be involved. The candidate selected for the post should
have worked for a minimum of three years in a country with similar conditions
as Bangladesh. Whilst it is preferred that candidates for the post would be
between 40 and 60 years of age. Highly qualified candidates over the age of
60 years would be accepted if the application is accompanied by physical and
medical clearance certificate including certification of fitness to undertake
extensive traveling. Specifically his responsibilities would include:
(a) under the direction of the Project Director (PIU), the overall
control of all engineering aspects of the project, and provision of
assistance as required to the Project Director and Project Director
Afforestation in all activities related to social forestry,
community participation in O&M, and NGO participation in all
project activities. He would also be responsible for coordinating
the activities of all the internationally and locally recruited
senior specialists;
(b) overall control of consultant staff in all administrative and
financial aspects in the PIU, the region, division, and polder;
(c) preparation of tender documents for the remaining works for 2 PWP
polders to be awarded following ICB procedures and provision of
assistance in the tendering and award of these contracts;
(d) the preparation of guidelines for use by consultant staff including
checking its implementation, to ensure effective contract
supervision particularly in respect of adherence to engineering
specifications and general condition of the major civil works
contracts;
(e) assisted by the regional consultant staff, organizing a practical
and workable field supervision set up involving staff of the BWDB

Chief Resident Engineers (3 nos for a total of 102 months)
2.        The Chief Resident Engineer should possess a degree in civil
engineering with a minimum of 15 years construction experience of which five
years should be as the contract manager of a major civil engineering project.
He should also possess a chartership in at least one of the recognized civil
engineering institutes. Under the direction of the Team Leader, his specific
responsibilities would include;
(a) the setting up of adequate and suitable site supervision staff and

(b) follow-up on the responsibilities of the contractor on the pre-work
surveys, and ensure that the plans are submitted in a timely manner
and that no work commences without prior authorization by him;
(c) review the contractor's superintendence and key personnel, plant
and equipment, work proposal, program, working drawings and in
consultation with the Team leader approve or suggest modifications,
and amend as required. During construction request the contractor
to revise the program to account for the current status of the
work;
(d) order the removal and replacement of improper materials and work,
and check and approve the necessary measurements and calculations
for the contractor's interim running bills and make recommendations
to the Team Leaser on the certifications of these bills;
(e) examine at site the measurement of any work which is about to be
covered up or put out of view, before permanent work is place

work;
(f) guide the consultant field staff in the provision of the required
information for the contractor to set out the works and carry out
spot checks to establish the accuracy of the setting out whilst the
consultant's site staff supervise fully the contractor's setting
out. Establish in consultation with the Team Leader, a roster for
the site supervisors and ensure that supervisors are present at
site during construction;

B. Rural Development
Community DeveloDment Advisor/Staae II Team Leader (72 months)
4.        The Community Development Advisor should have wide experience of
participatory rural development projects, and of working with NGOs as well as
government departments. The Advisor would be expected to learn Bangali within
the six months of arriving in Bangladesh. Specific tasks include:
(a) under the overall direction of the consultant's team leader, act as
leader of the non-engineering rural development section of the
consultant's team. Organize and supervise the work of other team
members.
(b) assist in the selection and contracting of NGOs to participate in
project implementation. In discussion with BWDB, FD and
prospective NGOs, refine the scope of activities and the extent of

to select NGOs. Assist in drafting contracts for between NGOs,
BWDB and FD. - note: much of this activity may take Dlace in 1995
before the arrival of the mobilization of the consultants. and
therefore would be carried out by the existing PWP consultants.
(c) coordinate and assist NGOs in planning and carrying out a
participative rural appraisal (PRA) exercise in each polder to plan
in detail the implementation of the afforestation component.
Advise on the methodology for PRA, and on how social and cultural
factors can be assessed and incorporated into the afforestation
plan (this could involve a training workshop). Organize as much
active participation in PRA teams by members of the consultancy
team as is possible within manpower constraints.
(d) review the results of the PRA, and assist NGOs in developing a
programme to support afforestation, O&M and other activities.
Together with NGOs draw up a detailed work plan covering all

(e) draw up guidelines for use of the Community Development Fund, and

be financed from this fund. Monitor the impact of the fund and

(f) monitor the progress of the land acquisition and resettlement
programmes, and make recommendations to remedy any deficiencies in
procedures or policies.

Social Forester (36 months)
5.        The Social Forester will have extensive experience in community

progresses.
(b) draw up plans for, and analyze the outcome of, pilot planting of

of embankments;
(c) review the foreshore afforestation proposals, and recommend any

Environmental Specialist (12 months)
6.        The Environmental Specialist would make two to four short visits to

recommend any further monitoring or mitigation measures that may be
appropriate. Any further monitoring that is required will be carried out by

monitoring of the socio-economic status of people losing property, housing,
business or employment as a result of the project. Specific activities
include:
(a) The MIS will compile data on the progress of engineering works, O&M

BANGLADESH
Coastal Embankment Rehabilitation Proiect
Technical Assistance for
Training of Staff and Communities in O&M and Staff Development
Background
1.          Over the last decade, BWDB has undertaken a program of training to
upgrade and prepare its staff for the different jobs in the organization. The
main thrust has been on the engineering skills of the higher and middle level
staff to effectively carry out investigations, planning, design and
construction of flood control, drainage and irrigation schemes. During this
period separate training programs funded under aided projects were carried out
without any co-ordination and directed to specific needs of the project.
Several issues were identified during the preparation of the Systems
Rehabilitation Project (SRP). The critical issue were a weak training unit at
BWDB headquarters, emphasis on class room lectures, lesser importance given to
on-site training, omission of O&M in the curriculum of training programs and
no efforts made to educate beneficiaries in the importance of effective
maintenance and the need for community participation in maintenance to ensure
sustainability of the project. Under the SRP Project (SRP), the headquarters
unit was strengthened with the appointment of the Director General Training
and Staff Development, a Deputy Director Staff Development, and a Deputy
Director Training Evaluation. In addition under the project, an O&M training
program, directed towards the middle-and lower-staff of BWDB, and
beneficiaries, has been initiated recently. The training proposal prepared by
consultants has been approved by BWDB and the training of trainers has
commenced.
2.          The proposal for financing under CERP includes the following:
Training
(a)   identifications of training needs;
(b)   training of staff trainers;
(c)   designing training courses: (i) for BWDB staff; and (ii) for
beneficiaries and maintenance groups;
(d)   producing training materials and manuals;
(e)   implementing and evaluating training courses including cost
benefit analysis;
(f)   planning and supervision of training facility improvement;



- 146 -
ANNEX 7
Page 2 of 7
Staff Develolment
(g)   preparing an inventory of present staff resources;
(h)   determining staff utilization and job descriptions;
(i)   developing comprehensive staffing plans;
(j)   designing measures for staff deployment;
3.          The training and staff development program under SRP is believed
to have provided a sufficient number of trainers in BWDB to undertake the
training of field staff of BWDB and beneficiaries. Staff development in BWDB,
with the exception of training in involving community participation during the
planning, construction and operational phases of projects, is comprehensively
covered under the SRP, and efforts in community participation training are
beginning. The training program proposed for CERP would build on the
activities of SRP with emphasis on the training of NGOs and beneficiaries and
improving the skills of BWDB staff to use the resources of the community to
effectively carry out maintenance activities. It would also include some of
the programs of staff training initiated under SRP, as adopted to the specific
needs of CERP.
Objectives
4.          The objectives of the training component are to:
(a)   improve the skills of BWDB staff in contract management and
supervision of construction work, O&M activities, and the
effective use of community resources in maintenance activities;
works;
(b)   improve the skills of Forest Department staff in coastal and
embankment afforestation;
(c)   improve the skills of NGOs in community development activities and
train them to be able to supervise routine maintenance of
embankments and in the technical aspects of afforestation
inclusive of propagation of nurseries;
(d)   train beneficiaries in maintenance activities of embankments and
afforestation; and



- 147 -
ANNEX 7
Page 3 of 7
Program
5.          The program would be mainly limited to in-country training and
would include:
(a)   a series of three regional workshops (Barisal/Khulna, Chittagong,
and Noakhali) to provide orientation and motivation for BWDB staff
to be involved in project implementation;
(b)   classroom based training of trainers in BWDB and the Forest
Department, and NGOs;
(c)   production of learning materials including O&M, community forestry
field manuals and environmental guidelines;
(d)   on-site training of project participants and beneficiaries; and
(e)   local study tours for BWDB, Forest Department, NGOs and community
group representatives participating in maintenance and
afforestation activities.
(f)   possible short study tour to Indonesia (or other Asian country)
where NGOs have been pioneering a community approach to coastal
afforestation.
Execution
6.          Training of Trainers.  BWDB staff trained under the SRP training
program would form the nucleus of the trainers to be responsible for
conducting courses, They would initially undergo a refresher course conducted
by specialist of the implementation support and training teams of CERP. With
their inputs and the inputs of specialists as required, additional BWDB staff
and selected NGO group organizers would be trained as trainers. Course
curriculum would be similar to that used for the SRP program with emphasis
directed towards preventive activities required to ensure sustainability of
the polders. Specifically training would be: directed towards ensuring the
correct approach in imparting knowledge; subject matter-oriented, need to have
on-site courses with minimum classroom courses; on the use and effectiveness
of audio-visual methods; on designing training materials; and methods for
evaluating the on-going and new training programs. The selected trainers
during the training program would assist the training coordinator in preparing
the target groups and the number of participants in each group, number of
courses, locations and duration of training. They would also assist in the
preparation of guidelines for the methodology to be adopted in evaluating the
program.
7.          Construction, Operation and Maintenance Training.  Construction
and operation training would be limited to BWDB staff. Staff of BWDB, NGOs,
and the beneficiary communities (representatives of families, and maintenance
groups) would be trained in routine and periodic maintenance activities.
Training activities would include:



1 148 -
ANNEX 7
Page 4 of 7
8.          Construction.  An intensive training program on contract
management, setting out, quality control and supervision currently in the
training program of SRP would continue to be conducted under the CERP. Upper-
and middle-level field staff of BWDB (SEs, Exens. and SDEs) would receive on-
site training in all aspects of construction, whilst SOs and Work Assistants
(WAs) would be trained in supervision, measurements and preparation of running
bills of contractors. Classroom courses would be very limited. All BWDB
staff involved in the administrative and financial management of major civil
works would be trained in this program. The specialists consultants of the
implementation support team would direct and conduct the training.
9.          Operation.  The upper-and middle-level staff of BWDB are well
experienced in operational activities. Training would be limited to SOs, WAs
and Khalashis. The responsibilities of these staff are described in great
detail in the current O&M manual. Refresher courses would be conducted under
the program. Class room lectures would be conducted in each polder followed
by instructions and demonstrations at site. The main thrust would be to
educate these staff on the need for early detection of weak sections of the
embankment, signs of deterioration in structures, the importance of the post
monsoon and pre-monsoon inspection and engineering surveys and the need for
timely repairs to ensure the security of the project works; Training would be
conducted by the program trainers and inputs by upper-and middle-level staff
of BWDB.
10.         Motivation and Orientation Workshops.  A series of one day
workshops in each of three regions would be conducted by the consultant's
community development adviser in collaboration with members of the forum of
participating NGOs to provide BWDB staff with an introduction to the concept
of community participation and working with NGOs. Senior engineers of BWDB
who have acquired some experience in community participation, working with
NGOs and landless groups for embankment maintenance, would take a key role in
these workshops.
11.         Maintenance.  BWDB, NGOS and resettled families would be involved
in routine maintenance of embankments. Periodic maintenance of embankments,
structures and the drainage network is carried out during the dry season
(frequency: annually,once every two years, etc.) and the preparation of
engineering documents, measurements for preparation of bills etc. require
special engineering skills. In view of this BWDB would be responsible for
all aspects of contract management. NGOs' responsibility for periodic
maintenance would be limited to: (i) group formation; (ii) assistance to the
group to elect a group representative to sign contracts, and contract
management including administrative and financial matters. BWDB trainers
would train groups of twenty five comprising SOs, WAs and Khalashis at three
central locations in the project area. Several such courses would be
conducted during FY96 and FY97 to cover all the SOs, WAs, and Khalshis of the
twenty one polders included in the Mid Term Plan. Separate courses would be
conducted for routine and periodic maintenance. Participating NGOs would be
included in the courses for routine maintenance. Classroom followed by on-
site training at polders in each central location would be included in the

Page 5 of 7
course. Curriculum would include all the routine and periodic activities
described in annex 5 and the job responsibilities described in the O&M manual.
12.         Training of families to carry out routine maintenance of
embankments would be conducted by NGO group organizers (trained under the
training of trainers program). Groups of about ten resettled families would
receive on-the-job training at a time. Training will consist of 2 days on-
site work, for which participants would receive payment as part of the
standard contract with BWDB.
13.         Forestry TraininQ Module.  The social forestry training module

emphasize practical hands-on experience, with development of graphic materials

would include the following elements:
(a)   A review of existing soeial forestry trainincr Droqrams being
developed by the Association of Development Agencies of Bangladesh
(ADAB) forum on forestry and environment (covering approach,
motivation, and mobilization) and the technical training module
developed by BARC, Winrock and Proshika which covers choice of
species and technical aspects of planting and management for
community forestry.
(b)   Development of a trainir course for NGO staff and the Forest
Rangers based on a four week course with weeks one and two in the
classroom, week three in the field working out the forestry model
for the embankment with beneficiaries and support of the Forest
Department ranger, week four back in the classroom.
(c)   Preparation and vroduction of a field manual for NGO field staff
which would provide pictures of the species, notes and symbols

maintenance, yield data and generalized market information.
(d)   Implementation of traininq course for two NGO staff per polder and

groups of beneficiaries and the forest ranger, the trainees would
draw up an afforestation plan for a one ha plot suitable for their
own polder (guidelines given in annex 4). This would be reviewed
by the class in the final class room session.
14.         Back up support to trained group organizers, during implementation

the implementation support consultant.
15.         Environmental Trainin .  The training module on environmental

16.         Short term sDecialized courses (2-3 weeks) in environmental and

the socio-economic implications of such projects. The course would provide
information on socio-economic survey techniques, the socio-economic impacts of
FCDI projects and the management of resettlement and compensation issues.
18.         The principal aim of these courses will be develop within senior
staff a wider awareness of water resources issues so that environmental and
social issues can be considered at all stages of FCDI projects (planning to
implementation and O&M). It is expected that this will minimize the potential
for adverse impacts to arise in future FCDI projects.
19.         A series of seminars for the junior staff of BWDB would also be
provided to build on and deepen the environmental awareness raising which
would be completed under the SRP training module. The seminars will contain
information of a similar nature to the short term courses listed above. The
environmentalist should discuss the detailed course content with appropriate
SRP and BWDB staff.

BANGLADESH
COASTAL EMBANKMENT REHABILITATION PROJECT (CERP)
Crop Production and Incomes
A. General
1.          Estimation of the project's expected impact on agricultural
production and farm incomes has been based on a detailed analysis of farming
practices and agro-ecological conditions in each of the 11 polders with major
works financed under the project. Each of the polders has been considered as
a sub-project. The analysis, based on a one hectare farm model, has been done
separately for each polder because of differences such as management
practices, intensity of input use, soil quality, yields and cropping patterns
between polders in the area covered by the project. Prevention of salinity
intrusions and storm surges and improved drainage conditions in the polders
during normal monsoons and cyclones would lead to increased agricultural
production. Higher output would come from changes in cropping patterns and
intensity and improved crop yields. In projecting the impact of the project
on agricultural production, account is taken of the small amount of land lost
to production due to project related land acquisition in areas where
embankment retirement is necessary. Account is also taken of further
retirement of sections of the embankments in Polders 59/2 (Ramgati) and 72
(Sandwip) expected to be required in year 15 due to erosion.
B. Cropping Patterns and Intensity
3.          The predominant crop in the area covered by the project is the low
yielding local variety (Lv) of T. Aman. B. Aus (LV) is the second major crop
in the area. B. Aman is also important in some of the polders. A range of
other crops such as pulses, oilseeds, millet, cowpea, chilli, sweet potatoes,
jute, and vegetables are grown during the rabi (winter) season. Adoption of
high yielding varieties (HYVs) of rice is limited in these polders because of
high soil and water salinity as these varieties have little or no tolerance to
salinity. All current HYV production is expected to switch to local varieties
within two years in the absence of the project given the present condition of
the embankments. Rehabilitation of the embankments would prevent salinity
intrusions and storm surges and thus bring about changes in the cropping
pattern. The present and expected cropping patterns in the "with project" and
"without project" scenarios are detailed polder by polder in Tables 1-11.
With protection against saline water and improvement of drainage conditions,
increased cultivation of HYVs of rice is expected to occur. The increased
security to be provided by the rehabilitated embankments would encourage the
investment in higher input levels required for HYV production. With the
project, between 20 and 40% of T. Aman area is expected to shift to HYV in the
kharif season 1, depending upon the degree of sharecropping in each polder,
since the incentives to sharecroppers to shift to HYV are much diminished.
1.    Summer cropping season (May through November).



- 153 -                            ANNEX 8
Page 2 of 3
During the rabi season 2, HYV boro cultivation is expected to increase only
in Polder 66/1 where fresh underground water is available for irrigation.
Cultivation of HYVs of other rabi crops such as water melon and betel leaf
would also increase in the "with project" situation, especially in the polders
in Cox's Bazar area such as Polder 66/1 and Polder 68. It is also expected
that more land would come under diversified crops (pulses, oilseeds, chillies,
winter vegetables, and sweet potatoes) in the rabi season due to reduced soil
salinity. Changes in the cropping pattern are expected to be realized over a
period of seven years.
4.          Cropping intensity in the project area under the present
conditions ranges from 108% (Polder 35/1) to 174% (Polder 72), the average
being 136t. The national average is about 150%. Saline soil conditions limit
crop cultivation during the dry season. The project is expected to increase
cropping intensity in the area to the national average because of increased
area under both kharif and rabi crops. The area under kharif crops would
increase due to protection from flooding as well as improved drainage while
the area under rabi crops would increase as a result of soil salinity
reduction. Cropping intensity is expected to range from about 130% (Polder
73/1A&B) to 210% (Polder 66/1), the average being 155%, in the "with project"
scenario.
5.          Aside from the yield gains from switching to HYV and increased
cropping intensity, yield per season for the same crops would also increase,
due to reduction in salinity, improvement in drainage conditions during the
kharif season, and protection against flooding caused by heavy rainfall during
normal monsoons or cyclone surges. An increase of 10-15% in yield is
expected, the level depending on crop varieties and specific conditions in a
given polder. In the absence of the project, yields for most crops would
remain low or decline to the levels observed outside the embankments. Due to
the effects of salinity intrusions through existing breaches in the
embankments or areas where breaches are imminent, and poor drainage and
flooding, it is anticipated that in the "without project" situation the
average yield of local varieties of rice would be reduced over a period of
five years to about one ton per hectare, which is the average yield of T. Aman
under saline conditions. Anticipated changes in crop yields under "with
project" and "without project" compared with the present conditions are also
detailed in Tables 1-12.
C. Crop Production
6.          Expected annual crop production under present, "without project"
and "with project" situations are also detailed in Tables 1-11 by polder.
Estimated annual incremental crop production is expected to range from about
20% (Polder 70) to 110% (Polder 40/2), the average being about 55%. Annual
production, at full development, with the project is expected to increase by
about 87,700 metric tons on average over the "without project" situation.
This production estimate is based on a net cultivable land of about 49,900
hectares in the polders protected by the 11 embankments included under the
major works program and excludes the cultivable land protected under the
2.    Winter cropping season (November through May).



-154-                            ANNEX 8
Page 3 of 3
Priority Works Project or by the two Km of embankment improvements provided
for polder 60.3 The expected increase in production would be higher if not
for the constraints that the farmers will still face. These constraints
include limited fresh water for irrigation during the dry season, unfavorable
tenancy agreements4 that discourage tenant farmers from investing in land
improvement and intensive cultivation, existence of absentee/large land owners
who do not pay much attention to crop intensification, and poor quality of
draught animals and shortage of mechanical cultivators for land preparation.
These constraints have been taken into account when projecting the increase in
crop production. The target production is expected to be achieved in seven
years' time when the projected changes in cropping pattern and intensity will
have been realized.
D. Farm Incomes
7.          The project's effect on annual farm incomes in the area has been
analyzed through the use of one hectare farm models prepared for each of the
11 polders. Prices of all inputs and outputs are based on constant 1994
farmgate prices (see Annex 11, Tables 1 and 2). Details on these prices are
discussed in Annex 11, para 1 (c). The cash flows are in constant 1991 taka.
Farm budgets detailing input use, costs of production, gross value of
production, and net income per hectare for each polder are shown in Tables 12-
23. The results show that investment in the project would increase incomes of
farm families in the area substantially. The average net increase in income
from one hectare farms would range from about 20% (Polder 59/2) to 110l
(Polder 35/1). In value terms, the range would be from about Taka 2,100
(Polder 73/lA&B) to Taka 11,300 (Polder 66/1). The average increase in net
income from a one hectare farm for all the polders would be about Taka 5,200
or 47%. The increase is expected to be higher in polders in Cox's Bazar
district, where intensive cropping is practiced, than in polders in Burguna or
Bagerhat districts. The additional farm incomes would also benefit tenants
and landless laborers, in addition to benefiting owner-operators. The
percentage share of the benefits is not expected to differ substantially from
the present arrangements. Everybody would gain in proportion to present
income levels.
3.    Polder 60, where project civil works include only 2 Km of embankment
strengthening plus khal excavation, is excluded from the analysis. More
time is required to test the effectiveness of recently completed anti-
erosion works before firm estimates can be justified.
4.    Thirty to forty percent of rural households operate land under different
tenancy arrangements, the most widespread being sharecropping under which
the tenant agrees to bear all costs and pays 50% of gross output to the
landlord. The agreement is usually for one crop season.

Land Acquisition and Resettlement
Background
1.    There are two major categories of Project Affected Persons (PAPs) under the
proposed project:
(a)   Squatters living on existing embankments being repaired under the
project; and
(b)   People affected by land acquisition for project facilities.
About 675 ha is to be acquired under the project consisting of (a) about 270 ha of land in
areas where embankments have to be retired due to the effects of erosion on existing
embankments; (b) about 175 ha for repair of existing embankments, of which about half
is for acquisition of land under one existing embankment where the land had previously
been made available on a voluntary basis, without transfer of ownership, and the
remainder is land required for widening of existing embankments; (c) about 55 ha for
new drainage structures; (d) about 35 ha for minor works (excluding khal excavation);
(e) about 35 ha for resettlement of embankment squatters and other landless people; and
(f) about 105 ha to be leased for three years in strips of 10 meters width on each bank of
kahls, as spoil dump areas for khal excavation.
2.    Paras. 2.12 and 2.16 of the SAR describes the program under which about 700
families presently squatting on embankment sections to be rehabilitated and about 1300
other landless families would be allocated an area of the embankment of about 1 ha each
(smaller areas in sections of two polders) for which they would be responsible for
afforestation and routine embankment maintenance. Each of these families would also be
allocated a homestead plot of 10 m. by 20 m. at the inland toe of the embankment. They
would be paid by BWDB on a declining scale for these services for about four years by
which time the value of the produce from their trees and their homestead plots is expected
to be sufficient to provide an adequate income. It is expected that most of the squatters
required to move to permit rehabilitation of embankments would opt to move
immediately to new homes at the back of the homestead plots, which would still allow
contractors enough room on the berm for construction activities. Alternatively, they
would be given the option of a rental allowance for about one year to cover the costs of
temporarily renting a plot or a house during construction. Squatters would be provided a
house construction grant sufficient to cover the costs of movement and erection of
shiftable materials from the prior house and purchase and erection of non-shiftable
materials for the new house. A squatter census report was produced in August 1993 by
the consultants employed for the PWP project. The resettlement consultants, assisted by
NGOs, would formally register the eligible PAPs, and would include any new squatters
up to the date of the new survey.



- 180 -                            ANNEX9
Page 2 of 6
3.    On about 3.7 km of embankment near Sandwip town and about 4 km near
Ramgati a variation of the above arrangements would be made for squatter families with
work or family connections requiring them to live near town. A continuous berm would
be constructed to accommodate about 1 00 families near Sandwip and 80 families near
Ramgati. Groups would be organized to take responsibility for embankment afforestation
and maintenance, with each family responsible for the maintenance and receiving the
benefit of the produce from a smaller area than in the standard model (perhaps 1/4 to 1/8
ha). This would not be expected to provide a subsistence income but (together with the
produce from the homestead plot) would supplement their current sources of income.
Impact of Land Acquisition
4.    Land acquisition in Bangladesh in the past has caused severe adverse impacts on
affected people, for the following reasons:
(a)   the level of compensation for land has generally been far below the market
value of the lost properties;
(b)   Compensation has frequently been delayed;
(c)   Because of the small average land holdings in Bangladesh, people are
extremely vulnerable to losses of productive land; and
(d)   Some categories of affected people have not been compensated.
5.   In compliance with the principles of the Bank's Operational Directive 4.30, a
resettlement action plan (RAP) has been prepared for the project, which addresses the
above-mentioned problems. The main challenges of this plan have been to: (a) develop
an improved mechanism for valuation of land; (b) develop policies which adequately
address the need for restoring income levels of agriculture-based households; (c) identify
and define non-property owners in need of assistance; and (d) provide an adequate and
fair grievance mechanism.
6.    A socio-economic survey has been carried out by consultants (Bangladesh
Institute of Development Studies staff members) of the land to be acquired for
embankment retirement. This study covered a total of 236 ha of land required as
compared to about 270 now estimated to be required for embankment retirement, taking
account of changes in alignment due to erosion and the addition of a section of one polder
not completed under the PWP program. This survey aimed to:
(i)   produce a comprehensive listing of all PAPs;
(ii)   ascertain the socio-economic status of PAPs;
(iii) make a complete list of all losses suffered by PAPs;



- 181 -                          ANNEX9
Page 3 of 6
(iv)  gather data on the prevailing market price of land;
(v)   elicit PAPs' views on compensation levels and modalities.
7.    Data from the complete census of PAPs has been entered into a computer data-
base and two listings produced for each of the 10 polders. One of these contains tables
showing informnation on each plot, referenced by the plot number from the mouza map.
The second contains a data sheet for each individual PAP. Although the information in
these print-outs is quite comprehensive, further information would need to be accessed
directly from the data-base. For instance, although PAP data sheets show the value of
losses of assets, such as houses and trees, information on the type, size and unit value of
each house or tree (which is needed to determine the overall value of the asset) is not
shown. Plot data tables only give information on the land owner, and details of tenants
can only be obtained from the computer.
8.    To retire a total of 65 km of embankment in 10 CERP polders, a total of 236 ha of
land will need to be acquired. Of this, 27 ha is khas (public) land, the remaining 209 ha
consisting of 2,448 plots of private land owned or occupied by 1 ,148 households. On
average the affected households lose 0.18 ha or about 1 1% of their land holdings. In
many cases the value and productivity of their remaining land would increase due to the
protection provided by the embankment. The total number of families rendered landless
due to this land acquisition would be four (all earning their livelihood outside of
agriculture). In addition to agricultural land, about 12% of PAPs would lose homestead
land, 11% buildings, 2% shops or mills, 16% trees, 3% fishponds, and 1% salt beds.
There is little sharecropping in the project area, but there are some longer term tenants.
9.    The remaining land to be acquired under the project is similar to that surveyed
above, except that in most cases the amount of land to be taken from any one PAP (e.g.
for widening existing embankments) would be less.
Compensation Policy
10.   The policies and mechanisms developed in the RAP are primarily cash-based,
with the exception of support for relocation of squatters (described in para 2 above). It is
recognized that land-for-land compensation is not feasible nor desirable with the
acquisition of long strips of land (right-of-way). Most farmers will lose only parts of
their holdings and will prefer not to move, and cannot make use of land allocated by the
Government somewhere else. The primary focus of the policy is therefore on adequate
cash compensation. Within this framework, GOB has agreed to: (a) offer compensation
for properties/assets based on their replacement value; (b) include compensation and
assistance to affected non-property owners; (c) compensate for relocation costs; and
(d) provide compensation for temporary loss of income. These provisions meet the
requirements of Operational Directive 4.30 with respect to resettlement and rehabilitation.



- 182 -ANNEX9
Page 4 of 6
11.   The following types of losses are recognized under the compensation and
resettlement policy:
(a)   agricultural or commercial land, including usufruct rights existing for
more than one year;
(b)   homestead land;
(c)   standing crop;
(d)   living quarters or other physical structures (including buildings used for
commercial or industrial activities);
(e)   trees or fish ponds;
(f)   income from displaced commercial or industrial activities;
(g)   regular wage income; and
(h)   occupied homestead land (either unauthorized use of Government land or
use of private land with owner's permission).
12.   Definition of the persons recognized as having these losses (i.e., entitled persons),
level/form of compensation and assistance offered, and specific guidelines for calculating
compensation are presented in the compensation and resettlement policy in the RAP (see
attachment.) The policy commitments and institutional arrangements reflected in this
policy matrix will be translated into Bangla and distributed to the affected people during
joint verification. In addition, local level information meetings will be arranged where
the text of the Bangla brochure will be explained publicly, both to the PAPs and to the
implementing functionaries at the field level. The concerned BWDB staff will request
the Union Council Chairmen to convene such meetings.
Institutional Arrangements
13.   Land and other properties will be acquired under the Acquisition and Requisition
of Immovable Property Ordinance, 1982. Under this Ordinance, the Deputy
Commissioner's (DC's) office is the acquiring body acting on behalf of the implementing
agency (in this case BWDB). The ordinance specifies that in determining the market
value of property, the DC shall take into account the average value of similar properties
in the vicinity sold during the preceding twelve months. Since such values are frequently
understated for tax avoidance reasons, the compensation values resulting from these
computations of recorded market prices were normally below the real market values of
the property. In consideration of this problems, the Ordinance was amended in
September 1993 to raise the premium on the calculated market value of property "in
consideration of the compulsory nature of the acquisition" from 20% to 50%.
Notwithstanding the increase in the premiums over recorded values, compensation for
property based on computed values could still, in some cases, be less than replacement
value. In addition, the ordinance does not fully cover all categories of loss by PAPs. The
excess of entitlements in the agreed matrix over what is paid by the DCs would be
financed from the project budget.



- 183 -                         ANNEX9
Page 5 of 6
14.   Land acquisition and resettlement activities would be managed by the Project
Resettlement Unit of the PIU, located in Chittagong, and headed by a Deputy Director
Resettlement. Work would be carried out by four subunits established by Cox's Bazaar,
Chittagong, Noahkhali, and Barisal. Each subunit has Regional Project Resettlement
Officers, Revenue Officers, an Accountant, and a Surveyor. Special Land Acquisition
Officers deputed to the Deputy Commissioners have been appointed at Cox's Bazaar,
Chittagong, and Laxmipur to assist the Deputy Commissioners in dealing with polders
with large numbers of affected people. Entitlements beyond those specified under the
law would be paid by the Project Resettlement Unit. The consultants who prepared the
draft resettlement plan with regard to land to be acquired for embankment retirement have
been contracted to use their data base from their survey to assist the DCs and the Project
Resettlement Unit to establish entitlements for land required for the first year of
construction. The resettlement consultants have subcontracted with NGOs active in the
project areas to work with people affected by land acquisition, verify payments, and
participate in grievance committees. Following credit signature, it is expected that the
contract of the resettlement consultants would be extended to cover land required for
subsequent construction years.
15.   All payments to PAPs will be made in the presence of a BWDB representative of
the rank of Subdivisional Engineer or above. Grievance Redressal Committees,
consisting of a representative of the DC, a BWDB Subdivisional Engineer, the local
Union Parishad Chairman, and a representative of the resettlement NGO, would be
formed for each of the four resettlement subunits. (Additional members such as a teacher
at a local educational institute and a BWDB Section Officer may be added, if needed,
while maintaining the ratio of committee members from government departments at 50%
or less.) These committees will be formed within two months of the establishment of the
resettlement subunits and shall commence hearing grievances within a week of issuance
of details of PAP entitlements following joint verification. Grievances may be brought to
the attention of either the resettlement NGO or the resettlement subunit. These
committees are expected to meet regularly to hear grievances at least once every two
weeks, at a place and time publicized to the PAPs.
Timing
16.   The Notice under Section 3 of the Ordinance notifying the public of plots
proposed for acquisition for the first year's construction program was given in mid-
August. A joint verification exercise to assess individual claims, using data provided by
the socio-economic survey, has been initiated for the first year's required land. The
committee carrying out this exercise includes representatives of the resettlement NGOs.
Finalization of the Joint Verification Report and determination of award amounts are
scheduled to be completed by the end of October. (For the one polder where it is
necessary to start on resectioning during the first construction season, the work will
proceed one month later, combining survey of PAPs and registration of squatters on the
embankment.) Payment of compensation in accordance with the compensation policy is
scheduled to be completed by January 31, with land to be handed over to contractors by



- 184 -                         ANNEX9
Page 6 of 6
March 1. The compensation policy includes extra payment for each week by which the
period between compensation and eviction is less than 12 weeks.
17.   Following credit signature, similar procedures would start for the land required for
the second and subsequent year's construction program. This includes updating of the
squatter survey and carrying out of socio-economic surveys of:
(a)   areas where as a result of subsequent erosion the alignments of
embankments to be retired have been modified since the original survey;
(b)   areas required for embankment rehabilitation, including land needed along
side existing embankments for embankment widening, and land
underneath one existing embankment which had been provided by the
owners on a voluntary basis without compensation;
(c)   areas required completion of PWP works;
(d)   areas required for homestead plots for settlers on the toe of the
embankment; and
(e)   areas required for minor rehabilitation and improvement works.
1 8.   The resettlement consultants would also carry out a sample survey of PAPs who
have received compensation, to determine the impact of land acquisition on their socio-
economic situation. The monitoring and evaluation specialist of the general project
consultants would liaise with the resettlement consultants to ensure adequate monitoring
of the PAPs. Under the allowance for contingencies in the provision for resettlement
costs, additional NGO self-help or training programs would be provided to any category
of PAPs identified by the survey as requiring such services.

BANGLADESH
COASTAL EMBANKMENT REHABILITATION PROJECT
Environmental Analysis
A. Introduction
1.        An environmental assessment of the Coastal Embankment
Rehabilitation Project (CERP) was undertaken by environmental consultants
financed by the European Commission. The report concluded that the overall
environmental impact of the project is positive. The expected impacts on
agriculture, fisheries, and the socio-economic status of the population in the
project area will be significantly positive in most respects. The consultants
concluded that there is adequate scope for mitigation of virtually all the
identified adverse environmental and social impacts of the project and
recommended mitigating measures and arrangements for impact monitoring. This
environmental annex summarizes the consultants' main findings.
B. Potential Environmental Impacts
2.        The potential environmental impacts were considered and likely
impacts were then characterized in terms of their scope, duration, probability
of occurrence, significance and other appropriate factors. An attempt to
characterize project impacts is summarized in Table 1. Since many of the
potential positive and negative impacts are inter-related, could occur
simultaneously amongst differing social groups and to a certain extent, negate
each other, they were grouped and considered under the following headings:
Agriculture.
Fisheries.
Socio-economics.
Miscellaneous.
This will allow a greater appreciation of the relative environmental costs and
benefits of the project.
Agriculture
3.        Two of the greatest constraints on agriculture in the coastal strip
are the salinisation of soil and flood damage due to incursion of saline and
tidal fresh waters. One of the main aims of the project is to offer greater
protection to coastal areas from high tide levels and cyclone surges. In the
post-construction years one would expect to see a decline in soil salinity
and flood recurrence and intensity. This would result in an increase in the
farmers confidence that investments in agriculture will be secure. It is
widely expected that these changes will manifest themselves as agricultural
intensification and increases in yields and incomes.

Annex 10
Page 4 of 14
4.        Predictions of the changes that will occur in cropping patterns
have been made by the project preparation consultants and reviewed by the IDA
appraisal mission. Generally, it appears that the percentage of cropped area
under single cropping will decline and the area that is double cropped will
increase. It is not likely that the area which is triple cropped will
increase substantially. It is estimated that over a period of five years from
completion of civil works, between 20% and 40% of the current T. Aman area
would shift to HYV in the Kharif season. Conversion would be at the high end
of the range in those polders with comparatively low levels of absentee
ownership and share cropping. During the rabi season, however, HYV boro
cultivation is expected to increase in only one polder due to the lack of
fresh water for irrigation in other areas.
5.        The sources of environmental impact which are generally associated
with agricultural intensification are as follows:
Increased use of agrochemicals.
Depletion of existing surface water resources within polders
for supplementary irrigation.
Increased use of improved seed varieties and the abandonment
of traditional local varieties.
Increased Use of Agrochemicals
6.        Intensification of agriculture is usually accompanied by increased
use of fertilizers and pesticides which may pollute surface waters by runoff
from soils and contaminate groundwater and aquifer bodies due to the downward
percolation of polluted soil water.
7.        Bangladesh has a comparatively low rate of fertilizer and pesticide
consumption compared to neighboring countries. Currently the rate of
agrochemical use in the coastal area is low. In other areas, where irrigated
agriculture has been introduced, the consumption of fertilizers and pesticides
has increased two to three fold. Much of this increased consumption is the
result of boro cultivation with HYV rice which has a greater demand for
nutrients than low yielding varieties and is also more prone to pests.
S.        It is unlikely that agrochemical consumption will increase
dramatically in coastal polders or that environmental problems will arise for
the following reasons:
Due to lack of irrigation water it is not expected that triple
cropping or the adoption of high yield boro varieties will be
widespread. Switching to HYV varieties would be confined in
most areas to the aman season. Therefore, one would not
expect the agrochemical consumption rate to increase as much
as two fold.
The build-up of unacceptable levels of agrochemicals within
polders is unlikely to occur due to the flushing effects of
seasonal flooding and drainage of polders.



- 189 -
Annex 10
Page 5 of 14
Many of the coastal aquifers are small and isolated and
'protected' by the impermeability of the confining layers;
widespread contamination by pesticides is therefore difficult
to envisage.
Supplementary Irrigation using Surface Water Resources
9.        In many polder areas intensification of agricultural production
leads to drawing down of surface water in ponds and khals, reducing water
availability for domestic use, for bathing and laundry, for livestock watering
and for fishing. While in the proposed project area there is some potential
for this occurring, it should be remembered that the project would increase
availability of fresh surface water, since in the absence of the project these
water bodies would become saline. Over the longer run encouragement of crops
requiring less water than rice will reduce this problem.
Salinization of groundwater
10.       While over-pumping leading to salinization of groundwater would
have significant detrimental effects, it is unlikely to occur. Since any
over-pumping would cause salinization of farmers' own sources of drinking
water, they can be relied upon to use appropriate restraint, as they have in
the past.
Soil Waterlogging
11.       One of the adverse impacts reported of the previous coastal
embankment project was that waterlogging of soils occurred in some areas as a
result of inadequate polder drainage. In general, this appears to have been
addressed by polder drainage improvement schemes. The rehabilitation of
embankments will result in their height being increased which could exacerbate
or create such problems. However, since the environmental consultants are not
aware of any significant waterlogging problems at present which are
attributable to coastal embankments per se, it is not considered that this
impact has a high probability of occurrence or significance. Further, the
project will finance additional drainage improvements in selected polders
which will help to prevent this impact from occurring.
Abandonment of Local Seed Varieties
12.       As the residual salinity of the soil declines due to the flushing
effects of yearly flooding and drainage, farmers are expected to replace local
salt tolerant varieties with HYV strains. While the impact at the farm level
is expected to be a positive, measures are required to conserve the country's
genetic resources. This is being undertaken under an ongoing IDA financed
project (as described in para 31).
13.       An additional impact related to the adoption of HYV rice is the
potential reduction in fodder availability. This arises because the residual
straw after rice harvesting is much less with HYV rice than with local
varieties. Whilst this impact may be of local significance, we do not



- 190 -
Annex 10
Page 6 of 14
anticipate that it will be of significance throughout most of the project
area. Where it is an issue, farmers can be encouraged to grow sufficient
fodder crops to provide for livestock needs.
Costs and Benefits
14.       The overall impact of agricultural intensification can be
determined by comparing the positive and negative impacts associated with
intensification which are described above. The key negative impacts which
have to be weighed against agricultural intensification are the loss of
genetic diversity and the possibilities of pollution due to increased use of
agrochemicals or depletion of surface water resources as a result of increased
profitability of irrigation. It is difficult to compare these impacts on a
quantitative basis with the benefits of increased crop yields. A more central
issue to the cost-benefit analysis however, is the scope and cost for impact
mitigation and the significance of any residual impacts. It is possible to
collect and preserve for future use indigenous seed (a component of the World
Bank/ODA Agriculture Support Services Project addresses this issue). It is
also possible to encourage farmers to plant crops of lower water demand and to
train farmers to use appropriate levels of agricultural inputs and integrated
pest management methods.
Fisheries
15.       The construction of coastal embankments affects the fisheries
sector in two ways. Open water capture fisheries are most likely to be
adversely affected due to the barrier effect of the embankments. In contrast,
embankment construction and the protection it affords will create a relatively
risk-free environment for investments in intensive aquaculture to take place.
Open Water Capture Fisheries
16.       Generally inland fisheries have been in decline for some time.  The
reason for this is not clear-cut and is likely to involve a combination of
factors in different areas, including overfishing and the development of flood
control, drainage and irrigation systems. However, open fresh water fisheries
are not of great biological or economic significance in the coastal polders
where marine subsistence and economic fishing predominate. Moreover, the
project embankments do not close up major or medium rivers or shoot channels
of these rivers which are the main routes for migration of fish to the
spawning grounds in flood plains. Therefore, it is unlikely that the CERP
will have any significant discernable impacts on the open water capture
fishery which are additional to existing pressures on the fishery.
Aquaculture
17.       Aquaculture production in the coastal areas of Bangladesh is well
below potential rates of production. With a greater degree of protection,
investments in both brackish and freshwater aquaculture could yield
significant increases in fish and shrimp production.



- 191 -
Annex 10
Page 7 of 14
Freshwater Aquaculture
18.       Estimates have been made of the area of cultivable and derelict
ponds which could be rehabilitated, and of the approximate production of
existing cultured ponds. Increased production benefits in existing cultured
ponds will accrue to the owner or leaser rather than to the population in
general. There may be some limited additional employment opportunities for
the underemployed, however. There is considerable potential for involving the
rural poor in reclaiming derelict ponds for freshwater fish and shrimp culture
with the assistance of NGOs. This could have significant positive impacts on
the nutritional and economic status of people involved, bringing project
benefits to a wider section of the rural community.
Brackish Water Aquaculture
19.       It is highly probable that the project will result in an increase
in the production of existing brackish water shrimp and fish farms, and also
an increase in the area under aquaculture activities. The net result will be
increased income to farm owners, increased employment opportunities and
stimulation of support activities such as product processing and packaging,
refrigeration services and transport.
20.       In most of the project area shrimp is grown in alternation with
salt production in areas unsuitable for rice. However, there is currently
some conflict of interest with rice farmers in areas where both activities co-
exist. It has been reported in some areas that shrimp farmers are forcing
rice farmers to yield their land for expansion. Even in areas where this does
not occur, salinisation of agricultural land adjacent to aquaculture farms is
an interference factor. This issue predates the proposed project and is
unlikely to be significantly worsened by the project.
Costs and Benefits
21.       While the damage to open water fisheries of flood control and
drainage projects can be compensated in production terms by increases in
aquaculture, it can generally be argued that the beneficiaries of increased
aquaculture production are mainly richer elements of the rural communities
while the loss of open water fisheries is mainly bourn by the poor. However,
in the project area open water fisheries are not significant and the project
does not adversely affect open water fisheries in other areas. Therefore,
these general arguments do not apply in this case. While the main
beneficiaries of expansion of acquacultural activities will still be the
richer elements of the community, there will be increased employment
opportunities without damage to the access of the poor to open water
fisheries.
Socio-Economic Impact
22.       The CERP gives rise to several socio-economic impacts and issues
which are related to the construction of embankments, agricultural and



- 192 -
Annex 10
Page 8 of 14
aquacultural intensification and to the afforestation component of the
project.
resettlement and compensation;
improved economic status of the landless; and
changes in the social fabric of rural communities.
Resettlement and Compensation
23.       The rehabilitation of embankments will require additional land on
both the seaward and landward sides of the embankments. Some additional land
may also be required for mangrove afforestation. This will affect existing
land owners within the polders in the following ways:
*     Some homesteads which lie close to the existing embankments
will have to be relocated.
Homestead gardens, fruit trees and ponds may be lost due to
land take.
Agricultural land may be lost, affecting the families ability
to sustain itself.
Squatter families on embankments which require rehabilitation
will have to be relocated.
A detailed polder-wise survey of affected assets along the proposed embankment
alignment and a resettlement and compensation plan are currently being
undertaken to ensure that no persons are significantly adversely affected by
the project. Provided the issue of compensation and resettlement is managed
efficiently and effectively, only short term inconvenience, which could be
deemed insignificant, may be experienced.
Economic Status of the Landless
24.       Some of the landless people currently living on the existing
embankments or on char lands will receive the opportunity to lease embankment
land which can be cultivated in suitable tree crops for the long term
livelihood of the landless family. Landless Contracting Societies will also
have the opportunity to undertake long term maintenance work for which they
will receive payment. This income will substantially improve their economic
status. The net result of successful afforestation and embankment leasing
will have long-term significant positive impacts on the landless community.
Social Fabric of Rural Communities
25.       There is the potential for social conflicts to arise between the
land owners who are affected by land take, and the newly settled landless who
may occupy the area of the formers land. In addition, the social fabric of
existing rural settlements may be disrupted by the resettlement of land owners
elsewhere. Overall such impacts may not be significantly adverse. However,



- 193 -
Annex 10
Page 9 of 14
at the local level serious conflict could arise. This can largely be
prevented by careful consideration during the planning stage and the
involvement of beneficiaries.
Costs and Benefits
26.       At the gross project level, it is apparent that project benefits
far outweigh the costs; one has to compare the loss of about 600 ha of land
due to land take (requiring compensation payments to up to 6000 families or
60,000 people most of whom would lose only a small portion of their holdings)
with the protection afforded to 1.2 million people. About 140,000 people were
killed during the 1991 cyclone.
27.       At the polder level, the positive impacts which arise from the CERP
are of high significance for the landless and land owning groups of the
project area. The enhanced opportunities for long and short term employment
for landless and marginal farmers and the reduced potential for loss of life
greatly outweigh, in opinion of the environmental consultants, the potential
negative impacts of disruption to rural communities and hardship for land
owners due to land take; there is considerable scope to prevent or mitigate
these negative impacts.
Hvdroloc*v and Sediment Dynamics
28.       The construction of embankments alters the natural pattern of river
flow and of seasonal flooding in the project area. However, the environmental
consultants conclude that no significant adverse impacts will arise since
there is ample scope to prevent or mitigate them.
Afforestation
29.       The provision of afforestation on the foreshore areas and on the
embankments for protection of the embankments against wave action and erosion
by rainwater and wind will have the following beneficial impacts upon the
environment:
Provide protection of the embankments and foreshore area
against erosion by waves;
Reduce the wind velocity of cyclones and strong monsoon winds
and thereby reduce wind generated damages and soil erosion;
Protect the pollen of the paddy from strong monsoon wind,
resulting in increase of crop yields;
Increase the vegetation coverage of the area which will
facilitate restoration of the ecological imbalances and
erratic behavior of the nature;
Help meet the demand of fuel energy in the project area; and



- 194 -
Annex 10
Page 10 of 14
Provide valuable food, and cash income to the local farmers,
resulting in uplift of the rural economy.
This component would also provide a long-term means of livelihood to a
significant number of landless families.
C. Environmental Management Plan for the Project Area
30.       Tables 1 and 2 summarize options for environmental monitoring and
mitigation of project impacts. These options constitute an environmental
management plan for the CERP and address the following requirements.
*    Monitoring of environmental impacts in a cost effective way
and where possible combining the project monitoring
requirements with those of related projects.
Enhancement of the positive impacts of the project by
providing assistance to beneficiaries in achieving sustainable
development of polder resources through technical assistance
and the development of self-help groups.
Prevention and/or mitigation of negative impacts through
improved polder management involving beneficiaries,
agricultural extension activities and NGO guidance and
assistance to beneficiaries.
Management of Agricultural Intensification
31.       The potential for negative environmental impacts of agricultural
intensification to arise are directly related to the nature of cultivation
practices in the project area. The Department of Agricultural Extension (DAE)
and selected NGOs will, through extension activities, promote sustainable
agricultural development. In particular, the IDA supported Agricultural
Support Services Project (ASSP) will support:
Integrated pest management.
Appropriate application and handling of agrochemicals.
Crop diversification (also involving lower water demand
crops).
Development of horticultural export businesses.
Appropriate irrigation water management.
In addition the ASSP will also support the development and cultivation of more
appropriate seed varieties and the establishment of genetic seed banks.
