Page 13 of 14
32.       Appropriate NGOs, who will be involved in other components of the
project will be invited to collaborate in efforts to increase agricultural
production and promote appropriate use of farm inputs. They will also play a
role in assisting the landless in afforestation activities on the embankments
and in foreshore areas. The DAE and the FD would provide technical support to
the NGOs in these activities.
Coordinating Development Efforts
33.       To stimulate and coordinate the above activities an Agricultural
Development Officer will be appointed to the Project Implementation Unit for a
10 month period spread over 18 months and starting in the second year of the
project. The main duties of this officer would be to ensure that the
necessary actions take place to promote agricultural intensification in the
project area, many of which will be promoted nationally under the ASSP and
NMIDP. He will collaborate with the ASSP and NMIDP PIU consultants, the CERP
Social Development Officer, the CERP Environmental Specialist and other CERP
TA specialists in ensuring that a holistic approach to the management of
agricultural and environmental impacts is adopted.
Management of Fisheries Issues
34.       While the project will not obstruct any major natural water courses
affecting fish migration, it could affect some small seasonal water bodies.
In such areas, drainage structures would be operated so as to facilitate
fisheries. The program for appropriate regulator operation would be developed
by the environmental specialist in consultation with local groups and DoF
staff at the upazila level. Shrimp inlets would be provided where needed to
avoid damage to shrimp production.
35.       Various on-going projects are involved in programs to promote fresh
and brackish water fisheries production, particularly through the leasing of
ponds, fish farm management and husbandry. The current project would increase
the opportunities for such activities.
Impact Monitoring
36.       As noted in Table 2, much of the monitoring of the impact of the
project can be achieved without additional work by drawing upon information
already being collected by other agencies and donor-supported monitoring
programs. The project implementation consultants terms of reference would
include responsibility for developing appropriate monitoring programs,
ensuring that working mechanisms are in place to obtain required data already
being collected and supplementing this data as necessary. The technical
assistance component of the proposed project includes funds for procurement of
such supplementary equipment as many be appropriate, such as portable soil and
water analysis kits. Data collected should be on a polder basis, to the
extent possible, to allow information generated to be used for management
decisions at the polder level.



- 198 -
Annex 10
Page 14 of 14
Polder Operation and Maintenance
37.       The operation and management of polders will ultimately rest with
the BWDB through its local offices. The BWDB will develop O&M schedules which
take into account the agricultural and fisheries requirements of polder
occupants. DAB and DoF would provide information on such requirements. The
implementation consultants would assist in developing mechanisms at the local
level to ensure that all concerned agencies are consulted.
Developing BWDB's Environmental Management Capabilities
38.       The BWDB is concerned with the development and management of a wide
range of flood control, drainage and irrigation measures throughout
Bangladesh. It is appropriate that an agency whose actions have far reaching
consequences for the human and natural environment should incorporate
environmental considerations into its activities. GOB is committed to
developing environmental cells within all key Government agencies and
Ministries. This approach is currently being defined within the GOB National
Environmental Management Action Plan.
39.       While this overall approach is being worked out, the current
project will support short term in-country training on environmental and
social issues for key technical, operational and management staff involved in
the operation and maintenance of flood control, drainage, and irrigation
structures and systems. This will help to develop social and environmental
awareness within BWDB and encourage incorporation of environmental
considerations in the planning and design of such projects.
Technical Assistance
40.       The project implementation technical assistance program will
include inter alia finance of short term inputs of the following international
consultants over the life of the project.
Environmental Specialist (21 staff-months)
*    Agricultural Development Officer (10 staff-months)
Community Development Advisor (19 staff-months)



- 199 -                        ANNEX 11
Page 1 of 5
BANGLADESH
COASTAL EMBANKMENT REHABILITATION PROJECT (CERP)
Economic Analysis
1.    Economic analysis was done separately for 11 of 12 polders with major
works financed under this project.1 Economic analysis was not done for those
polders where the major works were financed under the PWP, and where the
current project only includes complementary measures (minor works,
afforestation and improved operation and maintenance (O&M)) because the
return to such complementary works, treating the PWP investments as sunk
costs, is known to be high. The main assumptions of the economic analysis
follow.
2.    Prices.  All costs and benefits are expressed in constant 1994 prices.
Recent Bank commodity price projections were used to derive world prices of
traded goods. Economic farmgate prices were then calculated from the projected
world prices for 2000/2005, with appropriate adjustments for quality, freight,
insurance, handling, processing, and inland transport, as illustrated in Table
1. Import parity values are used for goods that are imported in normal years,
such as wheat; export parity values are used for goods that Bangladesh
normally exports, such as jute and urea. For non-traded goods and services,
financial prices were converted to economic prices using a standard conversion
factor (SCF) of 0.90. The official exchange rate of US$1 to Taka 40.25
(currently used for all projects in Bangladesh) converts prices in US dollars
to Taka. Table 2 summarizes the financial and economic prices of the principal
goods.
Costs
3.    Investment Costs.  These include the cost of major and minor civil
works: resectioning and/or retirement of embankments, replacement or repair of
existing drainage structures, new drainage structures, new protection works,
repairs to existing revetment works, surveys and investigations, khal re-
excavation, and establishment costs, land acquisition, afforestation, and
resettlement. All costs associated with these activities, minus price
contingencies, taxes and duties, have been included. Sections of the
embankments in polders 59/2 (Ramgati) and 72 (Sandwip) would have to be
retired again in about year 15 of the operation due to erosion, and the costs
1.    For Polder 60 (Sonagazi), the project does not include major civil works
and only 2 km of embankment strengthening plus khal excavation, and this
polder is therefore excluded from the economic analysis. More time is
required to test the effectiveness of recently completed anti-erosion
works before firm estimates can be justified. Anowar (where about US$2
million equivalent of expenditure is planned to complete major works
started under PWP) is also excluded from the analysis.



- 200 -                           ANNEX 11
Page 2 of 5
of retirement have accordingly been included. The salvage values of project
facilities have not been subtracted from the costs because their discounted
value would be very small.
4.    Operation and Maintenance Costs.  Incremental costs for O&M have been
estimated for each polder with respect to protective works and routine and
periodic maintenance, operation of structures, repair of cyclone damage, and
maintenance of afforestation on embankment and the foreshore. The cost of
family labor used in routine maintenance of the embankment and afforestation
is assumed to be equal to the opportunity cost of labor; the latter is
approximated by what laborers earn as casual workers or sharecroppers (about
Taka 9,000 per family per year). Taxes and duties are excluded.
5.    Value of Unskilled Labor.  There are significant seasonal variations in
agricultural wages in the project area and, because of widespread
unemployment, market wages overstate the opportunity cost of labor. A weighted
average wage rate for each polder was therefore applied to eliminate seasonal
variations in farm wages. The average wage was then multiplied by 0.75 to
reflect the true opportunity cost of labor.
Benefits
6.    The main benefits are reduction in loss of life and property during
major cyclones, and prevention of salinity intrusions and storm surges during
normal monsoons, allowing increased agricultural production. Production
during normal monsoons would increase over the without project situation due
to (i) the direct effects on yields due to reduced saline intrusions; (ii) the
introduction of higher yielding crops made possible by the decrease in
salinity and the reduced threat of periodic cyclone damage; and (iii) better
drainage. In addition to these normal year benefits, there would be reduced
agricultural losses in years of severe cyclones. Indirect benefits from
growth linkages and environmental goods are excluded for lack of data.
7.    Savincr of Human Life.  No attempt has been made to quantify benefits
from lives saved, but this is clearly a major benefit of the operation.
B.    Agricultural Benefits.  Agricultural benefits include crop, livestock
and fisheries production.
(a)   Crops. The components of incremental crop output during normal
years are higher yields, more valuable cropping patterns and
higher cropping intensity. The with and without project
situations are estimated in Annex B. In estimating the reduction
of damages during severe cyclones, it is assumed that high water
levels corresponding to cyclones of a severity that occurs with a
frequency of once in 40 years result in severe flooding both in
the "without project" and "with project" situations. Hardly any
standing crop would be saved. Estimation of benefits from
protection to standing crops has therefore been based upon
detailed analysis of damage from cyclone surges with 10 and 20
year return periods. Experience shows that about 50% and 40W of
the crops would be saved by the embankments in the case of



- 201 -                          ANNEX 11
Page 3 of 5
cyclonic surges with a return period of 10 and 20 years,
respectively.
(b)   Livestock. Based on the official estimate of 40% of livestock lost
in Sandwip (Polder 72) during the 1991 cyclone, a livestock loss
of 309 in the "Without Project" situation has been assumed in the
analysis for cyclones with a return period of 10 years or more.
(c)   Fisheries. Shrimp farming is an important activity in parts of the
project zone. Improved embankments and sluices would protect fish
ponds and shrimp farms, especially for cyclones with a return
period of less than 40 years. The expected loss in income from
such fisheries without the protection afforded by the project has
been based on official estimates.
9.    Salt Production.  Salt production is vulnerable to saline intrusion at
the end of the production period in April. Cyclone seasons are October-
November and April-May, implying a 25% probability of an April cyclone. The
probability of cyclone damage to salt production has accordingly been adjusted
by a factor of 0.25 when estimating losses.
10.   Protection of Property and Infrastructure.  The embankments would
protect houses and other buildings, roads, culverts and other structures. The
damage during cyclones of a return period of 10 years or more was estimated
based on data on damage during recent cyclones. About 864,000 houses were
completely destroyed and another 774,000 were partially damaged during the
1991 cyclone.  A value of Taka 15,000 has been assigned to each destroyed
house.  This low value is due to the fact that cyclones destroy mainly the
poorest houses. The average damage to roads and culverts, based on field
observations, is assumed to be 5% and 30%, respectively, of the replacement
value.  Based on a sample of four polders where road categories and number of
culverts have been registered and valued, the average losses have been
estimated at Tk 2,000 per ha. It has also been assumed that regulators and
other infrastructure in shrimp farms would be saved particularly with regard
to cyclones with return periods of 10 and 20 years.
11.   Destruction by high winds has been excluded because the embankments do
not protect against wind. Half of the damage to buildings has been attributed
to wind, as have all losses of telephone lines and power supplies.
12.   Afforestation Benefits.  Afforestation of the embankments and a 50 to
200 meter wide belt on the foreshore would reduce cyclone damage and
embankment maintenance costs. This benefit has been taken into account when
estimating the costs of periodic and routine maintenance of the embankment and
cyclone damage repairs.   In addition to protecting the embankments, resulting
in lower operations and maintenance costs, other direct economic benefits of
afforestation include timber, fuel wood, fruits, and fodder. On the negative
side, the foreshore afforestation would lead to loss of some agricultural land
leading to reduced agricultural production. This loss has been assumed to be
equal to the value added of T. Aman under saline conditions. (On only about
1,000 ha out of the 4,700 ha planned for foreshore afforestation are crops
currently growtn.) Indirect environmental benefits have not been included for
lack of data.



- 202 -                         ANNEX 11
Page 4 of 5
Proiect Execution
13.   Investment phasing will vary from polder to polder, depending on the
condition of the embankment and the earthworks involved. The earthworks for
the major embankment repairs are assumed to be completed within three years
while the minor works component (other than khal re-excavation) would be
completed in four years. Afforestation on the foreshore would start at the
end of year one. Afforestation of the embankments would begin generally in
year three, while starting in year two in a few polders. Project
implementation has been assumed to produce full benefits beginning in the
seventh year.
14.   Summary of Results. Table 3 summarizes the aggregate results and
information for individual polders is in Tables 4 to 14. The overall economic
rate of return (ERR) based upon quantifyable benefits for the 11 polders in
the project including major works, but excluding any value for reduced loss of
human life, is estimated at 19.2%. The net present value of the incremental
benefits is estimated at US$27.0 million. Polderwise ERRs range from 6.6%
(Polder 70, Matherbari) to 28.1% (Polder 35/1, Sharankhola and Polder 59/3B
Sudharam). All polders have ERRs greater than 12%, excepting Polders 48,
59/2, and 70 which have ERRs of about 7.9%, 6.9%, and 6.6%, respectively.
Taking account of the additional benefit of saving human lives, these polders
are considered justified for inclusion in the project. For example, during
the 1991 cyclone about 10,000 people were killed in polder 70 from a
population of 23,000.
15.   Risks and Sensitivity Analysis.  The economic analysis was supplemented
by a sensitivity analysis to include higher investment and O&M costs and lower
quantifiable benefits (agricultural, non-agricultural, and afforestation).
(a)   The effect of an increase in investment cost was tested because
there is a risk of implementation delays due to
management/institutional weaknesses and to inadequate Government
funding resulting in cost escalation.
(b)   O&M costs may also increase.  The proposed community participation
in routine embankment maintenance and afforestation has not been
tried in the proposed form. If the proposed afforestation
component fails, the cost of routine maintenance would increase
substantially.
(c)   The impact of a reduction in agricultural benefits was tested
because this is the most important variable among the quantifiable
benefits. Agricultural benefits would fall due to intrusion of
saline water. A major risk of the project is that the life of the
embankments may be reduced if inadequate funds are provided for
O&M. In this case, non-agricultural benefits (protection of
property and infrastructure), which are important in some polders,
would also be affected adversely. In the event that the life of
the embankments is reduced, available engineering data suggest
that it would be necessary to have major expenditure on embankment
rehabilitation in about year 10 and again in year 27 after project
completion. It has been assumed in the analysis that for the



- 203 -                            ANNEX 11
Page 5 of 5
southern polders, which are very prone to cyclone damage, and
polders in the south west about 75% and 50%, respectively, of the
present cost of rehabilitation would be required in the tenth and
twenty seventh year after project completion.
(d)   The impact of a reduction in afforestation benefits was tested
because there is a major risk in the community participation
component for afforestation and routine embankment maintenance.
16.   Tables 4 to 14 present the sensitivity analysis. Exceptionally large
adverse changes in the variables tested would have to take place before the
ERR for any of the polders, except Polders 48, 59/2, and 70, drops to 12%.
Excluding Polders 48, 59/2 and 70, investment and O&M costs would have to
increase by between 47% (Polder 73/1A&B, Hatiya) and 174% (Polder 59/3B,
Sudharam) to bring ERR to 12%. Total project benefits would have to fall by
between 32% (Polder 73/1A&B) and 64% (Polder 59/3B) to reduce ERR to 12%; in
the case of agricultural benefits the required fall would range from 44%
(Polder 73/1A&B, Hatiya) to 82% (Polder 72); for afforestation benefits the
necessary fall would range from 202% (Polder 73/A&B) to 931% (Polder 35/1);
and for a reduction in non-agricultural benefits the range would be from 390%
(Polders 40/2, Patherghata and 59/3C, Companiganj) to 978% (Polder 35/1).
The ERR is highly insensitive to changes in non-agricultural benefits because
the value of non-agricultural benefits is relatively small in most of the
polders. Major expenditure on embankment rehabilitation in year 10 and 27
after project completion (a proxy for cost effects of inadequate maintenance)
would reduce the average ERR from 18.2* to 17.1%.
17.   The table below gives the ERR for various combinations of unfavorable
developments. It shows that to bring the ERR on quantifiable benefits below
12% would require a combination of a two year delay in project completion and
a decrease in project benefits exceeding 30%, or a three year delay in project
completion and a decrease in project benefits of about 20%. Other
combinations which would reduce the ERR to 12% woud include a two year delay
in project completion and an increase in O&M costs of over 30%, or a three
year delay and an increase in O&M costs of about 15%. Such combinations of
unfavorable developments are considered unlikely. Even then, the additional
nonquantifyable benefits of saving human lives would still make the project
worth doing.

- rice; Thai, nilled, 5% broken, FOB Bangkok.
- wheat; Canadian No. I Western Redspring, Thunder Bay.
- jute; White D. Bangladesh, FOB Chalna
- soyabeans; US, CIF Rotterdam.
- urea; FOB Chittagong.
- TSP; FOB US Gulf.
- MP; FOB US Gulf.
2/ Soyabeen and mustard seed are assumed to be of equal value.
3/ Between Chittagong/Chains and source or destination.
4/ Average of Take 1,450 converted to border values by a SCF of 0.90.
5/ Average of 300 kmn @ Tk 1 .38/tkikn coverted to border values by SCF of 0.90.
6/ Average of 15 km 0 Tk 9/t/km convertd to border values by SCFo 0.90.
7/ As percentage of 'v"ue ex-store, adjusted by SCF of 0.90.

BANGLADESH
COASTAL EMBANKMENTb REHABILITATION PROJ13CT (CERP)
Reporting Reauirements and Performance Indicators
1.          The following reports would be sent regularly to IDA:
(a)   six monthly reports within one month of the closing of the first and
third quarters of each financial year.  The report would include
physical and financial progress and would follow the outlines given
in para 2 below;
(b)   quarterly progress reports on the resettlement program, including
the performance of families on afforestation of embankment slopes
and routine maintenance and the performance of community groups on
the foreshore afforestation program;
(c)   audited financial statement of the project,  together with the
auditor's report and opinion on the financial statement, and the
auditor's separate opinion on SOEs, within nine months of the end of
each fiscal year; and
(d)   the borrower's contribution (Section II) to the project Completion
Report, within three months of the Borrower's receipt of Sections I
and III from IDA.
2.          The six monthly reports should provide sufficient information for the
use of IDA supervision missions tentatively scheduled for August and February of
each financial year. The outline suggested for the report would include:
(a)   The Executive Summary
(b)   Physical Progress.   This para would contain information on the
components: civil works, resettlement, afforestation and O&M. The
achievements expected each year are detailed in the physical
progress performance indicators attached to this annex. The report
would include a tabulated statement giving the progress against the
performance indicators. In addition the report would include the
progress on preparatory activities for: (i) civil works construction
such as designs for minor works, procurement of major and minor
civil works, land acquisition for civil works construction inclusive
of khal re-excavation; (ii) land acquisition for resettlement;
(iii) temporary   relocation   of   displaced   families;    and
(iv) preparation of programs for resettlement, O&M and forestry
including forestry nurseries and trials.   The report would also
include a brief discussion on progress of each component.
Attachments to the report would include besides the tabulated
statement on the performance indicators, the physical progress for



- 227 -
ANNEX 13
Page 2 of 2
each project polder for all categories listed in Items A through E
of Table 2 of the detailed cost tables (Annex 12), giving
achievements up to the previous reporting period and achieved in the
current reporting period, concise information on the major civil
works contracts, updated schedules on procurement of civil works,
vehicles and equipment (Annexes l(b)), and implementation (Annex
la);
(b)   Implementation Support
(c)   Training
(c)   Financial Proaress.   The para would include:  (a) expenditures
(targets and achievements) incurred according to the items of
disbursements listed in para 2.33 of the SAR, and in addition for
the items financed by EC and those financed fully by GOB; (b) a
forecast on cost over run/savings when appropriate; and (c) status
of reimbursement applications;
(d)   Results of monitorinQ and imDact assessment studies:
(i) Sumary of findings of environmental monitoring activities and
proposals for any additional mitigation measures required.
(ii) Results of sample surveys and questionnaires (see Annex 7 para
15 (d) & (e)) covering trends in agricultural production and
farm incomes, cropping patterns, farm employment, incomes of
families  participating   in  embankment  maintenance   and
addorestation   programs,and   participants   in   foreshore
afforestation.
(iii) Any recommendations arising out of (ii).
(e)   Problems.  The para should include: (a) problems identified during
the previous reporting period, actions taken to resolve the problems
and results; and (b) problems identified during the reporting period
and proposed actions;
(f)   Attachments
